The Proof that the Outermost Sphere Has a Place
Proves that the outermost sphere has a place via rotational motion and concludes place is al-bu'd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the outermost sphere (al-muhaddad) has a place, and that place is the interior dimension (al-bu'd) rather than the surface (al-sath).

Core Idea
Rotational motion proves the outermost sphere's parts change place, so its place is the interior dimension.

This passage resolves a contradiction in the philosophers' position: they admitted every body has a natural place, yet denied the outermost sphere has a place. The commentator uses the rotational motion of the sphere and the observable motion of celestial bodies to show that its parts do change place, leading to the conclusion that place is the interior dimension (al-bu'd) not the surface (al-sath).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation to understand the argument.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice that the philosophers admit every body has a natural position (hayz) but then claim the outermost sphere has no place. This is the starting point of the argument.
The commentator calls this a contradiction.
3
Follow the motion argument
The commentator argues that although the outermost sphere as a whole does not change place in its rotational motion, its halves and all its individual parts do change place relative to the earth.
Think about the parts above and below the earth.
4
Observe the celestial evidence
The sun, moon, and stars are sometimes above the earth and sometimes below, proving they change place. This supports the claim that parts of the rotating sphere change place.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since every part of the outermost sphere is in a place and changes place, the whole sphere occupies a composite place. Therefore, place must be the interior dimension (al-bu'd) that contains the body, not the surface (al-sath).
This refutes the alternative view that place is the surface.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم المحيط بما عداه من الأجسام في مكان والثاني باطل بالضرورة كما ذكرنا . وبالاتفاق أيضاً أليس الحكماء لما أثبتوا الحيز الطبيعي للأجسام قالوا :) نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن كل جسم لو خلي وطبعه لكان في حيز) فقد اعترفوا بأن كل جسم يجب أن يكون في مكان وحكموا بذلك هناك وبنوا عليه إثبات المكان الطبيعي (فما بالهم نسوا ذلك وأنكروه حين ألزموا به فالقائلون بأن المحدد لا مكان له مناقضون لأنفسهم فيما ادعوه هناك بل نقول ( كيف ) لا يكون للمحدد مكان ( وأن الحركة الوضعية التي لا تقتضي تبدل المكان ( إنما تعرض لمجموع المحدد من حيث هو مجموع ( وأما نصفاه المتمايزان بحسب ما يعرض لهما من كونهما فوق الأرض أو تحتها فلا شك أنهما ( يستبدلان المكان ولهما نقلة ) من مكان إلى آخر وكذلك جميع أجزاء المحدد تستبدل أمكنتها بأمكنة أخرى حال حركته بالاستدارة ) ولو كان أجزاء المتحرك بالحركة الدورية ليس لها نقلة ) من إلى مكان آخر ( لم يكن للقمر والشمس وسائر الكواكب ولا لمكانها الذي ركزت فيه ( نقلة ) أصلاً لأنها لا تستبدل سطحاً بسطح ( والضرورة تبطله ) ألا ترى أنها تارة فوق الأرض وتارة تحتها، فكيف لا تكون منتقلة من مكان إلى آخر مع ثبوت هذه الحالة لها وإذا كان كل جزء من أجزاء المحدد في مكان ومستبدلا بسبب حركته الوضعية مكاناً آخر كان المحدد كله في مكان مركب من أمكنة أجزائه فوجب أن يكون المكان هو البعد دون السطح هذا.
The body that surrounds all other bodies is in a place, and the second [claim] is necessarily false as we mentioned. And also by agreement: do not the philosophers, when they proved the natural position (al-hayz al-tabi'i) for bodies, say: 'We know necessarily that every body, if left to its nature, would be in a position (hayz).' So they have admitted that every body must be in a place and they judged that there and built upon it the proof of natural place. So what is the matter with them that they forget that and deny it when they are forced with it? So those who say that the outermost sphere (al-muhaddad) has no place contradict themselves in what they claimed there. Rather we say: How could the outermost sphere not have a place, while the positional motion (al-haraka al-wad'iyya) that does not necessitate change of place occurs only to the whole of the outermost sphere insofar as it is a whole? But as for its two halves distinguished by their being above the earth or below it, there is no doubt that they substitute place and have a transfer from one place to another. Similarly, all parts of the outermost sphere substitute their places with other places during its rotational motion. And if the parts of the body moving with circular motion had no transfer from one place to another, then the moon, the sun, and all the planets, and their places in which they are fixed would have no transfer at all, because they do not substitute surface with surface. And necessity refutes it. Do you not see that sometimes it is above the earth and sometimes below it? So how could it not be transferred from one place to another while this state is established for it? And when every part of the outermost sphere is in a place and substitutes, due to its positional motion, another place, then the whole outermost sphere is in a place composed of the places of its parts. So it is necessary that place is the dimension (al-bu'd) not the surface (al-sath). This.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the outermost sphere (al-muhaddad) has a place, and it concludes that place is the interior dimension (al-bu'd) rather than the surface (al-sath). It begins by presenting an objection: the philosophers themselves admit that every body, if left to its nature, would have a natural position (hayz), and they built the proof of natural place on that. However, they later deny that the outermost sphere has a place, contradicting their own principle. The commentator then rebuts this by examining the rotational motion of the outermost sphere. Although the motion as a whole does not involve change of place, the two halves of the sphere—above and below the earth—clearly exchange places. Likewise, all its individual parts substitute their places during rotation. If parts of a rotating body did not change place, then the sun, moon, and stars would have no transfer, but our observation shows they are sometimes above and sometimes below the earth, necessitating a change of place. Since every part of the outermost sphere is in a place and changes place during rotation, the whole sphere occupies a composite place made of the places of its parts. Therefore, place must be the internal dimension (al-bu'd) that contains the body, not the surface (al-sath) that touches it. This logical argument is rooted in observational evidence and the principle of non-contradiction.

Key Terms
المحدد
الحركة الوضعية
البعد
السطح
الحيز الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rotational motion involves no change of place for any part of the rotating body.
Correction: While the whole sphere's motion does not change its location, its individual parts do change place relative to the earth, as seen by the rising and setting of celestial bodies.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the commentator draw from the argument about the parts of the outermost sphere?
A) The outermost sphere has no place.
B) Place is the surface that touches the body.
C) Place is the interior dimension (al-bu'd).
D) The parts of the outermost sphere do not move.

Key Takeaway
The outermost sphere has a place, and that place is the interior dimension (al-bu'd) because its parts change place during rotational motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers held a principle that every body has a natural place, yet denied the outermost sphere has a place when it became inconvenient. In your own life, where have you held a belief or principle in general but abandoned it when applying it to a specific situation that challenged you? What would it cost you to be consistent, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
The proof relies on the observable fact that the sun and moon change position relative to the earth. What observable truths about yourself—your habits, thoughts, or relationships—are you ignoring because acknowledging them would force you to change a comfortable but flawed self-image? What would it take for you to admit that you have been wrong?
?
If place is the dimension containing the body, then every part of the universe is contained within God's knowledge and power. Do you live as if you are beyond God's reach, as if there is a place where He does not see you? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that you are never outside His containment—not just in space but in every moment of your existence?

The Second Proof that Place is Not the Surface
A single-sentence proof against place as a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that place cannot be a relation of the body's parts to something external.

Core Idea
Place cannot be a relation because it would require a strange effect.

This lesson introduces the second proof that place is not merely a relation of the parts of a body to something outside. The argument is concise but critical: if place were such a relation, it would lead to an unnatural consequence. Understanding this sets the stage for the broader discussion of what place truly is.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Carefully read the single sentence that constitutes the proof. Notice the key phrase: 'because it would require a strange effect.' Ask yourself what this strange effect might be.
The proof is only one line, but its implications are significant.
2
Reflect on what 'strange effect' means
Consider that if place were a relation of parts to something external, then the parts would have to be related to that external thing in a way that seems unnatural for a body's intrinsic parts.
3
Connect to the larger debate
Understand that this proof is part of a series of arguments rejecting the definition of place as a surface or relation. It strengthens the position that place is something else.
This proof is contrasted with earlier definitions to show inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من الوجوه الدالة على بطلان أن المكان هو يعرض بسبب نسبة أجزاء الجسم إلى غير الجسم، لأنه مما يقتضيه تأثير غريب . وأما الوضع بالمعنى الثالث وهو كون الجسم بحيث يقبل الإشارة الحسية فهو أمر تقتضيه الجسمية الحالة الهيولى وليس مما يتعلق بالطبائع المختلفة انتهى، ولا شك في أن الوضع بهذا المعنى عارض لكل جسم ولو خلي وطبعه فالحيز الطبيعي بمعنى الوضع شامل لجميع الأجسام على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن لكل جسم وضعاً وللفلك الأقصى وضع وهو مباين للمكان بمعنى السطح فما معنى قوله وهو أعم من المكان وما الحاجة إلى اعتبار عمومه . قوله : (لهم أن يخصوا إلخ ) جواب باختيار أنّ الحيز هو المكان والكلية مخصوص بما سوى المحدد. وإليه تشير عبارة الإشارات حيث قال : إِنَّ الجسم إذا خلي وطباعه لم يكن له بد من موضع معين حيث لم يقل : كل جسم ويرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن لو خلي الجسم ونفسه المكان بمعنى السطح، كيف وقد انتفى ذلك الاقتضاء في المحدد وأنَّ للمحيط مدخلاً يقتضي في ذلك . قوله : ( إنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك فيه أنَّ الإشارة بهنا وهناك يقتضي نسبة الجسم إليه بالنظر فيه، ونسبة الجسم إلى الوضع بفي لا يقبله العقل السليم فالوجه أن يقولوا : إِنَّ كل جسم فهو مشار إليه في نفسه ولا نسلم أنه مشار إليه بهنا وهناك. قوله : ( تلك الأجزاء مفروضة أي جزئيتها لأن نفس الأجزاء ليست مفروضة . قوله : ( وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل) أي مع وصف الجزئية . كان الحيز في المحدد هو الوضع. أي الحالة التي يتميز بها الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، فلم لم يكتف بهذه الحالة في سائر الأجسام وأثبت فيه الحيز بمعنى المكان . قلت : لضرورة أنَّ الجسم الممتاز في الإشارة الحسية الكائن تحت آخر له محيط به يوجد فيه الخواص المثبتة للمكان . قوله فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية الظاهر أن ذوات الأجزاء محققة، والفرضية جزئيتها وأن تحقق الذات يكفي لعروض الحركة الخارجية. وسيأتي تتمة لهذا الكلام في مباحث الأين على رأي الفلاسفة.
(The second) of the proofs indicating the falsehood that place occurs due to the relation of the parts of the body to something other than the body, because it would require a strange effect. As for al-wad' in the third sense, which is the body being such that it admits sensory indication, it is something required by corporeality (al-jismiyya) inhering in hyle, and it is not related to different natures. End. There is no doubt that al-wad' in this sense is an accident for every body, even if left to its nature. So the natural al-hayz meaning al-wad' encompasses all bodies, according to what is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: that every body has a wad', and the outermost sphere has a wad' which is distinct from place in the sense of surface. So what is the meaning of his statement that it is more general than place? And what need is there to consider its generality? His statement: 'They may specify (etc.)' is an answer by choosing that al-hayz is place, and the universality is restricted to other than the outermost sphere. The expression of al-Isharat points to that where it said: 'Indeed, the body if left to its nature cannot but have a specific position (mawdi')' as it did not say: 'every body'. And it is objected: we do not accept that if the body is left to itself, it necessitates place in the sense of surface; how has that necessitation ceased in the outermost sphere, and that the surrounding has an influence that necessitates that? His statement: 'Indeed, what is pointed to by here and there' — in it, that indication by 'here' and 'there' necessitates the relation of the body to it by 'in' (fi), and the relation of the body to al-wad' by 'in' is not accepted by the sound mind. So the proper way is that they say: 'Indeed, every body is pointed to in itself, and we do not accept that it is pointed to by here and there.' His statement: 'Those parts are hypothetical' means their being parts, because the parts themselves are not hypothetical. His statement: 'And if they actually exist' means with the description of being parts. Al-hayz in the outermost sphere was al-wad', i.e., the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory indication. So why was this state not sufficient for other bodies, and they proved al-hayz in the sense of place for them? I say: because of the necessity that the body distinguished in sensory indication that is under another body having a surrounding possesses properties that prove place. His statement: 'So it does not undergo external motion' — the apparent meaning is that the essences of the parts are realized, and the hypothetical nature is their being parts, and that the realization of the essence is sufficient for the occurrence of external motion. And a continuation of this discussion will come in the investigations of al-ayn according to the opinion of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage delivers the second proof that place is not the surface: if place were a relation of parts to something external, it would involve a strange effect. It then discusses the third meaning of al-wad' (position): the body's capacity to be pointed to in sensory indication, which is a necessary attribute of every body (even the outermost sphere) arising from corporeality inhering in matter, not from specific natures. This raises a question: if al-hayz is identical to al-wad' (and thus more general than place), why is there still a need to consider it? The commentator then addresses objections. The phrase 'they may specify' is explained as an alternative view: al-hayz is place, but the statement 'every body has a position' applies only to bodies that have a place, excluding the outermost sphere. A reference to al-Isharat ('the body, if left to its nature, cannot but have a specific position') shows that it did not claim universality. An objection is raised: even the outermost sphere, if left to its nature, does not necessitate place in the sense of surface. The commentary on 'pointed to by here and there' argues that such pointing implies a relation 'in' (fi) to the body, which is not acceptable for al-wad'; instead, bodies are pointed to in themselves. Further, the parts of a rotating body: if they are hypothetical (i.e., mere parts), they do not undergo external motion; if actual (like planets), they change positions relative to fixed points but their transfer from place to place is not known necessarily. The passage ends by noting that al-hayz in the outermost sphere is al-wad' (the distinguishing state), but for bodies under another that have a surrounding, place is needed. A final note says that a continuation will come in the discussion of al-ayn (location) according to the philosophers.

الجسمية
الهيولى
المباحث المشرقية
الأين

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why cannot place be a relation of the parts of the body to something outside?
A) Because it would require the body to be in a place.
B) Because it would necessitate a strange effect.
C) Because parts have no relation.
D) Because place is only the surface.

Key Takeaway
The second proof rejects the idea that place is a relation of parts to something external, as it would imply a bizarre consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah or His creation that you have never questioned. What 'strange consequences' might it lead to if examined closely? Are you willing to test that belief with reason today?
?
This proof dismisses an idea because of a single absurd implication. In your own life, are there habits or assumptions you keep despite realizing they lead to harmful results? What would it take for you to abandon them?
?
The author calls the consequence 'strange' (غريب). Have you ever clung to a notion in your faith that, when honestly reflected upon, felt 'strange' or inconsistent? Will you commit to seeking clarity instead of avoiding the discomfort?

Al-Wad' in the Third Sense: Definition and Scope
Definition and scope of al-wad' (the third meaning).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third meaning of al-wad' as the body's capacity for sensory indication and its universality to all bodies.

Core Idea
Al-wad' (position) is an inherent property of every body, distinct from place as a surface.

This lesson focuses on al-wad' (position) in its third sense: the body’s ability to be pointed to in sensory indication. The author explains that this property is a necessary accident of corporeality (al-jismiyya) inhering in matter, and it is common to all bodies, even the outermost sphere. This sets the distinction between al-wad' and place (al-makan) as a surface, and raises a question about its generality relative to place.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-wad'
Read the first part where al-wad' is defined as 'the body being such that it admits sensory indication.' Note that it is said to be required by corporeality inhering in hyle, not by specific natures.
This definition comes from al-Isharat or al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.
2
Understand its universality
The passage then asserts that this type of al-wad' is an accident for every body, even if left to its natural state. It encompasses all bodies, including the outermost sphere, and is more general than place as a surface.
This universality is supported by reference to al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya.
3
Ponder the question raised
The author asks: if al-wad' is more general than place, what does it mean to say it is more general, and why do we need to consider this generality? This question sets up the next discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Objections and Clarifications on Place and Pointing
Series of objections and clarifications on place, pointing, and parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator addresses objections regarding the universality of place, the notion of pointing, and the nature of parts.

Core Idea
Place and pointing involve specific relations that differ from the inherent property of position.

This lesson addresses several objections and clarifications that follow from the definitions of place and al-wad'. The commentator tackles the claim that every body must have a place, the meaning of pointing 'here' and 'there', and the status of hypothetical parts in a rotating body. These clarifications are essential for a precise understanding of place as distinct from position.

Guided Path
1
Examine the objection about universality
Read the part where the commentator discusses the view that al-hayz is place, but only for bodies other than the outermost sphere. The reference to al-Isharat is used to support that even the outer sphere has a position but not a place in the surface sense.
The objection is that if left to nature, even the outer sphere would necessitate place as surface.
2
Understand the pointing objection
The argument about 'here' and 'there' is explained: pointing implies a relation 'in' (fi) the body, which is not suitable for al-wad'. Instead, bodies are pointed to in themselves.
This distinguishes between relating the body to a point and the body itself being pointed.
3
Clarify the nature of parts and motion
The final part discusses hypothetical parts of a rotating body: if parts are merely hypothetical (not real), they do not undergo motion. If actual, they can move. This supports the idea that parts are not real in the rotating sphere.
This is a subtle point about the ontology of parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من الوجوه الدالة على بطلان أن المكان هو يعرض بسبب نسبة أجزاء الجسم إلى غير الجسم، لأنه مما يقتضيه تأثير غريب . وأما الوضع بالمعنى الثالث وهو كون الجسم بحيث يقبل الإشارة الحسية فهو أمر تقتضيه الجسمية الحالة الهيولى وليس مما يتعلق بالطبائع المختلفة انتهى، ولا شك في أن الوضع بهذا المعنى عارض لكل جسم ولو خلي وطبعه فالحيز الطبيعي بمعنى الوضع شامل لجميع الأجسام على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن لكل جسم وضعاً وللفلك الأقصى وضع وهو مباين للمكان بمعنى السطح فما معنى قوله وهو أعم من المكان وما الحاجة إلى اعتبار عمومه . قوله : (لهم أن يخصوا إلخ ) جواب باختيار أنّ الحيز هو المكان والكلية مخصوص بما سوى المحدد. وإليه تشير عبارة الإشارات حيث قال : إِنَّ الجسم إذا خلي وطباعه لم يكن له بد من موضع معين حيث لم يقل : كل جسم ويرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن لو خلي الجسم ونفسه المكان بمعنى السطح، كيف وقد انتفى ذلك الاقتضاء في المحدد وأنَّ للمحيط مدخلاً يقتضي في ذلك . قوله : ( إنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك فيه أنَّ الإشارة بهنا وهناك يقتضي نسبة الجسم إليه بالنظر فيه، ونسبة الجسم إلى الوضع بفي لا يقبله العقل السليم فالوجه أن يقولوا : إِنَّ كل جسم فهو مشار إليه في نفسه ولا نسلم أنه مشار إليه بهنا وهناك. قوله : ( تلك الأجزاء مفروضة أي جزئيتها لأن نفس الأجزاء ليست مفروضة . قوله : ( وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل) أي مع وصف الجزئية . كان الحيز في المحدد هو الوضع. أي الحالة التي يتميز بها الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، فلم لم يكتف بهذه الحالة في سائر الأجسام وأثبت فيه الحيز بمعنى المكان . قلت : لضرورة أنَّ الجسم الممتاز في الإشارة الحسية الكائن تحت آخر له محيط به يوجد فيه الخواص المثبتة للمكان . قوله فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية الظاهر أن ذوات الأجزاء محققة، والفرضية جزئيتها وأن تحقق الذات يكفي لعروض الحركة الخارجية. وسيأتي تتمة لهذا الكلام في مباحث الأين على رأي الفلاسفة.
(The second) of the proofs indicating the falsehood that place occurs due to the relation of the parts of the body to something other than the body, because it would require a strange effect. As for al-wad' in the third sense, which is the body being such that it admits sensory indication, it is something required by corporeality (al-jismiyya) inhering in hyle, and it is not related to different natures. End. There is no doubt that al-wad' in this sense is an accident for every body, even if left to its nature. So the natural al-hayz meaning al-wad' encompasses all bodies, according to what is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: that every body has a wad', and the outermost sphere has a wad' which is distinct from place in the sense of surface. So what is the meaning of his statement that it is more general than place? And what need is there to consider its generality? His statement: 'They may specify (etc.)' is an answer by choosing that al-hayz is place, and the universality is restricted to other than the outermost sphere. The expression of al-Isharat points to that where it said: 'Indeed, the body if left to its nature cannot but have a specific position (mawdi')' as it did not say: 'every body'. And it is objected: we do not accept that if the body is left to itself, it necessitates place in the sense of surface; how has that necessitation ceased in the outermost sphere, and that the surrounding has an influence that necessitates that? His statement: 'Indeed, what is pointed to by here and there' — in it, that indication by 'here' and 'there' necessitates the relation of the body to it by 'in' (fi), and the relation of the body to al-wad' by 'in' is not accepted by the sound mind. So the proper way is that they say: 'Indeed, every body is pointed to in itself, and we do not accept that it is pointed to by here and there.' His statement: 'Those parts are hypothetical' means their being parts, because the parts themselves are not hypothetical. His statement: 'And if they actually exist' means with the description of being parts. Al-hayz in the outermost sphere was al-wad', i.e., the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory indication. So why was this state not sufficient for other bodies, and they proved al-hayz in the sense of place for them? I say: because of the necessity that the body distinguished in sensory indication that is under another body having a surrounding possesses properties that prove place. His statement: 'So it does not undergo external motion' — the apparent meaning is that the essences of the parts are realized, and the hypothetical nature is their being parts, and that the realization of the essence is sufficient for the occurrence of external motion. And a continuation of this discussion will come in the investigations of al-ayn according to the opinion of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage delivers the second proof that place is not the surface: if place were a relation of parts to something external, it would involve a strange effect. It then discusses the third meaning of al-wad' (position): the body's capacity to be pointed to in sensory indication, which is a necessary attribute of every body (even the outermost sphere) arising from corporeality inhering in matter, not from specific natures. This raises a question: if al-hayz is identical to al-wad' (and thus more general than place), why is there still a need to consider it? The commentator then addresses objections. The phrase 'they may specify' is explained as an alternative view: al-hayz is place, but the statement 'every body has a position' applies only to bodies that have a place, excluding the outermost sphere. A reference to al-Isharat ('the body, if left to its nature, cannot but have a specific position') shows that it did not claim universality. An objection is raised: even the outermost sphere, if left to its nature, does not necessitate place in the sense of surface. The commentary on 'pointed to by here and there' argues that such pointing implies a relation 'in' (fi) to the body, which is not acceptable for al-wad'; instead, bodies are pointed to in themselves. Further, the parts of a rotating body: if they are hypothetical (i.e., mere parts), they do not undergo external motion; if actual (like planets), they change positions relative to fixed points but their transfer from place to place is not known necessarily. The passage ends by noting that al-hayz in the outermost sphere is al-wad' (the distinguishing state), but for bodies under another that have a surrounding, place is needed. A final note says that a continuation will come in the discussion of al-ayn (location) according to the philosophers.

Key Terms
الأين
قوله
الكلية
المحدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pointing 'here' and 'there' establishes a relation similar to al-wad'.
Correction: Pointing 'here' and 'there' implies a relation 'in' the body, which is not how al-wad' works; bodies are pointed to in themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proper way to understand pointing to a body?
A) Every body is pointed to by here and there.
B) Every body is pointed to in itself, not by here and there.
C) Pointing is only applicable to the outermost sphere.
D) Pointing requires a relation of the body to a location.

Key Takeaway
The commentator clarifies that place and pointing involve different relations: pointing 'here' and 'there' does not capture the essence of al-wad', and the outermost sphere has position but not place in the surface sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you tend to define yourself by external pointers—where you live, your status, others' opinions—rather than by your intrinsic being before God? How can reflecting on the distinction between being 'pointed to in itself' versus 'pointed to by here and there' reshape your identity?
?
The outermost sphere has position without being contained in place. If God is described as having no place, have you ever allowed a subtle, hidden assumption that He is 'somewhere' to slip into your worship? What specific thought or attitude will you correct today to affirm His transcendence?
?
This lesson shows that even sophisticated thinkers can confuse types of relations (like pointing and position). In your own journey of faith, have you ever mistaken a human, relational concept for an attribute of God? Write down one concrete example from your past understanding and commit to re-learning it from revelation alone.

Place as Continuous Quantity and the Specification of 'Every Body Has a Place'
Sanusi defines place as continuous quantity and introduces a specification of the claim about bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of place as continuous quantity and the initial specification that every body occupies a place that excludes bodies without a place.

Core Idea
Place is more than just location; it is a continuous quantity related to what it contains.

In this passage, the commentator begins by defining place (al-makan) as a type of continuous quantity. He then presents a specification of the claim that every body is located in bodies that have a place, which excludes certain entities. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the nature of place and position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Note the definition of place as continuous quantity and its relation to what is under it.
2
Identify the specification
Find the phrase 'كل جسم ... لها مكان' and understand what it excludes.
3
Consider the alternative
Understand that the 'pointed to by here and there' may be the distinguishing state in sensory reference.
This is another way to repel the contradiction.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask why these specifications are needed and how they help the overall argument.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل فهو ونسبته بالقياس إلى ما تحته أمراً طبيعياً وأيضاً لهم أن يخصوا قولهم : كل جسم متحيز بالأجسام التي لها مكان فيخرج عنه ما لا مكان له وأن يقولوا : إِنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك قد يكون الحالة المميزة في الإشارة الحسية، وحينئذ تندفع المناقضة أيضاً، وأما حديث أجزاء المتحرك بالاستدارة فنقول : إن كانت تلك الأجزاء مفروضة، فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية قطعاً . وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل كالكواكب المنفصلة عن أجرام الأفلاك المركوزة هي فيها فالمعلوم من حالها بالضرورة تبدل أوضاعها بالقياس إلى الأمور الثابتة تبعاً للحركة الوضعية الحاصلة للفلك. وأما انتقالها من مكان إلى المكان فليس مما علم بالضرورة ( الثاني ) من الوجوه الدالة على بطلان أن المكان هو ... يعرض بسبب نسبة أجزاء الجسم إلى غير الجسم، لأنه مما يقتضيه تأثير غريب . وأما الوضع بالمعنى الثالث وهو كون الجسم بحيث يقبل الإشارة الحسية فهو أمر تقتضيه الجسمية الحالة الهيولى وليس مما يتعلق بالطبائع المختلفة انتهى، ولا شك في أن الوضع بهذا المعنى عارض لكل جسم ولو خلي وطبعه فالحيز الطبيعي بمعنى الوضع شامل لجميع الأجسام على ما في المباحث المشرقية أن لكل جسم وضعاً وللفلك الأقصى وضع وهو مباين للمكان بمعنى السطح فما معنى قوله وهو أعم من المكان وما الحاجة إلى اعتبار عمومه . قوله : (لهم أن يخصوا إلخ ) جواب باختيار أنّ الحيز هو المكان والكلية مخصوص بما سوى المحدد. وإليه تشير عبارة الإشارات حيث قال : إِنَّ الجسم إذا خلي وطباعه لم يكن له بد من موضع معين حيث لم يقل : كل جسم ويرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن لو خلي الجسم ونفسه المكان بمعنى السطح، كيف وقد انتفى ذلك الاقتضاء في المحدد وأنَّ للمحيط مدخلاً يقتضي في ذلك . قوله : ( إنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك فيه أنَّ الإشارة بهنا وهناك يقتضي نسبة الجسم إليه بالنظر فيه، ونسبة الجسم إلى الوضع بفي لا يقبله العقل السليم فالوجه أن يقولوا : إِنَّ كل جسم فهو مشار إليه في نفسه ولا نسلم أنه مشار إليه بهنا وهناك. قوله : ( تلك الأجزاء مفروضة أي جزئيتها لأن نفس الأجزاء ليست مفروضة . قوله : ( وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل) أي مع وصف الجزئية . كان الحيز في المحدد هو الوضع. أي الحالة التي يتميز بها الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، فلم لم يكتف بهذه الحالة في سائر الأجسام وأثبت فيه الحيز بمعنى المكان . قلت : لضرورة أنَّ الجسم الممتاز في الإشارة الحسية الكائن تحت آخر له محيط به يوجد فيه الخواص المثبتة للمكان . قوله فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية الظاهر أن ذوات الأجزاء محققة، والفرضية جزئيتها وأن تحقق الذات يكفي لعروض الحركة الخارجية. وسيأتي تتمة لهذا الكلام في مباحث الأين على رأي الفلاسفة.
The second observatory - the ninth objective: place, and it is of continuous quantity. So it and its relation by measurement to what is under it is a natural matter. And also they may specify their statement: every body is located in bodies that have a place, so it excludes that which has no place. And they may say: the pointed-to by 'here' and 'there' may be the distinguishing state in sensory pointing, and then the contradiction is also repelled. As for the talk about parts of the thing moving in rotation, we say: if those parts are assumed, then no external movement occurs to them absolutely. And if they exist actually, like the separate celestial bodies embedded in the spheres, then it is known necessarily from their state that their positions change relative to fixed things due to the positional movement occurring to the sphere. As for their transfer from place to place, it is not known necessarily. (Second) of the proofs showing that place is false... it occurs due to the relation of parts of the body to what is not the body, because it requires a strange effect. As for position in the third sense, which is the body being such that it accepts sensory reference, that is something required by corporeality in the state of hyle, and it is not related to the different natures. End. There is no doubt that position in this sense is an accident for every body, even if left to its nature. So natural space in the sense of position includes all bodies, according to what is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that every body has a position and the outermost sphere has a position, and it is distinct from place in the sense of surface. So what is the meaning of his saying it is more general than place, and what is the need to consider its generality? His saying: (They may specify etc.) is an answer by choosing that space is place and the universality is restricted to other than the limited. And the statement of al-Isharat indicates that, where he said: Indeed, the body, if left to its nature, must have a particular position, as he did not say: every body. And it is objected to it: We do not accept that if the body is left to itself, place in the sense of surface is left, how can that requirement be negated in the limited, and that the surrounding has an entrance that requires that. His saying: (Indeed, the pointed-to by 'here' and 'there') in it is that the pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires the relation of the body to it by consideration in it, and the relation of the body to position by 'fi' the sound intellect does not accept. So the correct way is to say: every body is pointed-to in itself, and we do not accept that it is pointed-to by 'here' and 'there'. His saying: (Those parts are assumed) means their partialness because the parts themselves are not assumed. His saying: (And if they exist actually) means with the description of partialness. The space in the limited is position, i.e., the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory reference. So why was this state not sufficient for other bodies, and space in the sense of place was established in it? I said: due to the necessity that the body distinguished in sensory reference which is under another that surrounds it, there exist in it the properties that establish place. His saying: So no external movement occurs to them. The apparent is that the essences of the parts are realized, and the assumption is of their partialness, and that the realization of the essence suffices for the occurrence of external movement. And a completion of this speech will come in the investigations of 'where' according to the opinion of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of place (al-makan) as position (al-wad') and addresses objections. The commentator begins by stating that place belongs to continuous quantity. He then presents the possibility of specifying the claim that every body occupies a place, excluding that which has no place (like the outermost sphere). The discussion involves the concept of 'pointed-to by here and there' as a distinguishing state in sensory reference. Then an objection about rotating bodies' parts is countered by distinguishing assumed parts from actual parts like celestial bodies, which change position relative to fixed things due to the sphere's motion, but not necessarily transfer from place to place. Next, the commentator examines the third meaning of position (al-wad'), which is the body being receptive to sensory pointing. This is required by corporeality itself and not by different natures. He asserts that position in this sense is an accident for every body, including the outermost sphere, and that natural space (al-hayyiz al-tabi'i) as position is more general than place as surface. He then questions why it is said to be more general and what need there is to consider this generality. Subsequent glosses explain that the response to choosing space as place is to restrict the universality to other than the limited (muhaddad). The statement from al-Isharat is cited, and an objection is raised: it is not accepted that if the body is left alone, the place is surface, because that requirement is negated in the limited. Another gloss discusses that pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires a relation of the body to the pointer, which the sound intellect does not accept. The correct view is that every body is pointed-to in itself. Further explanations deal with the parts being assumed or actual, and why the limited body has space as position while other bodies need space as place: because the limited body is under a surrounding that gives it properties establishing place. The passage ends with a note that this discussion will be continued in the investigations of 'where' (al-ayn) according to the philosophers.

الكم المتصل
الإشارة الحسية

According to this passage, place belongs to which category?
A) Discrete quantity
B) Continuous quantity

Key Takeaway
Place is a continuous quantity that has a natural relation to the body it contains, and the claim about bodies having places is specified to exclude those that don't.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in a physical body that occupies a place. But do you ever consider that your true essence might not be confined to that place? How does this distinction between place as continuous quantity and the possibility of existence without place affect your understanding of your own soul's relationship to the material world? Take a moment to reflect: Are you too attached to the physical places you inhabit, forgetting that your ultimate destination is a place beyond material dimensions? What concrete step will you take today to detach your heart from a specific place and orient it toward the eternal?
?
The passage speaks of a 'distinguishing state in sensory pointing.' When you point to something as 'here' or 'there,' are you aware that you might be pointing to a mere mental distinction rather than a real place? This is a reminder that your senses can be deceptive. When did you last assume that what you perceived with your senses was the complete truth, only to find it was limited? How can you train yourself to question your sensory perceptions in matters of faith? Write down one area where you need to rely more on revelation than on your senses.
?
Sanusi specifies that not every body has a place, excluding that which has no place. This implies that there are realities beyond spatial confinement. If God, the ultimate Reality, is not in a place, how should that change your worship and your sense of closeness to Him? Do you sometimes act as if God is distant because you cannot locate Him? Commit to a specific act of worship today that reminds you of His transcendence beyond place.

Objection from Rotating Bodies: Are Their Parts Moving?
Sanusi addresses an objection about rotating bodies and distinguishes two scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about the parts of a rotating body is addressed by distinguishing between assumed parts and actual parts.

Core Idea
Not all movement is external; assumed parts do not move, but actual parts like celestial bodies change position relative to fixed things.

In this section, the commentator addresses an objection concerning the parts of a body moving in rotation. He argues that if the parts are merely assumed (intellectually divided), they do not undergo external movement. However, if they exist actually, like the separate celestial bodies within spheres, they change position relative to fixed points due to the sphere's rotational motion. This distinction clarifies the nature of place and motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin with the phrase 'وأما حديث أجزاء المتحرك بالاستدارة' and understand the problem.
2
Analyze the two cases
The commentator presents two scenarios: parts assumed (مفروضة) and parts existing actually (موجودة بالفعل). Note the difference.
3
Examine the conclusion for each
For assumed parts, no external movement. For actual parts, position changes but not necessarily transfer from place to place.
This is key to understanding the response.

يخصوا إلخ ) جواب باختيار أنّ الحيز هو المكان والكلية مخصوص بما سوى المحدد. وإليه تشير عبارة الإشارات حيث قال : إِنَّ الجسم إذا خلي وطباعه لم يكن له بد من موضع معين حيث لم يقل : كل جسم ويرد عليه أنا لا نسلم أن لو خلي الجسم ونفسه المكان بمعنى السطح، كيف وقد انتفى ذلك الاقتضاء في المحدد وأنَّ للمحيط مدخلاً يقتضي في ذلك . قوله : ( إنَّ المشار إليه بهنا وهناك فيه أنَّ الإشارة بهنا وهناك يقتضي نسبة الجسم إليه بالنظر فيه، ونسبة الجسم إلى الوضع بفي لا يقبله العقل السليم فالوجه أن يقولوا : إِنَّ كل جسم فهو مشار إليه في نفسه ولا نسلم أنه مشار إليه بهنا وهناك. قوله : ( تلك الأجزاء مفروضة أي جزئيتها لأن نفس الأجزاء ليست مفروضة . قوله : ( وإن كانت موجودة بالفعل) أي مع وصف الجزئية . كان الحيز في المحدد هو الوضع. أي الحالة التي يتميز بها الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، فلم لم يكتف بهذه الحالة في سائر الأجسام وأثبت فيه الحيز بمعنى المكان . قلت : لضرورة أنَّ الجسم الممتاز في الإشارة الحسية الكائن تحت آخر له محيط به يوجد فيه الخواص المثبتة للمكان . قوله فلا يعرض لها حركة خارجية الظاهر أن ذوات الأجزاء محققة، والفرضية جزئيتها وأن تحقق الذات يكفي لعروض الحركة الخارجية. وسيأتي تتمة لهذا الكلام في مباحث الأين على رأي الفلاسفة.
The second observatory - the ninth objective: place, and it is of continuous quantity. So it and its relation by measurement to what is under it is a natural matter. And also they may specify their statement: every body is located in bodies that have a place, so it excludes that which has no place. And they may say: the pointed-to by 'here' and 'there' may be the distinguishing state in sensory pointing, and then the contradiction is also repelled. As for the talk about parts of the thing moving in rotation, we say: if those parts are assumed, then no external movement occurs to them absolutely. And if they exist actually, like the separate celestial bodies embedded in the spheres, then it is known necessarily from their state that their positions change relative to fixed things due to the positional movement occurring to the sphere. As for their transfer from place to place, it is not known necessarily. (Second) of the proofs showing that place is false... it occurs due to the relation of parts of the body to what is not the body, because it requires a strange effect. As for position in the third sense, which is the body being such that it accepts sensory reference, that is something required by corporeality in the state of hyle, and it is not related to the different natures. End. There is no doubt that position in this sense is an accident for every body, even if left to its nature. So natural space in the sense of position includes all bodies, according to what is in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that every body has a position and the outermost sphere has a position, and it is distinct from place in the sense of surface. So what is the meaning of his saying it is more general than place, and what is the need to consider its generality? His saying: (They may specify etc.) is an answer by choosing that space is place and the universality is restricted to other than the limited. And the statement of al-Isharat indicates that, where he said: Indeed, the body, if left to its nature, must have a particular position, as he did not say: every body. And it is objected to it: We do not accept that if the body is left to itself, place in the sense of surface is left, how can that requirement be negated in the limited, and that the surrounding has an entrance that requires that. His saying: (Indeed, the pointed-to by 'here' and 'there') in it is that the pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires the relation of the body to it by consideration in it, and the relation of the body to position by 'fi' the sound intellect does not accept. So the correct way is to say: every body is pointed-to in itself, and we do not accept that it is pointed-to by 'here' and 'there'. His saying: (Those parts are assumed) means their partialness because the parts themselves are not assumed. His saying: (And if they exist actually) means with the description of partialness. The space in the limited is position, i.e., the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory reference. So why was this state not sufficient for other bodies, and space in the sense of place was established in it? I said: due to the necessity that the body distinguished in sensory reference which is under another that surrounds it, there exist in it the properties that establish place. His saying: So no external movement occurs to them. The apparent is that the essences of the parts are realized, and the assumption is of their partialness, and that the realization of the essence suffices for the occurrence of external movement. And a completion of this speech will come in the investigations of 'where' according to the opinion of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of place (al-makan) as position (al-wad') and addresses objections. The commentator begins by stating that place belongs to continuous quantity. He then presents the possibility of specifying the claim that every body occupies a place, excluding that which has no place (like the outermost sphere). The discussion involves the concept of 'pointed-to by here and there' as a distinguishing state in sensory reference. Then an objection about rotating bodies' parts is countered by distinguishing assumed parts from actual parts like celestial bodies, which change position relative to fixed things due to the sphere's motion, but not necessarily transfer from place to place. Next, the commentator examines the third meaning of position (al-wad'), which is the body being receptive to sensory pointing. This is required by corporeality itself and not by different natures. He asserts that position in this sense is an accident for every body, including the outermost sphere, and that natural space (al-hayyiz al-tabi'i) as position is more general than place as surface. He then questions why it is said to be more general and what need there is to consider this generality. Subsequent glosses explain that the response to choosing space as place is to restrict the universality to other than the limited (muhaddad). The statement from al-Isharat is cited, and an objection is raised: it is not accepted that if the body is left alone, the place is surface, because that requirement is negated in the limited. Another gloss discusses that pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires a relation of the body to the pointer, which the sound intellect does not accept. The correct view is that every body is pointed-to in itself. Further explanations deal with the parts being assumed or actual, and why the limited body has space as position while other bodies need space as place: because the limited body is under a surrounding that gives it properties establishing place. The passage ends with a note that this discussion will be continued in the investigations of 'where' (al-ayn) according to the philosophers.

Key Terms
متحرك بالاستدارة
مفروضة
موجودة بالفعل
حركة وضعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the status of assumed parts of a rotating body regarding external movement?
A) They move externally
B) No external movement occurs
C) They change position but not place
D) They transfer from place to place

Key Takeaway
The objection from rotating bodies is resolved by distinguishing assumed parts (no movement) from actual parts (positional change but not necessarily place transfer).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that assumed parts do not move, but actual parts change position relative to fixed things. Think about a belief you hold about yourself—is it based on an 'assumed part' (something you imagine about yourself) or an 'actual part' (a real trait)? How does this distinction affect whether you feel compelled to change it? Will you examine one such belief today and decide if it is assumed or real?
?
Sanusi says that actual parts like celestial bodies change position relative to fixed things due to the sphere's movement, but they do not necessarily transfer from place to place. In your life, have you mistaken a change in your circumstances (positional) for a change in your very essence (place transfer)? How might that misperception be keeping you from seeing your true state before Allah? Make a commitment to identify one area where you've confused the two.

5 min
The Third Meaning of Position: Sensory Reference and Its Generality
Sanusi defines the third sense of position and argues for its universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third meaning of position (al-wad') as being receptive to sensory reference, and why it is more general than place.

Core Idea
Position in the sense of sensory reference is an accident of every body, including the outermost sphere, making it more general than place.

The commentator now introduces a third meaning of position (al-wad'), distinct from the earlier meanings. This meaning is that a body is such that it can be pointed to by sensory reference. He argues that this is required by corporeality itself and is independent of specific natures. This position (al-wad') is an accident for every body, even if left to its own nature. Thus, natural space (al-hayyiz al-tabi'i) in this sense includes all bodies, including the outermost sphere, and is more general than place as surface. The passage then questions the need to consider this generality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the third meaning
Start with 'وأما الوضع بالمعنى الثالث' and note what it entails.
2
Understand its cause
It is required by corporeality itself, not by different natures.
3
Recognize its universality
It is an accident for every body, even the outermost sphere.
4
Compare to place as surface
Natural space as position is more general than place; understand why.
The passage ends with a question about this generality.

Specification of the Claim and the Concept of Pointing
Sanusi comments on the earlier specification, addressing objections about pointing and the limited body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary on the earlier specification, particularly the response to objections about pointing and the nature of the limited body.

Core Idea
The specification of 'every body' is restricted to other than the limited; pointing involves a relation that the intellect does not accept if taken as 'in'.

In this section, the commentator provides glosses on the earlier statements. He explains that the specification of 'every body is located' to exclude the limited is a response that chooses to equate space (al-hayyiz) with place (al-makan). He cites al-Isharat and raises an objection: it is not accepted that if a body is left alone, place is surface. Then he discusses the concept of pointing by 'here' and 'there', arguing that it implies a relation that is not intellectually sound. The correct position is that every body is pointed-to in itself. He also clarifies that the limited body (the outermost sphere) has space as position because it is under a surrounding that gives it properties establishing place.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on the specification
Start with 'قوله : (لهم أن يخصوا إلخ )' and understand the response.
2
Analyze the citation from al-Isharat
Note the statement and the objection that follows.
3
Examine the discussion on pointing
Understand why pointing by 'here' and 'there' is problematic.
4
Understand the limited body's special case
Why does it have space as position? Because it is under a surrounding.

The second observatory - the ninth objective: place, and it is of continuous quantity. So it and its relation by measurement to what is under it is a natural matter. And also they may specify their statement: every body is located in bodies that have a place, so it excludes that which has no place. As for the talk about parts of the thing moving in rotation, we say: if those parts are assumed, then no external movement occurs to them absolutely. And if they exist actually, like the separate celestial bodies embedded in the spheres, then it is known necessarily from their state that their positions change relative to fixed things due to the positional movement occurring to the sphere. As for their transfer from place to place, it is not known necessarily. (Second) of the proofs showing that place is false... it occurs due to the relation of parts of the body to what is not the body, because it requires a strange effect. And it is objected to it: We do not accept that if the body is left to itself, place in the sense of surface is left, how can that requirement be negated in the limited, and that the surrounding has an entrance that requires that. His saying: (Indeed, the pointed-to by 'here' and 'there') in it is that the pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires the relation of the body to it by consideration in it, and the relation of the body to position by 'fi' the sound intellect does not accept. So the correct way is to say: every body is pointed-to in itself, and we do not accept that it is pointed-to by 'here' and 'there'. His saying: (Those parts are assumed) means their partialness because the parts themselves are not assumed. His saying: (And if they exist actually) means with the description of partialness. The space in the limited is position, i.e., the state by which the body is distinguished in sensory reference. So why was this state not sufficient for other bodies, and space in the sense of place was established in it? I said: due to the necessity that the body distinguished in sensory reference which is under another that surrounds it, there exist in it the properties that establish place. His saying: So no external movement occurs to them. The apparent is that the essences of the parts are realized, and the assumption is of their partialness, and that the realization of the essence suffices for the occurrence of external movement. And a completion of this speech will come in the investigations of 'where' according to the opinion of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of place (al-makan) as position (al-wad') and addresses objections. The commentator begins by stating that place belongs to continuous quantity. He then presents the possibility of specifying the claim that every body occupies a place, excluding that which has no place (like the outermost sphere). The discussion involves the concept of 'pointed-to by here and there' as a distinguishing state in sensory reference. Then an objection about rotating bodies' parts is countered by distinguishing assumed parts from actual parts like celestial bodies, which change position relative to fixed things due to the sphere's motion, but not necessarily transfer from place to place. Next, the commentator examines the third meaning of position (al-wad'), which is the body being receptive to sensory pointing. This is required by corporeality itself and not by different natures. He asserts that position in this sense is an accident for every body, including the outermost sphere, and that natural space (al-hayyiz al-tabi'i) as position is more general than place as surface. He then questions why it is said to be more general and what need there is to consider this generality. Subsequent glosses explain that the response to choosing space as place is to restrict the universality to other than the limited (muhaddad). The statement from al-Isharat is cited, and an objection is raised: it is not accepted that if the body is left alone, the place is surface, because that requirement is negated in the limited. Another gloss discusses that pointing by 'here' and 'there' requires a relation of the body to the pointer, which the sound intellect does not accept. The correct view is that every body is pointed-to in itself. Further explanations deal with the parts being assumed or actual, and why the limited body has space as position while other bodies need space as place: because the limited body is under a surrounding that gives it properties establishing place. The passage ends with a note that this discussion will be continued in the investigations of 'where' (al-ayn) according to the philosophers.

الإشارة

Correction: The commentator argues that this phrase implies an incorrect relation (using 'fi') and that the proper understanding is that every body is pointed-to in itself.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever accepted a theological claim without examining its logical implications? The commentator shows that even a term like 'pointing' demands precise analysis. The sound intellect rejects certain relations as inappropriate. When you speak about Allah, are you careful not to imply relations that are not befitting, like spatial containment? Reflect on the phrases you use in your prayers and conversations. Will you intentionally refine your language to avoid such implications?
?
The limited body is treated uniquely—it does not follow the same rules as others. Are there people or situations in your life that you treat as exceptions without justification? Refuting Place as Surface: The Bird and Moon Argument
Refutation of place as surface via absurd consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of place as encompassing surface leads to absurd conclusions through the bird and moon argument, and how later redefinitions of local motion attempt to avoid these absurdities.

Core Idea
If place is the surface, then a stationary bird is moving and a moving moon is stationary—an absurdity.

The nature of place (al-makan) was a central question in classical Islamic cosmology. One prominent definition held that place is the inner surface of the containing body. But what happens when we test this definition against everyday phenomena? This passage presents a powerful refutation using the examples of a bird in the wind and the moon in its sphere, showing that the surface definition leads to impossible consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Implication
If place is the surface, then local motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya) must be replacement of surfaces. This sets up the argument.
2
Examine the Bird Example
A bird standing still in blowing wind is stationary, but if place is the surface, it constantly replaces the surrounding air surfaces, implying it is moving—absurd.
3
Examine the Moon Example
The moon moves through the sky but does not replace the surface of its sphere; thus, if place is the surface, it would be stationary—absurd.
4
Analyze the Objection and Response
The objection redefines motion as change of relation to fixed things, not replacement of surfaces. The response shows this change is caused by motion, not the definition, so the absurdity remains.
The arguer did not claim actual motion in the bird, only that the surface definition implies it.

The second observatory - the ninth objective: place, and it is of continuous quantity. (The surface) That if place were the surface, it would necessitate the motion of the stationary while it is stationary (and the rest of the moving) while it is moving, and the consequent is evidently false. (As for the explanation of the implication, it is that the bird standing in the air) i.e., the blowing wind (is stationary) necessarily (and it follows) from the fact that place is the surface (its motion) in that state (since local motion) is nothing but replacement of place with another place. And there is no doubt that it (i.e., the bird in that state) is replacing the surfaces surrounding it that come upon it, so it is moving with local motion by replacing places. (And that the moon is moving) as you know (and it follows) from the fact that place is the surface (its rest) in the state of its motion (because it does not replace the surface in which it is fixed from its sphere. Likewise the case of one transported from country to country in a chest. (And it may be answered) i.e., about the second aspect (by denying the implication), i.e., we do not accept that if place were the surface, it would necessitate the motion of the stationary and the rest of the moving, and what was mentioned in its explanation is incomplete. (It), i.e., change of relation to fixed things, (is not realized in the bird) standing, so it is not moving despite the succession of surfaces upon it, but it is stationary. (It is realized in the moon) and in what is transported in the chest, so they are moving despite the absence of change of surfaces upon them. (And the answer to this answer is that change of relation to fixed things is caused by motion, for it is said: the body moved so its relation to fixed things changed. His saying: (in the air i.e., the wind). Al-hawa' in language is the atmosphere, and in their usages it is one of the elements. On the first, it is interpreted as wind by mentioning the place and intending the condition to correct its description as blowing. On the second, to indicate that its femininity is because al-hawa' is masculine by interpreting it as wind. His saying: (i.e., the blowing wind). He only prioritized the interpretation of al-hawa' as wind over its description as blowing, although the apparent is to delay, because the wind is the blowing air, indicating the aspect of femininity of 'al-habba' by interpreting al-hawa' as wind, and wind is feminine. God said: 'and a wind in which is a painful punishment' (al-Ahqaf: 24), and because the customary is to describe wind as blowing. His saying: (in a chest) such that all the interior of the chest contacts all the exterior of that transported body. His saying: (and the answer that change of relation is caused by motion, so its absence is by its absence. So if you said: if change is caused by motion in existence and non-existence, it is equivalent to it. So how can motion exist without change in the stationary bird? I said: The arguer did not claim the existence of motion in the mentioned bird in actual fact. Rather, its necessity from the interpretation of place as surface. So what you mentioned is another aspect of the corruption of the mentioned interpretation and is not harmful to the arguer.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a classical refutation of the definition of place (al-makan) as the encompassing surface (al-sath). The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if place were the surrounding surface, then a stationary object would be moving and a moving object would be stationary, which are absurd. The example of a bird standing in blowing wind is used: although the bird is stationary, if place is the surface, then since it is constantly replacing the surfaces of the air that come upon it, it would be undergoing local motion (al-haraka al-ayniyya) by replacing places. Similarly, the moon is moving through the sky, but it does not replace the surface of its sphere; rather, it is fixed in that surface, so it would be stationary if place were that surface. Likewise, a person transported in a chest that contacts its interior surfaces would not change surfaces, so he would be stationary despite moving. The objection (al-jawab) is to deny the implication by redefining local motion: it is not replacement of places, but change of relation to fixed things (taghayyur al-nisba ila al-umur al-thabita). Under this definition, the bird does not change relation to fixed things, so it is stationary; the moon and chest do change relation to fixed things, so they are moving despite not changing surfaces. The response to this objection is that change of relation is caused by motion, not identical to it. The arguer did not claim actual motion in the bird, but only that the surface definition would imply it—this itself shows the definition's corruption. The passage then provides lexical commentary on 'fi al-hawa' (in the air) explaining its interpretation as wind and the grammatical reasons for femininity, as well as a gloss on 'fi sunduq' (in a chest) meaning the chest contacts the transported body entirely.

Key Takeaway
The definition of place as surface leads to absurd consequences because it confuses the replacement of surfaces with genuine local motion, which is a change of relation to fixed things.

?

Motion as a Continuous State and Its Concomitant
Sanusi defines motion as a continuous state and distinguishes it from change of place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion is a continuous state inherent in the moving body, and that replacement of place is a necessary concomitant of motion, not identical to it.

Core Idea
Motion is not just change of place; it is an internal continuous state.

Sanusi addresses a subtle objection about the nature of motion by defining it precisely. He distinguishes between the motion itself, which is a continuous state, and the observable effect of changing places. This distinction is crucial for understanding causality and the nature of physical change. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to hear Sanusi's own words before analysis.
Note the technical terms: الحركة, حالة مستمرة, استبدال الأمكنة.
2
Identify the definition of motion
Locate where Sanusi defines motion as a continuous state (حالة مستمرة) from start to end.
3
Understand the concomitant relationship
Grasp that replacement of place (استبدال الأمكنة) is a lazim of motion, not its essence.
Lazim is a necessary concomitant.
4
Analyze the stationary bird example
The bird's feathers move in the wind, showing replacement without motion, disproving identity.
The replacement arises from external cause, not bird's own motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والحق) في الجواب عن الوجه الثاني ( أن الحركة ) الموجودة ( عندهم ) في الخارج ( حالة مستمرة ) للمتحرك ) من أول المسافة إلى (آخرها أي ثابتة له في كل حد من حدودها الواقعة فيما بين المبدأ والمنتهى، ومن المعلوم أن هذه الحالة ليست عين استبدال الأمكنة التي ( تسمى التوجه والوسط أيضاً ( واستبدال المكان لوازمها ) أي من لوازم الحالة التي هي الحركة لا عينها . ( فلا يتم الدليل إذ ليس يلزم من وجود اللازم في الطير الواقف وجود الملزم فيه أعني الحركة لجواز أن يكون اللازم أعم فإن استبدال الأمكنة إذا كان ناشئاً من المتمكن فيها كان حركة وإذا كان ناشئا من غيره
And the truth) in answer to the second aspect (is that motion) as it exists (according to them) externally (is a continuous state) for the moving body (from the beginning of the distance to) its end, i.e., constant for it at every limit of its limits lying between the starting point and the endpoint. And it is known that this state is not identical to the replacement of places, which (is also called 'direction' and 'intermediate'), and (replacement of place is a concomitant of them), i.e., a concomitant of the state which is motion, not its essence. (So the proof does not hold) because the existence of the concomitant in the stationary bird does not necessitate the existence of the necessitating entity in it, namely motion, since it is possible that the concomitant is more general. For the replacement of places, if it arises from the occupant in them, is motion; and if it arises from something else, (it is not).
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides the definitive answer (الحق) to the second objection regarding the nature of motion. It begins by defining motion: it is a continuous state (حالة مستمرة) that exists in the moving body from the start to the end of the distance traversed, persisting at every intermediate point. The author then clarifies that this state is not identical to the replacement of places (استبدال الأمكنة), also called 'direction' (التوجه) and 'intermediate' (الوسط). Rather, replacement of place is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of motion, not its essence. To illustrate, the author uses the example of a bird standing still in a blowing wind: the bird experiences replacement of place (its feathers are displaced) but is not in motion because the replacement arises from the external wind, not from the bird itself. This shows that the concomitant is more general than the motion—it can occur without motion when the cause is external. Therefore, the opponent's argument that motion is nothing but replacement of place fails, since the presence of replacement does not prove the presence of motion.

Key Terms
الحركة (motion)
حالة مستمرة (continuous state)
استبدال الأمكنة (replacement of places)
لازم (concomitant)
ملزوم (necessitating entity)
الطير الواقف (stationary bird)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is identical to the replacement of place.
Correction: Motion is a continuous state; replacement of place is a concomitant that can occur without motion when caused externally.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how is motion defined in relation to the replacement of places?
A) Motion is identical to the replacement of places.
B) Motion is a continuous state, and replacement of places is a concomitant of it.
C) Motion is the same as the direction and the intermediate.
D) Motion is defined by the absence of replacement of places.

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined as a continuous state inherent in the moving body, while replacement of place is merely a necessary concomitant that can occur without motion when the cause is external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a recent action you performed that you thought was from your own free will. Could it be that what you considered 'your motion' was actually just a replacement of place caused by external pressures—like social expectation or habit? How can you ensure your deeds arise from an internal, conscious choice (motion) rather than mere reaction (replacement)?
?
If motion is defined as a continuous state initiated by the moving body itself, what does that imply about your spiritual journey toward Allah? Are you moving because you are genuinely pulled from within, or are you being pushed by circumstances? What would it take to make your worship a true motion of the heart rather than a habitual replacement of actions?
?
The stationary bird experiences replacement of place (feathers moved by wind) but is not in motion. In your life, are there areas where you are 'being moved' by external forces—cultural trends, peer pressure, or even spiritual lethargy—while mistaking it for genuine progress? How can you identify the true source of your actions and cultivate inner motion toward what pleases Allah?
