المكان (al-makan)
البعد (al-bu'd)
الخلاء (al-khala')
المتصل (al-muttasil)
الكم (al-kamm)
السطح (al-sath)
المقعر (al-muq'arr)
المحدب (al-muhaddab)
المتوسط (al-mutawassit)
الهيولى (al-hayula)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument from imagination proves the real existence of a vacuum in the physical world.
Correction: The author shows that the argument only proves the existence of a dimension as a mental conception distinct from physical impossibility. The imagined vacuum is not claimed to be physically real; it is a thought experiment to reveal an underlying simple existent.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Razi's objection to the argument that place is the existing dimension?
A) The dimension cannot be quantified.
B) The imagined vacuum is impossible, so its consequent may also be impossible.
C) The vessel has both concave and convex surfaces.
D) The dimension is a mental fabrication.

Key Takeaway
The concept of place as a dimension is defended through imaginative analysis, but critics argue that the impossibility of the vacuum renders the dimension merely conceptual; the debate highlights the role of mental supposition in kalām.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Place as Supposed Dimension: The Vacuum Debate
Defines the third view of place as supposed dimension (vacuum) and discusses disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third theory of place as a supposed dimension (vacuum) and the key disagreements between theologians and philosophers regarding its existence.

Core Idea
Vacuum is a supposed dimension that philosophers deny inside the world but all agree is non-existent outside.

This passage introduces the third view of place as a supposed dimension (vacuum), then immediately plunges into the controversies it sparked. The mutakallimun accept it, the philosophers reject it—except that philosophers themselves disagree about whether an existing dimension can be empty. A key argument from quantification structures the debate, drawing a line between inside and outside the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Let the argument unfold before interpreting.
Notice the definition of vacuum as a supposed dimension between two non-touching bodies.
2
Identify the definition of vacuum
The passage defines vacuum as a supposed dimension (al-bu'd al-mafrud) between two bodies that do not touch, with nothing in between—empty but suitable for a third body.
3
Trace the two layers of disagreement
First: mutakallimun permit vacuum, philosophers prohibit it. Second: among philosophers who hold place as existing dimension, some allow it to be empty, others do not.
Note that those who permit agree with mutakallimun on the possibility but differ on whether the dimension is imaginary.
4
Understand the quantification argument
Philosophers argue that what is between bodies is quantifiable, and non-existence cannot be quantified, so vacuum cannot exist inside the world. Outside the world, quantification is absent, so all agree vacuum is non-existent.
This argument is used to deny vacuum inside but not outside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثالث في المكان أنه البعد المفروض ، وهو الخلاء وحقيقته أن يكون الجسمان بحيث لا يتماسان وليس أيضاً بينهما ما يماسهما فيكون ما بينهما بعداً موهوماً ممتداً في الجهات صالحاً لأن يشغله جسم ثالث لكنه الآن خال عن الشاغل وجوزه المتكلمون ومنعه الحكماء القائلون بأنَّ المكان هو السطح، وأما القائلون بأنه البعد الموجود فهم أيضاً يمنعون الخلاء بالتفسير المذكور، أعني البعد المفروض فيما بين الأجسام لكنهم اختلفوا فمنهم من لم يجوز خلو البعد الموجود عن جسم شاغل له ومنهم من جوزه، فهؤلاء المجوزون وافقوا المتكلمين في جواز المكان الخالي عن الشاغل، وخالفوهم في أن ذلك المكان بعد موهوم فالحكماء كلهم متفقون على امتناع الخلاء بمعنى البعد المفروض لما مر من التقدر. فإنّ ما بين الجسمين اللذين لا يتماسان قابل للتقدر بالتنصيف وغيره ومتصف بالتفاوت مقيساً إلى ما بين جسمين آخرين لا يتماسان كما عرفته ولا شيء من المعدوم، كذلك فما بين المذكورين أمر موجود إما جسم كما هو رأي القائل بالسطح، وإما بعد مجرد رأي القائل به وهذا الخلاف إنما هو في الخلاء داخل العالم بناء على كونه متقدراً قطعاً وإن تقدره. هل يقتضي وجوده في الخارج أو لا ؟ وأما الخلاء خارج العالم فمتفق عليه إذ لا تقدر هناك بحسب نفس الأمر فالنزاع فيما وراء العالم، إنما هو في التسمية بالبعد فإنه عند الحكماء عدم محض ونفي صرف يثبته الوهم قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ) أي لا نسلم عدم الفرق فإن الحقيقة المكانية تقتضي امتلاء المكان بالمتمكن ينسب إليه بكلمة في وهو متحقق في السطح المحيط دون المحاط . قوله : ( وحقيقته أن يكون إلخ فيه تسامح فإنه لازم لحقيقته وحقيقته الفراغ المحدود بين الجسمين. قوله : ( وجوزه) أي الفراغ المحدود بين الجسمين. قوله : ( متفقون إلخ ) إنما الخلاف بينهم في الخلاء، بمعنى خلو المكان عن الشاغل . قوله : ( وإن تقدره) عطف على قوله الخلاء. فالحكماء يقولون : إن التقدر يقتضي الوجود والمتكلمون يمنعونه . قوله : ( وحقيقته أن يكون الجسمان إلخ) حقيقة الخلاء المتنازع فيه لا حقيقة الخلاء مطلقاً بقرينة قوله : بعد ذكر الاختلاف فيه، وأما الخلاء خارج العالم فمتفق عليه فلا يلزم أن لا يكون للمحدد مكان عند المتكلمين.
The third possibility concerning place: that it is the supposed dimension, which is vacuum. Its reality is that two bodies be such that they do not touch, and there is also nothing between them that touches them, so what is between them is an imaginary dimension extended in directions, fit for a third body to occupy, but now empty of an occupant. The mutakallimun permitted it and the philosophers prohibited it – those who say that place is the surface. As for those who say that it is the existing dimension, they also prohibit vacuum according to the mentioned interpretation, i.e., the supposed dimension between bodies, but they differed: some of them did not permit the existing dimension to be empty of an occupying body, and some permitted it. So those permitters agreed with the mutakallimun in permitting the place empty of an occupant, but disagreed with them in that that place is an imaginary dimension. For the philosophers all agree on the impossibility of vacuum in the sense of the supposed dimension, due to what passed about quantification. For what is between two bodies that do not touch is susceptible to quantification by division and other means, and is characterized by difference when measured against what is between two other bodies that do not touch, as you have known, and nothing non-existent is like that. So what is between the mentioned [bodies] is an existent thing: either a body, as is the view of the proponent of the surface, or a mere dimension, the view of its proponent. And this disagreement is only about vacuum inside the world, based on its being certainly quantifiable. Is its quantification necessitating its existence externally or not? As for vacuum outside the world, it is agreed upon, for there is no quantification there according to the actual state. The dispute about what is beyond the world is only in the naming of it as dimension, for according to the philosophers it is pure non-existence and sheer negation that the imagination affirms. His statement (And it may be said, etc.): i.e., we do not accept the lack of difference, for the reality of place requires the filling of the place by the occupant, related to it by the word 'in', and this is realized in the surrounding surface but not the surrounded. His statement (And its reality is that it be, etc.): there is looseness in it, for it is a necessary consequence of its reality, and its reality is the limited void between two bodies. His statement (And he permitted it): i.e., the limited void between two bodies. His statement (They agree, etc.): the disagreement between them is only about vacuum in the sense of the emptiness of place from an occupant. His statement (And its quantification): coordinated with his statement 'the vacuum'. The philosophers say: quantification necessitates existence, and the mutakallimun deny it. His statement (And its reality is that the two bodies be, etc.): the reality of the disputed vacuum, not the reality of vacuum absolutely, by the context of his statement after mentioning the disagreement about it. And as for vacuum outside the world, it is agreed upon, so it does not follow that the bounded [body] has no place according to the mutakallimun.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the third option for the nature of place: it is the supposed dimension (al-bu'd al-mafrud), i.e., vacuum (al-khala'). The definition given: two bodies not touching with nothing in between, leaving an imaginary extension suitable for a third body but currently empty. The mutakallimun (theologians) permit vacuum, while the philosophers (al-hukama') prohibit it. Among those who hold place to be an existing dimension, some allow the dimension to be empty (agreeing with mutakallimun) and some do not. All philosophers agree that vacuum in the sense of a supposed dimension is impossible because what is between bodies is quantifiable, and non-existence cannot be quantified. The dispute is only about vacuum inside the world, where quantification is certain; does quantification demand external existence? Outside the world, vacuum is agreed to be non-existent. The commentary then clarifies: the reality of place requires the occupant to fill it (related by 'in'), which is realized in the surrounding surface. The definition of vacuum is the limited void, and the disagreement is about empty space, not naming. The quantification argument: for philosophers, quantification implies existence; mutakallimun deny that. The final note distinguishes the vacuum under dispute from absolute vacuum.

Key Terms
الاحتمال الثالث
البعد المفروض
الخلاء
المتكلمون
الحكماء
التقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimun and philosophers disagree on all forms of vacuum.
Correction: The passage shows they agree that vacuum outside the world is non-existent; they only disagree about vacuum inside the world.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main point of agreement between philosophers and mutakallimun regarding vacuum?
A) Vacuum inside the world is possible.
B) Vacuum outside the world is impossible.
C) The supposed dimension is non-existent everywhere.
D) Vacuum outside the world is agreed to be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The third view of place as a supposed dimension (vacuum) is rejected by philosophers inside the world due to quantification, but all agree vacuum outside the world is non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that our imagination can project existence onto non-existence (vacuum as supposed dimension). Where in your life are you treating imagined concepts as if they are actually real, causing you to stray from the truth that only comes from revelation?
?
Philosophers agreed that what is quantifiable must exist. Do you measure your spiritual progress by things that are quantifiable (e.g., actions, knowledge) at the expense of that which is truly real but not measurable (e.g., sincerity, reliance on God)? How will you realign your priorities today?
?
This lesson highlights that even in disagreement, there are points of agreement (vacuum outside world is non-existent). Think of a person you disagree with on a matter of faith. What is one point of agreement you can affirm to start a meaningful dialogue, rather than focusing on the dispute?

Definition and Proof of the Smooth Surface
Defines the smooth surface and proves its existence via two alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a smooth surface and the logical proof that establishes its existence by showing the impossibility of the two alternatives.

Core Idea
A smooth surface must exist because its absence leads to absurd contradictions.

In this passage, the theologian presents a rigorous logical proof for the existence of a smooth surface, which is central to the argument for the possibility of void. The proof proceeds by assuming a surface with equal-positioned parts and showing that if it is not smooth, we are led to either a contradiction or an infinite regress. This establishes the necessary existence of a smooth surface.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of smooth surface
Carefully read the Arabic definition of the smooth surface: a surface whose supposed parts are equal in position and continuous, with no gaps (whether penetrating as pores or non-penetrating as angles).
Pay attention to the two types of gaps: مسام (pores) and زوايا (angles).
2
Understand the two alternatives
The proof assumes a surface with equal-positioned parts. If it is not smooth, the non-smoothness must be due either to (1) actual discontinuity between parts or (2) the presence of angles. These are the two alternatives that the proof eliminates.
The proof aims to show that both alternatives are false, so the surface must be smooth.
3
Follow the elimination of the first alternative
The first alternative (non-continuity) is dismissed by intuition: a body's surface must have continuous parts between openings; otherwise, it would consist of separate discrete parts, which is absurd.
This step relies on a basic intuition about surfaces.
4
Follow the elimination of the second alternative
The second alternative (angles) is eliminated by showing that placing smaller parts to fill angles leads either to elimination of angles or to an infinite actual division, which is impossible. Therefore, the angles must be eliminated, proving the surface is smooth.
Note that 'actual division' (انقسام فعلي) means real separation, not just conceptual.
5
Conclude the proof
Since both alternatives are false, the initial assumption that a smooth surface does not exist is false. Therefore, a smooth surface exists, establishing the desired conclusion.
This proof is an example of proof by contradiction (إثبات بالخلف).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Second Observatory - The Ninth Aim: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. He estimates it from himself, and no consideration is given to his estimation that does not correspond to the thing itself. Its right is that there is no void either. (According to the theologians) it is (a) imaginary dimension (بعد موهوم) like what is assumed between bodies according to their view. (They have) for proving the permissibility of void (خلاء) meaning empty place devoid of occupant (شاغل) (two aspects). The first is that the existence of a smooth surface (صفحة ملساء) is not impossible; otherwise, either non-continuity of parts (عدم اتصال الأجزاء) or the going of angles (زوايا) to infinity would follow. Explanation of that: The smooth surface is that whose supposed parts are equal in position and continuous such that there is no gap (فرج) between those parts, whether penetrating (نافذة) called pores (مسام) or non-penetrating called angles (زوايا). So if we assume a surface whose parts are equal in position, then if it is smooth, that is what is sought; otherwise, its non-smoothness is either due to non-continuity of parts in reality—that is false, for the surface of a body, even if it may have penetrating pores, must have between every two openings or between only two openings of its openings a continuous surface that we are concerned with; otherwise the surface would consist of separate discrete parts in reality, and that is false by intuition—or due to the existence of angles between its parts. Then we make other parts in it; if the angles are eliminated, the desired result is obtained; otherwise, they become smaller than they were, so we place other parts in them. Then either they are eliminated or the angles go in actual division to infinity. The second is false, so the first is determined, and it becomes [smooth]... [Commentary follows on the definitions and logical steps.]
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by stating that according to the theologians, place is an imaginary dimension, and they have two arguments for the possibility of void. The first argument is based on the impossibility of a smooth surface not existing. The passage then defines a smooth surface as one whose hypothetical parts are equal in position and continuous, without any gaps (whether penetrating as pores or non-penetrating as angles). The proof proceeds by assuming a surface with equal-positioned parts. If it is smooth, the desired conclusion (that smooth surfaces exist) is reached. If not, the lack of smoothness must be due to either (1) actual discontinuity of parts, which is false because a body's surface must have a continuous surface between openings, otherwise it would consist of separate parts, which is absurd; or (2) the presence of angles. In the latter case, we place smaller parts to fill the angles. If the angles disappear, we are done. If they persist but become smaller, we repeat the process. Eventually, either the angles are eliminated, or they undergo actual division infinitely, which is impossible. Therefore, the first alternative (elimination) must occur, proving the existence of a smooth surface. The included commentary clarifies key terms: 'equal in position' means at the same level whether flat or curved; 'non-penetrating gaps' do not contradict continuity but affect equal positioning; 'pores' are holes; 'actual division' refers to external distinction of parts, not mere conceptual division.

مسام
زوايا

بعد موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes the existence of a smooth surface rather than proving it.
Correction: The proof does not assume existence; it assumes a surface with equal-positioned parts and then shows that if it were not smooth, contradictions arise, thereby proving it must be smooth.

Checkpoint

A) Pores and angles
B) Non-continuity of parts and existence of angles
C) Imaginary division and infinite regression
D) Equal positioning and continuity

Key Takeaway
The smooth surface exists because its non-existence leads to either a violation of intuition (discrete parts) or an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demands that you accept the impossibility of an infinite regress of actual divisions. Have you ever avoided following a chain of reasoning about your own faith because it seemed to lead to an uncomfortable infinite regress? What belief are you afraid to trace back to its logical end?
?
The smooth surface is defined as having parts equal in position and continuous. Reflect on your own spiritual state: Are the 'parts' of your life—your actions, thoughts, and intentions—aligned and continuous in their devotion to Allah, or are there 'angles' of hypocrisy and 'gaps' of heedlessness? What small 'corner' can you fill today to bring more consistency?
?
The proof eliminates one alternative after another until only the truth remains. Is there a false belief or habit you have been protecting from scrutiny because you fear that if it falls, everything else might unravel? Are you willing to submit to the logic of revelation, even if it means letting go of a comfortable assumption?

Objection and Response on Constructing a Smooth Surface
Objection and answer about constructing a smooth surface from metal and lead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the theoretical possibility of a smooth surface and void against empirical objections involving pores and angles.

Core Idea
A smooth surface is possible theoretically even if practical attempts fail.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection that claims a smooth surface can be produced by melting lead on iron. He shows why such empirical constructions do not disprove the theoretical need for a void, and refines the argument with a discussion of angles and air trapping.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the objector's claim that melting lead on iron and separating them yields a smooth surface.
2
Understand why the commentator says mere melting is insufficient
The commentator argues that pores in the iron would allow air to fill gaps, so no void is produced. This refutes the objector's claim.
Pores (مسام) are key to the argument.
3
Examine the role of angles
The commentator concedes that angles do not harm the argument, because the aim is to show the possibility of a smooth surface, which is clearer without angles.
4
Consider the counter-objection about very small angles
The objector further says that if the angle is very small, air enters but lead cannot, so the claim 'we place parts in it' fails. The commentator acknowledges and says to reflect.
This shows the dialectical nature of the text.
5
Reflect on the commentator's conclusion
The passage ends with a call to reflect (فليتأمل), indicating the argument is subtle and requires careful thought.
The next objection begins with 'otherwise its non-smoothness'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الأول أنَّه لا يمتنع وجود صفحة ملساء قيل : إذا اتخذنا صفحة من حديد وأذبنا مثل الرصاص عليه ثم فصلنا أحدهما عن الآخر حصل المقصود سواء ثبت الملاسة أم لا ومنع الانطباق مكابرة وأنت خبير بأن مجرد ما ذكر لا يكفي إذ لو وجد فيها مسام جاورها الهواء لم يلزم الخلاء لانجذاب الهواء إلى البين مع ارتفاع ذلك المذاب . نعم ثبوت الزاوية لا يضر في المقصود فبيان إمكان الصفحة الملساء لكونها أظهر فيه فإن قلت : الزاوية إذا كانت صغيرة جداً دخلها الهواء واحتقن فيها للطاقتها ولا يدخلها الرصاص ونحوه . قلت : فحينئذ لا يتم قوله : فنضع فيها أجزاء فليتأمل.
قوله : ( وإلا فعدم ملاستها إلخ فإن قلت : الترديد بين عدم الاتصال وبين وجود الزوايا
He said: (The first: that the existence of a smooth surface is not impossible) It was said: If we take a surface of iron and melt something like lead upon it, then separate one from the other, the desired result is obtained whether smoothness is established or not, and denying the fit is obstinacy. And you know that mere mention is not sufficient, for if there are pores in it, air would be adjacent to it; void would not necessarily follow because air would be attracted into the interval when that molten substance rises. Yes, the existence of an angle does not harm the purpose, so the demonstration of the possibility of a smooth surface is clearer in it. For if you say: If the angle is very small, air enters it and becomes trapped due to its subtlety, and lead and the like do not enter it. I say: Then his statement 'we place parts in it' is not realized, so reflect. “His statement: (Otherwise its non-smoothness etc.) For if you say: The disjunction between non-continuity and existence of angles...

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer within the commentary. The objector argues that a smooth surface can be practically made by melting lead on an iron sheet and then separating them; even if the surface is not perfectly smooth, the fit suffices, and denying the fit is stubborn. The commentator replies that this is insufficient because if there are pores, air will fill them when the lead rises, so no void results. However, the presence of an angle (non-penetrating gap) does not harm the argument, because the aim is to show the possibility of a smooth surface, which is more evident. A further objection: if the angle is very small, air enters but lead cannot, so the claim 'we place parts in it' fails. The commentator acknowledges this and says to reflect. The passage then begins a new objection about the disjunction between non-continuity and angles.

Hypothetical Assumptions in Kalam
Addresses an objection about impossible assumptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars handle objections by distinguishing hypothetical assumptions from actual reality.

Core Idea
A hypothetical assumption does not need to be actual to be valid.

In theological arguments, it is common to make an assumption for the sake of proof. But what if an opponent objects that the assumption itself is impossible? Sanusi's commentator shows us the key move: the assumption does not have to be real—it only needs to be conceivable for the argument. This lesson unpacks that subtle but crucial point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the reply with 'قلت' (I say).
The objector claims the assumption has 'no justification' because angles disprove equality.
2
Identify the core distinction
The commentator distinguishes between assuming something and it being actual in reality (نفس الأمر). Find where he says the assumption does not entail actualization.
This is the key: hypothetical vs. actual.
3
Understand the response to 'if it is smooth'
The original proof assumed a smooth surface. The commentator explains that 'smooth' means smooth in reality, which is exactly what the assumption posits, so no contradiction arises.
The assumption and the reality can match in the hypothetical frame.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how this style of reasoning protects theological arguments from certain objections by clarifying the nature of assumptions.
This is a common kalam technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على تقدير فرض تساوي وضع الأجزاء مما لا وجه له لأن وجود الزوايا لا يجامع التساوي قلت : فرض التساوي لا يستلزم تحققه في نفس الأمر، ومعنى قوله : فإن كانت ملساء أنها كانت ملساء في نفس الأمر كما هو كذلك على الفرض فلا محذور.
On the supposition of assuming equal positioning of parts, it has no justification because the existence of angles does not comport with equality. I say: The assumption of equality does not entail its actualization in reality, and the meaning of his statement 'if it is smooth' is that it is smooth in reality, just as it is so on the assumption, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that assuming the parts are equally positioned is unjustified because if angles exist, equality is contradicted. The commentator replies that the assumption of equality does not require it to be actual in reality; it is a hypothetical. The meaning of 'if it is smooth' in the original proof is that the surface is smooth in reality, and that is exactly what is assumed for the sake of argument. Therefore, there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
فرض تساوي
زوايا
ملاسة
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an assumption is impossible in reality, it cannot be used in a proof.
Correction: The passage clarifies that assumptions are hypothetical and do not require actual existence; they only need to be conceivable for the argument.

Checkpoint

A) He admits the assumption is false
B) He says the assumption is hypothetical and does not need to be actual
C) He argues that angles cannot exist with equal positioning
D) He changes the definition of smooth surface

Key Takeaway
In kalam, a hypothetical assumption does not need to be actualized in reality to serve as a valid premise in an argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection: Imaginary Gaps and the Possibility of Void
The commentator refutes an objection about imaginary gaps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the infinite divisibility argument against the objection of imaginary gaps.

Core Idea
Imaginary gaps do not threaten the argument because they do not trap air.

This lesson examines a specific objection to the argument for infinite divisibility: what if an imaginary gap remains that cannot be filled by a part? The commentator carefully distinguishes between real external gaps and merely imaginary ones, showing that only the real gaps affect the argument. The response also introduces subtle physical considerations about air and rarefaction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the argument for infinite divisibility involves placing parts into any gap. The objection tries to find a gap that cannot be filled.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the structure: a statement by the author, an objection, and the commentator's reply.
3
Analyze the objection
Identify the objection: an imaginary gap that cannot receive an external part. The commentator refutes it.
4
Examine the reply
The reply distinguishes between real external gaps and imaginary gaps. Imaginary gaps do not trap air, so they pose no problem.
The commentator adds a caveat about air's subtlety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فنضع فيها أجزاء أخرى هذا جار في المسام أيضاً. وإنما لم يذكره هناك لعدم الاحتياج إليه . فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يبقى فرجة وهمية لا يمكن أن يوضع فيه جزء خارجي؟ قلت : الفرجة الواقعة في الخلال فرجة خارجية البتة ولو سلم فالفرجة الوهمية لا يقدح في المقصود إذ لا يحتقن فيه الهواء بحسب الخارج، كما لا يخفى فلا محذور. اللهم إلا أن يصار إلى أن ما أشرت إليه من الفرجة لغاية صغرها يدخل فيه الهواء للطاقته دون غيره من الأجسام التي لا تقبل التخلخل فتدبر
His statement: (Then we place other parts in it) applies also to pores. He only did not mention it there because there was no need. For if you say: Why is it not permissible for an imaginary gap (فرجة وهمية) to remain, into which an external part cannot be placed? I say: The gap occurring in the interstices is absolutely an external gap; and even if it is granted, the imaginary gap does not harm the purpose, since air does not become trapped in it externally, as is not hidden, so there is no problem. Unless one considers that the gap I referred to, due to its extreme smallness, air enters it because of its subtlety, unlike other bodies that do not allow rarefaction, so reflect.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer about the possibility of an imaginary gap that cannot be filled by a part. The commentator first notes that the process of placing parts also applies to pores but was not mentioned earlier. The objector asks: Why cannot an imaginary gap remain that no external part can fill? The reply: The gap in the interstices is an external gap, not merely imaginary. Even if one concedes an imaginary gap, it does not harm the argument because air does not become trapped externally. However, the commentator adds a caveat: if the gap is extremely small, air may enter due to its subtlety, but other bodies do not rarefy to fill it. This subtle point is left for the reader to ponder.

Key Terms
فرجة وهمية
مسام
هواء
خلاء
تخلخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an imaginary gap that cannot be filled by a part proves the impossibility of complete void or some problem.
Correction: The commentator explains that imaginary gaps do not affect the argument because they do not trap air externally; only real external gaps matter.

Checkpoint

A) Imaginary gaps can be filled by air
B) Imaginary gaps are actually external gaps
C) Imaginary gaps do not affect the argument because air does not become trapped externally

Key Takeaway
Imaginary gaps do not harm the argument for infinite divisibility because they cannot trap air externally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Experiment Against Void and Interpenetration
Al-Mawaqif describes an experiment to prove void and interpenetration impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical argument presented by al-Mawaqif against the possibility of void (khala') and interpenetration (tadakhul).

Core Idea
A clever experiment with a sealed tube suggests that nature abhors void.

In this passage, al-Mawaqif describes a physical experiment that seems to demonstrate the impossibility of void and interpenetration. Theologians often used such empirical evidence to support their metaphysical claims. Let's examine the reasoning and the setup step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the experiment description
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the setup: a sealed tube inserted into a sealed flask.
Pay attention to the sealing details—they are crucial for the argument.
2
Visualize the actions and results
Imagine pushing the tube further into the flask and pulling it out. Notice that the flask breaks outward in the first case and inward in the second.
Why does it break outward when pushed in? The passage explains this.
3
Understand the logical inference
The passage argues that if the flask could accommodate change without void or interpenetration, it would not break. The breakage shows it cannot, so void and interpenetration are impossible.
This is a modus tollens argument: if there were void, no breakage; breakage occurred, so no void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضعنا أنبوبة مسدودة الرأس أو خشبة مستوية (في قارورة) بحيث يكون بعض الأنبوبة في داخل القارورة وبعضها خارجاً عنها ( وسددنا رأسها بحيث لا يدخلها هواء ولا يخرج عنها ) وذلك بأن نسد الخلل بين عنق القارورة والأنبوبة سداً لا يمكن نفوذ الهواء فيه ) فإذا أدخلنا الأنبوبة فيها أكثر مما كان بحيث لا يخرج شيء من الهواء عنها ( انكسرت ( القارورة إلى خارج وإذا أخرجناها عنها) بحيث لا يدخل فيها شيء من الهواء ( انكسرت) إلى داخل ولولا أنها مملوءة) بالهواء وما فيها من الأنبوبة بحيث لا تحتمل شيئاً آخراً (لم تكن كذلك ) أي لم تنكسر بالإدخال إلى خارج ولولا أنها يستحيل خلوها عما يكون شاغلاً لها مالكاً إياها لم تنكسر بالإخراج إلى داخل فدل ذلك على امتناع التداخل وامتناع الخلاء معاً ( والجواب أنَّ شيئاً منها ) أي من العلامات المذكورة ) لا يفيد القطع بامتناع الخلاء ( لجواز أن يكون ما ذكرتم من الأمور الغريبة ( بسبب آخر مغاير لامتناع الخلاء لكنا ( لا نعرفه ) بخصوصه ( فهي) أي العلامات المذكورة ( أمارات) مفيدة للظن لا براهين مفيدة للقطع بالمطلوب.
We put a tube with a sealed head or a flat piece of wood (in a flask) such that part of the tube is inside the flask and part outside it, (and we sealed its head such that no air enters it nor exits from it) by sealing the gap between the neck of the flask and the tube with a seal through which air cannot penetrate. Then if we insert the tube into it more than it was, such that no air escapes from it, (the flask breaks) outward; and if we remove it from it, such that no air enters it, (it breaks) inward. Had it not been filled with air and what is in it of the tube such that it cannot bear anything else, (it would not be so)—i.e., it would not break upon insertion outward; and had it not been impossible for it to be empty of what occupies it and possesses it, it would not break upon removal inward. So that indicates the impossibility of interpenetration and the impossibility of void together. (And the answer: that none of these signs—i.e., of the mentioned signs—gives certainty of the impossibility of void, because it is possible that what you mentioned of strange occurrences (is due to another cause different from the impossibility of void, but we (do not know it) specifically. So they (the mentioned signs) are indicators (amārāt) giving conjecture (ẓann), not proofs giving certainty of the required conclusion.)
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents an empirical argument from al-Mawaqif against the possibility of void (khala') and interpenetration (tadakhul), followed by the commentator's rebuttal. The proof describes an experiment: a sealed tube inserted into a sealed flask; pushing it in breaks the flask outward, pulling it out breaks it inward. This is said to show that the flask cannot accommodate more or less without void or interpenetration. The commentator answers that the experiment only yields conjecture (ẓann), not certainty (qaṭ'), since the observed effects might be due to an unknown cause. The signs are merely indicators (amārāt), not demonstrative proofs.

التداخل (interpenetration)

Why the Experiment Does Not Prove Certainty
Al-Mawaqif responds by denying the experiment achieves certainty, classifying it as conjecture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the experiment gives only conjecture (ẓann), not certainty (qaṭʿ), and the distinction between amārāt (indicators) and barāhīn (demonstrative proofs).

Core Idea
Without excluding unknown causes, empirical signs remain indicators, not proofs.

After presenting the experiment, the commentator offers a crucial epistemological critique: the observed effects might have a different unknown cause. This highlights the difference between amārāt and barāhīn in kalam. Let's examine the refutation and its implications for theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the refutation. Notice the key terms: القطع (certainty), الظن (conjecture), الأمارات (indicators), البراهين (proofs).
The answer begins with 'والجواب' (and the answer).
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator states that none of the signs from the experiment yield certainty about the impossibility of void.
This is the central point.
3
Understand the reason
The reason given is that the observed strange occurrences could be due to another cause that we do not know.
This is a classic skeptical move – alternative explanations.
4
Learn the classification
Because of this possibility, the signs are classified as amārāt (indicators) giving ẓann (conjecture), not barāhīn (proofs) giving qaṭʿ (certainty).
This distinction is crucial in kalam epistemology.

The Epistemic Status of Multiple Indicators
Sanusi notes that multiple indicators may produce personal certainty but do not bind opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that multiple indicators may produce personal intuitive certainty but do not constitute a binding proof against opponents.

Core Idea
Multiple indicators can convince you, but they do not compel others.

Sanusi observes a crucial distinction: the power of cumulative evidence to produce personal certainty does not automatically make it a proof that binds an opponent. This passage clarifies the nature of argumentation in kalam and the limits of subjective conviction. Understanding this prevents confusion between personal confidence and universal proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with 'And know that...' signaling an important methodological point.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two claims: (1) multiple indicators may produce intuitive certainty in the soul, and (2) this certainty does not constitute a binding proof against an opponent. Identify these two parts.
The second claim is the main point.
3
Reflect on the implication for debate
Ask yourself: If you are personally convinced by many indicators, can you demand that others be convinced? According to Sanusi, no. This shows that personal certainty is not equivalent to an irrefutable proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( واعلم أنَّ الأمارات إذا كثرت واجتمعت ربما أقنعت النفس وأفادتها يقيناً حدسياً لا يقع به للخصم إلزام فهذه الأمارات لا تقوم حجة علينا، وإن أمكن أن تفيدهم جزماً يقينياً يكفيهم في ثبوت هذا المطلب عندهم )
The author said: 'And know that if indicators (amārāt) multiply and converge, they might convince the soul and give it an intuitive certainty (yaqīn ḥadsī) which does not constitute a binding argument against the opponent. So these indicators do not constitute a proof against us, although they may give them a decisive certainty sufficient for them in establishing this matter for themselves.'
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is a methodological remark from the author of al-Mawaqif. He acknowledges that multiple indicators (amārāt) can produce intuitive certainty (yaqīn ḥadsī) in the soul of the one who considers them. However, such certainty is not binding (ilzām) on an opponent. The indicators do not constitute a proof (ḥujja) against the opposing side. Nonetheless, they may give the proponents themselves decisive certainty (j azm yaqīnī) sufficient for their own conviction on the matter. This is a subtle epistemological point: internal certainty is not always externally probative.

Key Terms
الأمارات (indicators)
اليقين الحدسي (intuitive certainty)
الإلزام (binding)
الحجة (proof)
الجزم (certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple indicators always produce a proof binding on opponents.
Correction: The author says they produce intuitive certainty that is not binding on others.

Checkpoint

A) A proof binding on the opponent
B) An intuitive certainty that does not bind the opponent
C) A logical certainty that refutes the opponent
D) No effect on the soul

Key Takeaway
Multiple indicators can produce personal intuitive certainty but do not constitute a binding proof against opponents.

Three Branches of Views on the Void
The text enumerates three branches of views on the void: its definition, its emptiness, and its forces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main areas of debate about the void (khalāʾ): its definition, whether it can be empty, and what forces it may possess.

Core Idea
The void is not simple empty space; its nature is debated in three key ways.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents three branches (furūʿ) of opinions on the void (khalāʾ). Each branch addresses a different dimension: what the void is, whether it can be empty of bodies, and what forces it may possess. By studying these branches, you will see the richness of the kalām discussion on the void and how scholars differed on its reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice how the text is structured into three distinct branches.
The passage begins with '(Furūʿ)' indicating it is a set of sub-discussions.
2
Identify the three branches
The first branch deals with the definition of the void: is it an existing dimension or pure non-existence? The second branch asks whether the void can be empty of a body. The third branch discusses forces (attractive or repulsive) attributed to the void.
Each branch has its own proponents and counterviews.
3
Understand the majority view on forces
Pay special attention to the third branch: the majority (al-jumhūr) hold that the void has no force, and the author affirms that this is the truth.
This reflects the dominant Ashʿarī position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( فروع ) على القول بالخلاء (الأول من قال : بالخلاء منهم من جعله بعداً موجوداً (فإذا حل البعد الموجود عندهم ( في مادة فجسم (وإلا أي وإن لم يحل في مادة فخلاء) أي بعد موجود مجرد في نفسه عن المادة سواء كان مشغولاً ببعد جسمي يملؤه أو غير مشغول به فإنه في نفسه خلاء ) ومنهم من جعله عدماً صرفاً كما مر) من أنه حقيقة الخلاء عند القائلين: بأنَّ المكان بعد موهوم أن يكون الجسمان بحيث لا يتلاقيان ولا يكون بينهما ما يلاقيهما ( الثاني (منهم) أي من القائلين بالخلاء أعني بالبعد الموجود المجرد في نفسه عن المادة (من جوز أن لا يملأه جسم فيكون حينئذ خلاء بمعنى أنَّه بعد مجرد عن المادة وبمعنى أنه مكان خال عن الشاغل ( ومنهم من لم يجوزه) فيكون حينئذ خلاء بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني مذهب من قال بالسطح أن فيما بين أطراف الطاس على هذا المذهب بعداً موجوداً مجرداً في نفسه عن المادة قد انطبق عليه بعد الجسم فهناك بعدان، إلا أنَّ الأول لا يجوز خلوه عن انطباق الثاني عليه . وأما على القول : بأنَّ هو السطح فليس هناك إلا بعد الجسم الذي هو في داخل الطاس . ( الثالث قال ابن زكريا في الخلاء قوة جاذبة للأجسام، ولذلك يحتبس الماء في السراقات) وينجذب في الزراقات كما مر ( وقال بعضهم : فيه قوة دافعة ) للأجسام ( إلى فوق فإِنَّ التخلخل الواقع في الجسم بسبب كثرة الخلاء في داخله أعني أن يتفرق أجزاؤه ويداخلها خلاء ( يفيد ) ذلك الجسم ( خفة دافعة له إلى الفوق، والجمهور على أنه ليس في الخلاء قوة جاذبة ولا دافعة وهو الحق .
Branches (furūʿ) on the opinion of the void: First: Among those who affirm the void, some make it an existing dimension (buʿd mawjūd). So when that dimension (according to them) resides in matter, it is a body; otherwise, i.e., if it does not reside in matter, it is a void (khalāʾ)—an existing dimension abstracted in itself from matter, whether it is occupied by a bodily dimension that fills it or not occupied by it, for it is in itself a void. And some of them make it pure non-existence, as has passed, that it is the reality of the void according to those who say that place is an imaginary dimension such that two bodies do not meet and there is nothing between them that meets them. Second: Among them—i.e., among those who affirm the void, meaning the existing dimension abstracted in itself from matter—some allow that a body does not fill it, in which case it is a void both in the sense of being a dimension abstracted from matter and in the sense of being a place empty of an occupant; and some of them do not allow that, in which case it is a void only in the first sense, not the second. The doctrine of those who say the surface is that between the rim of the basin according to this doctrine there is an existing dimension abstracted in itself from matter upon which the dimension of the body has been superimposed. So there are two dimensions, except that the first cannot be empty of the superimposition of the second upon it. But according to the opinion that it is the surface, there is only the dimension of the body that is inside the basin. Third: Ibn Zakarīyā said that in the void there is an attractive force for bodies, and therefore water is retained in saraqāt and is attracted in zaraqāt as has passed. And some said that in it there is a repulsive force for bodies upward, for the rarefaction that occurs in the body due to the abundance of void inside it—meaning that its parts separate and void penetrates them—gives that body a lightness repelling it upward. The majority hold that there is in the void neither an attractive nor a repulsive force, and that is the truth.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three branches (furūʿ) of opinions regarding the void (khalāʾ). The first branch concerns the definition: some hold that the void is an existing dimension (buʿd mawjūd) that becomes a body when it resides in matter, and remains a void (an existing dimension abstracted from matter) when not in matter; others hold it is pure non-existence (ʿadam ṣirf). The second branch distinguishes views on whether the void can be empty of a body: some allow it to be empty (so it is a void in both the sense of abstract dimension and empty place), others deny it (so it is void only in the first sense). This branch also contrasts the surface theory (that place is a surface) with the void theory, explaining that according to the surface view, there are two dimensions in a basin (the void dimension and the body's dimension), but they always coincide. The third branch deals with forces attributed to the void: Ibn Zakarīyā claimed an attractive force, others a repulsive force (via rarefaction), but the majority hold that the void has no force, which is the correct view.

Key Terms
الخلاء (void)
البعد (dimension)
المادة (matter)
القوة الجاذبة (attractive force)
القوة الدافعة (repulsive force)
التخلخل (rarefaction)
العدم (non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The void is simply empty space with no further complexity.
Correction: Islamic theologians debated whether the void is an existing dimension or pure non-existence, whether it can be truly empty, and whether it has forces. The passage shows nuanced positions.

Checkpoint
According to the third branch, what is the position of the majority regarding forces in the void?
A) The void has an attractive force
B) The void has a repulsive force
C) The void has neither attractive nor repulsive force
D) The void has both forces

Key Takeaway
The void is a complex concept: its definition, capacity for emptiness, and possible forces are all disputed, with the majority denying any forces and affirming the void as an existing dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The void, according to the majority, is an existing dimension abstracted from matter—neither empty nor full on its own. Think of a space in your daily routine that feels 'empty' or wasted. Do you treat it as a void to escape, or as an existing dimension that you can fill with worship? What would it mean to see that gap as a created reality that belongs to Allah?
?
Some said the void has an attractive force pulling bodies toward it. In your spiritual life, what 'voids' do you find yourself drawn to—like scrolling through social media in idle time? The truth is the void has no force; the attraction is in your own nafs. How will you reclaim the void as neutral ground for obedience?

Definition of Quality and Its Three Restrictive Conditions
Defines quality and explains the three conditions of its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of quality (kayf) as an accident with three restrictive conditions and why it is considered an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ).

Core Idea
Quality is an accident that neither entails division nor non-division by its essence.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes the precise definition of quality (al-kayf), one of the ten Aristotelian categories. The author, al-Jurjani, explains that quality is an accident that does not essentially imply divisibility or indivisibility, and he provides three conditions to exclude other accidental categories. Understanding this definition is foundational for kalam discussions about divine attributes and the nature of created realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get an overall sense of the author's argument.
2
Understand rasm nāqiṣ
Notice that the definition is called an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ). Reflect on why highest genera like quality cannot be fully defined.
The text says quality is a highest genus, so it can only be described by clearer matters.
3
Identify the three restrictive conditions
Find the three conditions in the definition: (1) does not entail division, (2) does not entail non-division, (3) primary entailing. See what each condition excludes.
The first excludes quantity, the second excludes unity and point, the third excludes knowledge of one and two.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider why the examples (substance/accident, quantity/other categories) are invalid definitions, and how the conditions correctly exclude them.

المتن أعني قوله : والعلم المتعلق بالمعلومين فلا اقتضاء هاهنا لا بأصالة وهو ظاهر ولا بالتبع إذ لا اقتضاء في المعلومين للقسمة، وإن اتصفا بها بخلاف المعلوم البسيط، فإنه لبساطته يقتضي اللاقسمة والعلم مطابق له فكون مقتضياً لها بالتبع ولأجل ذلك جعل الإمام في المباحث المشرقية والكاتبي في شرح الملخص والشارح في حواشي شرح التجريد هذا القيد أعني اقتضاء أولياء متعلقاً بيقتضي اللاقسمة فقط وأما ما قيل أنه مبني على أنه إذا اعتبر قبول القسمة واللاقسمة في العرض فلا بد من جعله متعلقاً بالاقتضاء مطلقاً وإن اعتبر قبول القسمة واللاقسمة في محله على ما هو المنصوص في بعض العبارات فهو متعلق بالاقتضاء المقيد باللاقسمة لأن عدم انقسام الحال يقتضي عدم انقسام المحل في الحلول السرياني، فالعلم بالبسيط يقتضي عدم انقسام النفس بخلاف انقسام الحال، فإنه لا يقتضي انقسام المحل فإنّ العلوم المتعددة قائمة بالنفس مع عدم انقسامها، فليس بشيء أما أولاً فلانه على أن يكون قيد في محله متعلقاً بالقسمة واللاقسمة أي لا يقتضي انقسام المحل ولا انقسامه اقتضاء أولياء ولا يخفى فساده لأنه حينئذ لا يخرج النقطة بقيد اللاقسمة لأنها لا تقتضي عدم انقسام محلها أعني الخط بل عدم انقسام نفسها فهو ظرف مستقر حال من فاعل يقتضي أي لا يقتضي حال حصوله في محله وفائدته أن المعتبر عدم الاقتضاء بحسب الوجود الخارجي دون الذهني وإلا لم يخرج الكم لأن اقتضاءه القسمة ليس في الذهن، وإلا لم يمكن مبني عدم هذا مناقض لما ذكره في حواشيه على التجريد حيث صرح هناك بأن الأولية قيد لاقتضاء اللا قسمة وأنه لا حاجة إلى تقييد اقتضاء القسمة بذلك القيد والجواب التحقيقي أن القسمة واللاقسمة، إنما اعتبرت في التعريف المذكور في هذا الكتاب بالنسبة إلى نفس العرض، فمراده هاهنا هو أن العلم المتعلق بمعلومين يقتضي انقسام ذلك العلم المتعلق، وهو صحيح إذ لا يتعلق علم واحد شخصي بمعلومين لكن ذلك الاقتضاء لتعلقه بمعلومين لا لذاته لأن الكلام مبني على ان الحاصل في الذهن أشباح الماهيات؛ لا أنفسها كما أشار إليه في حواشي حكمة العين، وأما في التعريف الذي ذكره في حواشي التجريد، فإنما اعتبرنا بالنسبة إلى الموضوع ولا شك أنَّ العلم المتعلق بمعلومين لا يقتضي انقساماً في محله فإن النفس الواحد تدرك معلومات كثيرة مع أنه لا انقسام في تلك النفس أصلا بخلاف العلم بمعلوم بسيط فإنه يقتضي اللاقسمة في محله إذ لولاه لا نقسم ذلك العلم لأن انقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال إذا كان حلوله فيه بحسب ولكن لا لذاته بل لبساطة معلومه فلا مناقضة بين الكلامين.
The first objective concerning its definition and divisions. It has passed that it includes the primary. As for its definition, it is an accident that does not entail division and non-division with primary entailing (i.e., by essence and without intermediary), and its meaning is not understood by relation to another. This definition is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) for quality (al-kayf), and it is the ultimate among the highest genera. Indeed, due to their simplicity on the view that prevents their composition from equal matters, they are not defined at all nor described with a complete description. Their formal description is permissible by existential and non-existential matters, on condition that those matters are clearer than what is defined from the highest genera. Thus it is not valid to say, for example: 'Substance is what is not an accident', for substance and accident are equal in knowledge and ignorance, so it is not permissible to mention one in the definition of the other. Nor to say: 'Quantity is what is not quality nor where up to the other categories', because they are not clearer than quantity such that they are taken in its definition. So our saying 'accident' includes all accidents. And we guarded by our saying 'does not entail division' against quantity, for it entails division by essence. And by our saying 'does not entail non-division' against unity and point, which entail it, according to whoever says they are accidents, i.e., on the view that they exist externally. As for the view that they are [؟] mental considerations, there is no need for this restriction due to their not being included in accident, as was indicated. And by our saying 'primary entailing' against the exclusion of knowledge of one single thing—it is a true simple—and knowledge of two things. For the first knowledge entails non-division, but its entailing is not [primary]. His saying: 'about the exclusion' (he added the word exclusion because the restrictions in the scope of negation indicate comprehensiveness and inclusion). His saying: 'knowledge' and transient sounds. His saying: 'and knowledge of two things'—rather the qualities accidental to quantities, or to their substrate like blackness subsisting on a surface or body, and those that are their subjects like temporal sounds, all are excluded by this restriction. And in it there is no entailing here; rather it is only acceptance of division by way of following. As for the example of the text, namely his saying: 'and knowledge connected to two things', there is no entailing here, neither fundamentally, which is apparent, nor by way of following, since there is no entailing in the two things for division, even if they are qualified by it, unlike the simple known thing, for due to its simplicity it entails non-division, and knowledge corresponds to it, so it is an entailer of it by way of following. For that reason, the Imam in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, al-Kātibī in Sharḥ al-Mulakhkhaṣ, and the commentator in his glosses on Sharḥ al-Tajrīd made this restriction—namely 'primary entailing'—attached only to 'entails non-division'. As for what was said that it is based on: if the acceptance of division and non-division is considered in the accident, then it must be made attached to entailing absolutely; and if the acceptance of division and non-division is considered in its subject, as is stated in some expressions, then it is attached to entailing restricted by non-division, because non-division of the state entails non-division of the subject in solution by penetration; thus knowledge of the simple entails non-division of the soul, unlike division of the state, for it does not entail division of the subject, indeed multiple knowledges subsist in the soul with its non-division. So it is nothing. As for first, because it would be a restriction in its subject attached to division and non-division, i.e., it does not entail division of the subject nor its non-division with primary entailing, and its corruption is not hidden, because then the point is not excluded by the restriction of non-division, since it does not entail non-division of its subject, namely the line, but rather non-division of itself. So it is a circumstantial adverb of state from the agent of 'entails', i.e., it does not entail while occurring in its subject. Its benefit is that consideration is the absence of entailing according to external existence, not mental; otherwise quantity would not be excluded because its entailing of division is not in the mind, and otherwise it would not be possible. This contradicts what he mentioned in his glosses on al-Tajrīd where he stated there that primarity is a restriction for entailing non-division and that there is no need to restrict entailing of division by that restriction. The true answer is that division and non-division are considered in the mentioned definition in this book with respect to the accident itself. So his intent here is that knowledge connected to two things entails division of that connected knowledge, which is correct, since a single individual knowledge is not connected to two known things. But that entailing is due to its connection to two known things, not by essence, because the discussion is based on the fact that what is in the mind are forms of essences, not the essences themselves, as he indicated in his glosses on Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn. As for the definition he mentioned in his glosses on al-Tajrīd, we considered with respect to the subject. No doubt that knowledge connected to two things does not entail division in its subject, for the single soul perceives many known things while there is no division in that soul at all, unlike knowledge of a simple known thing, for it entails non-division in its subject, since otherwise that knowledge would divide because division of the subject necessitates division of the state if its indwelling in it is according to [essence], but not by essence, rather due to the simplicity of its known thing. So there is no contradiction between the two statements.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of quality (al-kayf) as an accident that does not entail division or non-division with primary entailing, and it explains the nuances of this definition. The author begins by stating that quality is an accident, meaning it is a category of accident that does not by its essence imply divisibility (like quantity) or indivisibility (like unity or point). The phrase 'primary entailing' (iqtiḍāʾ awwaliyyan) means that the entailment is by essence and without intermediary. This definition is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) because quality is a highest genus (jins ʿālī) and such genera, due to their simplicity, cannot be defined by a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm) nor even a complete description (rasm tāmm). However, they can be described formally by existential or non-existential matters, provided those matters are clearer than what is being defined. The author then gives examples of invalid definitions: defining substance as 'what is not an accident' fails because substance and accident are equally known; defining quantity as 'what is not quality nor where...' fails because those categories are not clearer than quantity. Next, the three restrictive parts of the definition are explained. The first, 'does not entail division' (lā yaqḍī al-qisma), excludes quantity, which entails division by essence. The second, 'does not entail non-division' (lā yaqḍī al-lā qisma), excludes unity and point, which entail non-division according to those who consider them accidents existing externally. If they are considered merely mental considerations, no restriction is needed. The third, 'primary entailing' (iqtiḍāʾ awwaliyyan), is discussed at length. It excludes knowledge of one simple thing (which entails non-division, but not primarily? The text says the first knowledge entails non-division but its entailing is not primary) and knowledge of two things (which entails division of that knowledge). The ensuing discourse in the commentary addresses whether 'primary entailing' should be attached only to 'entails non-division' or to both parts. The commentator resolves the apparent contradiction between different works by explaining that the definition in this book considers division and non-division with respect to the accident itself, whereas in another work (glosses on al-Tajrīd) it is considered with respect to the subject. Thus, knowledge of two things entails division of the knowledge itself (as it is multiple), but does not entail division in the subject (the soul). No contradiction exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition of quality is complete and includes everything.
Correction: It is actually a rasm nāqiṣ and the three conditions are necessary to exclude other categories like quantity, unity, and knowledge of multiple things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the condition 'primary entailing' (اقتضاء أولياً) exclude from the definition of quality?
A) Quantity (الكم)
B) Unity and point (الوحدة والنقطة)
C) Knowledge of two things only
D) Knowledge of one simple thing and knowledge of two things

Key Takeaway
The definition of quality is an accident that does not entail division or non-division with primary entailing, and it is an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) that requires three restrictive conditions to accurately differentiate it from other categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of quality excludes knowledge of two things because its entailing division is not primary. In your own intellectual life, are there convictions you hold that are based on secondary or borrowed evidence rather than primary, essential understanding? Will you examine the foundation of one such belief today?
?
The author insists that defining terms must be clearer than the defined. When you explain aspects of your faith to others, do you use terms that are equally or more confusing? Commit to simplifying one concept you often discuss so that it is truly clearer.
?
The precision of this definition shows how careful scholars were to avoid ambiguity. In your own relationship with Allah, are there areas where you settle for vague or imprecise understanding of His attributes? Will you seek out a precise definition of one attribute this week?

Logic and Technical Terms

Why 'Primarily' in the Definition of Knowledge
Defends the necessity of 'primarily' in knowledge's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'primarily' (أولياً) is a crucial qualifier in the definition of knowledge as a quality that entails non-division, and how it resolves an apparent contradiction between different formulations.

Core Idea
The word 'primarily' saves the definition from excluding simple and composite knowledge.

This lesson examines a subtle but critical point in the definition of knowledge: why must we say that knowledge 'primarily' entails non-division? Without this qualifier, the definition would incorrectly exclude certain types of knowledge that are still considered qualities. The passage defends the necessity of 'primarily' by addressing an objection based on different ways of considering division, and shows that the two formulations are consistent when the perspective is distinguished.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the repeated use of 'primarily' and the discussion of 'accident' vs. 'locus'.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding every detail on first read.
2
Identify the core problem
The passage explains that without 'primarily', both simple knowledge (which entails non-division) and knowledge of two known things (which entails division) would be excluded from the definition. Ask: why would they be excluded? The commentary will clarify.
Simple knowledge entails non-division, but not primarily (it entails it through its object). Knowledge of two known things entails division, but not primarily (it entails it through its relation to two objects).
3
Understand the objection and answer
The passage responds to a potential contradiction: in one formulation, 'primarily' is tied only to entailment of non-division; in another, it is tied to both divisions. The answer distinguishes considering division in the accident itself vs. its locus.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Summarize the investigative answer
The final answer: in this book, division is considered in the accident itself, so knowledge of two known things entails division of that knowledge. In the glosses on al-Tajrid, division is considered in the subject (soul), so it does not entail division. Both formulations are valid in their own contexts.
The qualification 'primarily' is necessary in both contexts to keep the definition correct.

حقيقي (و) العلم (بمعلومين) فإنَّ العلم الأول يقتضي اللاقسمة لكن ليس اقتضاؤه قوله : ( عن خروج الخ ) زاد لفظ الخروج لأن القيود في حيز النفي يفيد الشمول والدخول. قوله : ( العلم الخ) والأصوات الآنية . قائمة بالنفس مع عدم انقسامها، فليس بشيء أما أولاً فلانه على أن يكون قيد في محله متعلقاً بالقسمة واللاقسمة أي لا يقتضي انقسام المحل ولا انقسامه اقتضاء أولياء ولا يخفى فساده لأنه حينئذ لا يخرج النقطة بقيد اللاقسمة لأنها لا تقتضي عدم انقسام محلها أعني الخط بل عدم انقسام نفسها فهو ظرف مستقر حال من فاعل يقتضي أي لا يقتضي حال حصوله في محله وفائدته أن المعتبر عدم الاقتضاء بحسب الوجود الخارجي دون الذهني وإلا لم يخرج الكم لأن اقتضاءه القسمة ليس في الذهن، وإلا لم يمكن مبني عدم فهذا مناقض لما ذكره في حواشيه على التجريد حيث صرح هناك بأن الأولية قيد لاقتضاء اللا قسمة وأنه لا حاجة إلى تقييد اقتضاء القسمة بذلك القيد والجواب التحقيقي أن القسمة واللاقسمة، إنما اعتبرت في التعريف المذكور في هذا الكتاب بالنسبة إلى نفس العرض، فمراده هاهنا هو أن العلم المتعلق بمعلومين يقتضي انقسام ذلك العلم المتعلق، وهو صحيح ذكره في حواشي التجريد، فإنما اعتبرنا بالنسبة إلى الموضوع ولا شك أنَّ العلم المتعلق بمعلومين لا يقتضي انقساماً في محله فإن النفس الواحد تدرك معلومات كثيرة مع أنه لا انقسام في تلك النفس أصلا بخلاف العلم بمعلوم بسيط فإنه يقتضي اللاقسمة في محله إذ لولاه لا نقسم ذلك العلم لأن انقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال إذا كان حلوله فيه بحسب ولكن لا لذاته بل لبساطة معلومه فلا مناقضة بين الكلامين. أولياً، بل بواسطة معلومة والعلم الثاني يقتضي القسمة كذلك فلولا تقييد الاقتضاء بالأولية لخرجا عن الحد مع أنهما من مقولة الكيف
Real (and) knowledge of two known things: the first knowledge entails non-division but its entailment is not... His statement: (concerning the exclusion, etc.) He added the word 'exclusion' because restrictions in the scope of negation indicate inclusion and entrance. His statement: (knowledge, etc.) and instantaneous sounds. His statement: (and knowledge of two known things but rather the qualities accidental to quantities, or to their locus like blackness subsisting on a surface or body and the two things possessing them like temporal sounds are all excluded by this restriction. It contains that there is no entailment here but rather it is acceptance of division accidentally. As for the example of the text, namely his statement: and knowledge related to two known things, there is no entailment here neither essentially—which is apparent—nor accidentally, for there is no entailment of division in the two known things even if they are described by it, unlike the simple known thing, for due to its simplicity it entails non-division and knowledge corresponds to it, so it is an entailer of it accidentally. For that reason Imam [al-Razi] in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya and al-Katibi in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas and the commentator [al-Taftazani] in his glosses on Sharh al-Tajrid placed this restriction, namely 'primarily entail', relating to 'entails non-division' only. As for what is said that it is based on if acceptance of division and non-division is considered in the accident, then it must be related to entailment absolutely, and if acceptance of division and non-division is considered in its locus according to what is prescribed in some expressions, then it is related to entailment restricted to non-division because the non-division of the accident entails non-division of the locus in penetrating inherence, so knowledge of the simple entails non-division of the soul, unlike the division of the accident, for it does not entail division of the locus; indeed, multiple knowledges subsist in the soul without its division. So it is not valid; firstly because it would be a restriction in its locus related to division and non-division, i.e., it does not entail division of the locus nor its non-division primarily, and its invalidity is not hidden because then the point would not be excluded by the restriction of non-division, for it does not entail non-division of its locus, namely the line, rather its own non-division. So it is a circumstance settled as a state from the agent of 'entails', i.e., it does not entail in the state of its occurrence in its locus. Its benefit is that the considered thing is non-entailment according to external existence, not mental; otherwise quantity would not be excluded because its entailment of division is not in the mind, otherwise it would not be possible—built on non-existence. So this contradicts what he mentioned in his glosses on al-Tajrid where he stated there that 'primarily' is a restriction for the entailment of non-division and that there is no need to restrict the entailment of division with that restriction. The investigative answer is that division and non-division are considered in the definition mentioned in this book with respect to the accident itself, so his intent here is that knowledge related to two known things entails division of that related knowledge, and it is correct, for one individual knowledge cannot relate to two known things, but that entailment is due to its relation to two known things, not by itself, because the discourse is based on the fact that what obtains in the mind is the likenesses of essences, not the essences themselves, as he indicated in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn. As for the definition he mentioned in his glosses on al-Tajrid, we considered it with respect to the subject, and there is no doubt that knowledge related to two known things does not entail division in its locus; indeed, one soul perceives many pieces of information while there is no division in that soul at all, unlike knowledge of a simple known thing, for it entails non-division in its locus, because otherwise that knowledge would not be divided, since division of the locus necessitates division of the accident if its inherence is in it by way [of penetrating inherence], but not by itself rather due to the simplicity of its known object. So there is no contradiction between the two statements. Primarily, but through a known thing, and the second knowledge entails division similarly. Were it not for restricting entailment with 'primarily', they would both be excluded from the definition, whereas they are from the category of quality.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer defending the qualification 'primarily' (أوليا) in the definition of knowledge as a quality that entails non-division. The author begins by referencing the text: 'real knowledge of two known things' and explains that the first knowledge (simple) entails non-division, but not primarily. He then comments on the text's use of 'exclusion' and the types of knowledge excluded. The core argument is that without 'primarily', the definition would either include or exclude incorrectly. An objection is raised based on different formulations (considering division in the accident itself vs. in its locus). The answer distinguishes between considering the accident itself (where knowledge of two known things entails division of that knowledge) and considering its subject (where it does not entail division of the soul). Thus, the two formulations (this book vs. glosses on al-Tajrid) are not contradictory. The passage concludes that with 'primarily', both simple knowledge (entailing non-division) and composite knowledge (entailing division) remain within the category of quality.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the necessity of 'primarily' contradicts other formulations that tie 'primarily' only to non-division.
Correction: The two formulations are consistent because they consider division in different things: the accident itself (this book) versus its locus (other works). In each context, 'primarily' plays the same role of ensuring that the entailment is not immediate but through something else.

Key Takeaway
The qualification 'primarily' is necessary in the definition of knowledge to ensure that both simple and composite knowledge remain included, and it resolves an apparent contradiction between different classical formulations by distinguishing between division in the accident itself and in its locus.

The Third Condition for Qualities
Defines and defends the third condition for qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third condition for a quality (al-kayf): that its meaning must not be intelligible only in relation to another, and how this distinguishes qualities from relative accidents.

Core Idea
A quality is understood by itself; a relative accident is only understood in relation to something else.

The definition of quality (al-kayf) requires several conditions to exclude things that appear similar but are not true qualities. The third condition ensures that the meaning of a quality is not dependent on another thing for its intelligibility. This passage explains this condition, shows why it excludes relative accidents, and addresses a nuance about qualities that involve objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own formulation come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'ma'qul bil-qiyas ila al-ghayr' as the key term.
2
Identify the third condition and its target
Find the statement of the third condition: 'and its meaning should not be intelligible in relation to another'. Then see what it excludes: relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya). Ask why relative accidents violate this condition.
Relative accidents like fatherhood only make sense in relation to another entity.
3
Understand the alternative wording and the problem
Some scholars formulated the condition as 'its conception does not depend on the conception of another'. The passage points out that qualities like knowledge require their objects for conception. How does the author resolve this?
The resolution is the distinction between dependence (tawaqquf) and entailment (istilzam).
4
Follow the objection and response
The passage then anticipates an objection about composite vs. simple qualities and explains why the definition still holds. Work through the logic of the response.
The key point: composition is nullified by division, so composite qualities do not entail division; simple qualities like blackness are divisible by locus.

إلى غيرها لاقتضائها النسبة الموجبة لكونها معقولة بالقياس إلى ما ينسب إليه . قوله : ( لا تقتضي لذاتها النسبة ) وإن كانت عارضة لها . قوله على تصور غيره المراد بالغير الأمر الخارجي كما هو المتبادر فلا يلزم خروج الكيفيات المركبة . قوله : ( فإن الأعراض النسبية الخ هذا على تقدير كون النسبة ذاتياً لها ظاهر وأما على تقدير عروضها لها فلا لأن تصور المعروض لا يتوقف على تصور العارض ولأجل هذا عدل عنه إلى
