His statement: (and its meaning should not be intelligible in relation to another) i.e., we guarded by the final restriction, which is our saying: 'and its meaning should not be intelligible in relation to another' against relational [accidents] (al-nisab), i.e., relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya), for they are intelligible in relation to other than themselves. As for qualities (al-kayfiyyat), their meanings in themselves are not measured in relation to other than themselves, because you know that they do not entail relation by themselves. Some have mentioned in place of the final restriction his saying: 'and its conception should not depend on the conception of another', for relative accidents' conceptions depend on the conception of other things, unlike qualities, for they may entail the conception of other than themselves such as perception, knowledge, power, desire, anger, and their likes, for they are not conceived without their objects, I mean the perceived and the known, for example, but their conceptions are not dependent on the conceptions of the objects as effects in the way of relations; rather, their conceptions necessitate the conceptions of their objects. Its conception without the conception of division, and secondly, because in penetrating inherence, the locus and the accident are concomitant in division and its absence into parts that are distinct in position, so the claim of separation is an illusion. It cannot be said: qualities are either composite in existence, so they entail division, or simple, so they entail non-division, so the definition would not be true of any individual of the defined, for we say: composition is nullified by division, so it is not an entailer of it because the entailer co-exists with the entailed, and external simplicity requires that it have no external part, not that it is not divided. For blackness is simple externally yet divided according to the division of its locus. His statement: (intelligible in relation to other than itself because it entails relation which necessitates it being intelligible in relation to that to which it is related). His statement: (they do not entail relation by themselves) even if it is accidental to them. His statement: 'on the conception of another', the intended meaning of 'another' is the external thing as is the immediate understanding, so it does not necessitate the exclusion of composite qualities. His statement: (for relative accidents, etc.) this, on the assumption that relation is essential to them, is apparent; but on the assumption that it is accidental to them, it is not, because the conception of the subject does not depend on the conception of the accident. For this reason he turned from it to his saying: 'and its meaning should not be intelligible in relation to another'. His statement: (effect for them) indicates that the intended meaning is the negation of dependence that necessitates priority, not co-implication. His statement: (and similarly the case) i.e., in that they necessitate the conceptions of their objects, so they are not dependent on them. His statement: (rather their conceptions necessitate the conceptions of their objects) in that the state of relative accidents on the famous doctrine is that relation is necessary for them, not essential, and therefore it is said: their conception necessitates the conception of another and causes it, but dependence is denied. His statement: (and like the 'jadhriyya' and 'ka'biyya') he repeated the 'kaf' because they are from numerical accidents, not quantitative, and know.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the third condition in the definition of quality (al-kayf): that its meaning should not be intelligible only in relation to another (ma'qul bil-qiyas ila al-ghayr). The author begins by identifying this as the final restriction that excludes relative accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya), because relative accidents are only understood in relation to something else (e.g., fatherhood). Qualities, by contrast, have meanings that in themselves are not measured relative to another, because they do not entail relation by their essence. Some scholars formulate the same condition as 'its conception does not depend on the conception of another', but this is problematic because some qualities (like knowledge, power) require their objects for conception. However, the author clarifies that this dependence is not like that of relative accidents: for qualities, the conception of the object is entailed by the quality's conception, not a prerequisite. The passage then addresses an objection: if qualities are either composite (entailing division) or simple (entailing non-division), the definition might seem to exclude them all. The answer is that composite qualities are nullified by division, so they do not entail division, and simple qualities (like blackness) are divisible according to their locus without having external parts. Finally, the author discusses alternative wordings and notes that relative accidents' relation is necessary but not essential, and mentions two examples (jadhriyya and ka'biyya) as numerical accidents. The commentary emphasizes the nuanced distinction between dependence and co-implication, and why the third condition is preferred.

Correction: The passage clarifies that such qualities entail their objects rather than depending on them; they remain true qualities.

The First Method of Proving the Divisions: Exclusive Division and Its Critique
Presents and critiques the first method of proving the four divisions by exclusive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that attempts to prove the four categories of quality through exclusive division and why it ultimately relies on induction rather than pure logic.

Core Idea
Exclusive division alone cannot prove the categories; it only hides induction.

This lesson examines the first method presented in the text for proving the four divisions of quality: the method of exclusive division (الترديد) between negation and affirmation. The argument seems to derive the categories logically, but a careful critique shows that it fails to exclude all alternatives, leaving induction as the real basis. We will follow the argument step by step and see why the critique matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and trace the division tree
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the series of binary divisions: quality is either specific to quantity or not; if not, either sensible or not; if not sensible, either disposition or perfection. The last category is claimed to be psychic qualities.
Notice how each division claims to exhaust possibilities.
2
Understand the critique
The author asks: why must the 'perfection' category be limited to ensouled beings? Could it not apply to bodies that are not sensible, not dispositional, and not specific to quantity? The division does not exclude that possibility.
The critique reveals a gap in the logical division.
3
See the conclusion: reliance on induction
The text states that since we have not found such bodily qualities, the division ultimately rests on induction (الاستقراء). The method of exclusive division was an unnecessary effort.
This shows the limits of pure deduction in theological categories.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
This passage illustrates a key principle in kalam: logical divisions alone cannot establish categories; empirical observation (induction) often underlies them. The critique saves the student from overrelying on formal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنهم من أراد إثباته بالترديد بين النفي والإثبات فذكر وجوهاً) أربعة . (الأول) وهو أجودها ( أنه ( أي الكيف (إما أن يختص بالكم أو لا يختص به (وهذا) الذي لا يختص بالكم (إما محسوس) بإحدى الحواس الظاهرة ) أو لا وهذا الذي ليس محسوساً بها، إما استعداد نحو الكمال أو كمال وهذا الأخير هو الكيفيات النفسانية . قلنا : ولم قلتم أن الكمال ) الخارج من القسمة (هو الكيفية النفسانية ولم يثبت ذلك الكمال لغير ذوات الأنفس) ؟ فإن ما لا يختص بالكم ولا يكون ،محسوساً، ولا يكون حقيقته استعداداً جاز أن يكون كيفية غير مختصة بذوات الأنفس من الأجسام غايته ( إنا لم نجده فالمآل هو الاستقراء فلنعول عليه (أولاً) حذفاً لمؤونة الترديد .
(And among them is one who wanted to prove it by dividing between negation and affirmation, and he mentioned four ways. The first—and it is the best of them—is that it (quality) is either specific to quantity or not specific to it. And that which is not specific to quantity is either sensible by one of the external senses or not; and that which is not sensible by them is either a disposition toward perfection or perfection itself, and this latter is the psychic qualities. We said: And why do you say that the perfection emerging from the division is the psychic quality, and that perfection has not been established for other than ensouled beings? For that which is not specific to quantity, nor sensible, nor whose reality is a disposition, could be a quality not specific to ensouled beings from bodies; its utmost is that we have not found it, so the outcome is induction, so let us rely upon it, firstly, removing the burden of division.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the first method of proving the four divisions of quality by exclusive division (الترديد). The argument goes: quality is either specific to quantity or not; if not specific to quantity, it is either sensible by external senses or not; if not sensible, it is either a disposition toward perfection or perfection itself; the latter is what is meant by 'psychic qualities'. The author then criticizes this division: why must the 'perfection' that emerges be exclusively psychic? It could be a quality of bodies that is not specific to quantity, not sensible, and not dispositional—it is simply not observed. Therefore, the division ultimately fails to prove the enumeration deductively; instead, the enumeration is still based on induction (الاستقراء). The lesson concludes that induction is the reliable method, and the division was unnecessary effort.

Correction: The critique shows that the division does not exclude all possibilities; the categories are ultimately established by induction, not pure logic.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to prove the four divisions by exclusive division fails because it cannot exclude all alternatives, leaving induction as the real foundation.

The Third Division of Qualities and Its Logical Extension
Third way of dividing qualities with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third way of dividing qualities (kayf) into categories, including the objection about sensible qualities and its resolution.

Core Idea
Not all active qualities are sensible—some are purely acts without disposition.

This passage presents a detailed classification of qualities (kayfiyyat) as found in Avicenna's al-Shifa. It builds on earlier divisions by considering whether a quality relates to the soul, to quantity, and then to disposition or act. The author then raises a critical objection and provides an answer that refines the division. Pay attention to the branching logic and the defense of the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Trace the branching classification
Follow the division: first, quality either pertains to the soul or not. Then, if not pertaining to the soul, it either pertains to quantity or not. Finally, that which does not pertain to quantity is either disposition or act.
This is a hierarchical tree, not a flat list.
3
Identify the objection
The objector asks: why did you claim that the last category (act) is the sensible qualities? The assumption is that act-qualities are necessarily sensible.
4
Understand the answer
The answer points out that a quality whose essence is pure act (without disposition) could be non-sensible. Thus, the identification is not forced, and the classification remains valid.
This refines the division without breaking it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثالث) من وجوه الحصر وهو أيضاً مذكور في الشفاء ( أنْ يقال : الكيف) إما أن يتعلق بوجود النفس، وذلك بأن يكون للنفوس أو للأجسام من حيث إنها ذوات النفوس ( أولاً ) يتعلق بوجود النفس ( والثاني إما أن يتعلق بالكمية أو لا يتعلق بها. ( والثاني إما استعداد أو فعل . قلنا : ولم قلت : إنَّ الأخير) أعني الفعل هو الكيفيات ( المحسوسة ) ؟ لجواز أن يكون كيفية هويتها الفعل دون الاستعداد ولا تكون محسوسة
(The third) of the ways of enumeration, and it is also mentioned in al-Shifa: that it is said: quality (al-kayf) either pertains to the existence of the soul, and that is by being for souls or for bodies insofar as they possess souls, or it does not pertain to the existence of the soul. The second either pertains to quantity or does not pertain to it. And the second is either disposition or act. We said: Why did you say that the last, i.e., the act, is the sensible qualities? Because it is possible that a quality whose essence is act (not disposition) is not sensible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the third way of enumerating the categories of quality (kayfiyyat), as found in Avicenna's al-Shifa. The division begins by considering whether the quality pertains to the existence of the soul (tawajjuh bi-wujud al-nafs). If it does, it belongs either to souls themselves or to bodies insofar as they have souls. If it does not pertain to the soul, then either it pertains to quantity (kammiyya) or not. That which does not pertain to quantity is further divided into disposition (isti'dad) and act (fi'l). The author then raises an objection: why does the objector claim that the last category (act) is restricted to sensible qualities? The answer is that it is possible for a quality whose nature is pure act, without disposition, to be non-sensible. This shows that the division does not force all act-qualities to be sensible.

Key Terms
كيف
كيفية
وجود النفس
الكمية
استعداد
فعل
محسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All act-qualities (fi'l) are sensible.
Correction: The passage shows that a quality whose essence is pure act can be non-sensible, so not all act-qualities are sensible.

Checkpoint
According to the third way of dividing qualities, what is the division of those qualities that do not pertain to the soul?
A) They are either sensible or non-sensible.
B) They either pertain to quantity or not, and if not, are either disposition or act.
C) They are either active by resemblance or not.
D) They are either bodily or spiritual.

Key Takeaway
The third division of qualities shows a logical tree that separates soul-related, quantity-related, and then disposition vs. act, and clarifies that act-qualities are not necessarily sensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you tend to value only what you can see and feel in your spiritual life? What non-sensible quality—like sincerity or trust in Allah—is an 'act' you perform daily? How can you make that act more conscious and intentional, knowing it is not sensed by others but is real and weighty before Allah?
?
Consider a disposition (isti'dad) you have—a potential good habit or skill you have not yet turned into an act (fi'l). What is holding you back? How can you move from having the capacity to actually doing it, starting today?
?
The passage defends that an act can be non-sensible. In your relationship with Allah, what 'unseen act' do you need to cultivate more (e.g., dua, gratitude, repentance)? Are you comfortable with acts that have no worldly appearance? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of reality?

The Fourth Division of Qualities and Its Critique
Presents the fourth division of qualities and its critique regarding number qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth division of qualities and why it is criticized for excluding number qualities of immaterial beings.

Core Idea
The fourth division fails to account for number qualities like evenness in immaterial beings.

The author presents a fourth way of dividing qualities, based on whether they act by resemblance, pertain to bodies or souls, and then by quantity or nature. However, this division is criticized because it omits qualities that attach to numbers in immaterial entities, such as evenness. This lesson unpacks that division and its main flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the division
Read the Arabic passage and translation that lays out the fourth division of qualities. Note the branches: acting by resemblance or not, relating to bodies or souls, etc.
2
Identify the critique
Find where the author points out that the division 'loses the quality specific to numbers occurring in immaterial beings' like evenness. Understand why this is a problem.
The critique is that such qualities are not bodily, so they don't fit the division.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this critique matters: if the division cannot cover all qualities, it is incomplete. This highlights the challenge of classifying qualities comprehensively.
This connects to the broader debate on the categories of qualities in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع) من تلك الوجوه وقد ذكره في الشفاء أيضاً لكنه زيفه بما ستعرفه أن يقال: الكيف (إما أن يفعل بالتشبيه ) كما مر ( أو لا . والثاني إما أن لا يتعلق بالأجسام) بل بالنفوس ( أو يتعلق بالأجسام . ( والثاني إما من حيث الكمية أو (الطبيعة) . أي يتعلق بالأجسام إما من حيث كميتها أو من حيث طبيعتها والقسم الأخير هو الاستعداد نحو الفعل أو ما الانفعال. ولا يخفي ما فيه وهو مر في الوجه الثاني من أنه لم يثبت أن المحسوسة كلها فاعلة بالتشبيه إلى آخره . ( مع أنه ( مزيف بما ذكر في الشفاء من أنه ( يضيع الكيفية المختصة بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات فإن هذه الكيفية كالزوجية مثلا . غير مندرجة في التقسيم لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام. قوله: (يضيع الكيفية الخ) في الشفاء : فإن لم يدخل تلك الكيفيات في هذه المقولة وكانت الكيفيات ما يعرض للجواهر الجسمانية فيجب أن ينقسم على نحو ما قلنا . قوله : ( بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات) قيل عليه : إِذا ثبت عروض العدد للمجردات لم يكن علم الحساب الباحث عن أحوال العدد من الرياضيات لتصريحهم بأن البحث فيها عن أحوال ما يستغني عن المادة في الذهن لا في الخارج. أجيب : بأن الحساب ليس ينظر فيه في العدد مطلقاً بل من حيث لا يوجد إلا في العدد المقارن للمادة كما يدل عليه تتبع مباحثه . قوله : ( لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام فإن قلت : هذا مناف لما سبق من تخصيص الشارح في أول المرصد للكيفيات بالماديات . قلت : قد نبهناك في أوائل مباحث الكم أن المراد عدم عروضها للمجردات أولاً . وبالذات ويمكن أن يقال : في دفع الاعتراض بضياع الكيفية المذكورة أن المراد إما أن لا تتعلق بالأجسام بدون النفس أصلاً، أو تتعلق بها في الجملة، وإن لم تختص به وكيفيات العدد كذلك فلا تضيع.
(The fourth) of those ways, and it is also mentioned in al-Shifa but he refuted it as you will know: that it is said: quality either acts by resemblance (at-tashbih) as before, or not. The second either does not pertain to bodies but to souls, or pertains to bodies. The second either in terms of quantity or nature. That is, it pertains to bodies either in terms of their quantity or in terms of their nature, and the last division is disposition towards action or passion. And it is not hidden what is in it, and it has passed in the second way that it has not been established that all sensible qualities act by resemblance, etc. Moreover, it is refuted by what is mentioned in al-Shifa that it loses the quality specific to numbers that occur in immaterial beings, for this quality, like evenness for example, is not included in the division because it does not occur in bodies. His saying: (It loses the quality...) in al-Shifa: For if those qualities are not included in this category, and qualities are what occur to bodily substances, then it must be divided as we said. His saying: (By numbers occurring to immaterial beings) It was objected: If occurrence of numbers to immaterial beings is established, then the science of arithmetic which investigates the states of number would not be a mathematical science, since they state that its investigation is about states of what is independent of matter in the mind, not in the external. It was answered: Arithmetic does not consider number absolutely, but only insofar as it does not exist except in number associated with matter, as tracking its investigations shows. His saying: (Because they do not occur in bodies) If you say: This contradicts what the commentator previously specified at the beginning of the observatory that qualities are restricted to material things. I say: We have alerted you at the beginning of the discussions of quantity that the intention is that they do not occur in immaterial beings primarily and essentially. And it can be said in repelling the objection of losing the mentioned quality: The intention is either that they do not pertain to bodies without the soul at all, or they pertain to them in some respect, even if not specifically, and likewise qualities of number, so they are not lost.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth way of dividing qualities, also from al-Shifa, which is then refuted. The division is: quality either acts by resemblance (bil-tashbih) or not. The second category either does not pertain to bodies but to souls, or pertains to bodies. The latter is further divided by quantity or by nature. The nature division is disposition towards action or passion. The author notes that this division is problematic because it has not been established that all sensible qualities act by resemblance (as in the second way). Moreover, a major critique from al-Shifa is that this division loses those qualities that attach to numbers in immaterial (mujarradat) beings, such as evenness (zawjiyya). Since such qualities are not bodily, they are not included in this division, which restricts qualities to bodies. The commentary then addresses a counter-argument: if numbers attach to immaterial beings, arithmetic would not be a mathematical science since mathematics abstracts from matter. The answer is that arithmetic only considers number as it exists in association with matter, not absolutely. Another objection suggests that the division contradicts the commentator's earlier restriction of qualities to material things. The response is that the restriction was primary and essential, not absolute; qualities can occur in immaterial beings secondarily. The passage concludes with a possible alternative: perhaps the division intends qualities to either not pertain to bodies without souls at all, or to pertain to them in some way; and number qualities like evenness can be seen as pertaining to bodies in some way, so they are not lost.

فاعلة بالتشبيه
محسوسة
استعداد
انفعال
كيفية العدد
مجردات
زوجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all qualities are either actions or dispositions pertaining to bodies, and that number qualities are not real qualities.
Correction: This passage shows that number qualities like evenness are genuine qualities that occur in immaterial beings, and a complete classification must include them.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the fourth division of qualities is criticized?
A) It does not include qualities related to souls.
B) It loses qualities that attach to numbers in immaterial beings.
C) It fails to distinguish between quantity and nature.
D) It incorrectly assumes all sensible qualities act by resemblance.

Key Takeaway
The fourth division of qualities is insufficient because it omits number qualities that apply to immaterial beings, showing the difficulty of a comprehensive classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you categorize aspects of your faith—like the attributes of Allah or the nature of your soul—do you ever assume your categories are complete? Where might you be missing something because your categories are too narrow, just as the fourth division missed number qualities?
?
The critique here shows that even scholars can overlook entire categories. In your own learning and practice of Islam, are you open to the possibility that your understanding might miss something essential? What would it take to remain humble before the complexity of truth?
?
Number qualities like evenness exist independently of bodies. In your spiritual life, are there realities—like the mercy of Allah or the weight of your deeds—that you treat as if they only apply to physical matters, ignoring their deeper, abstract significance? How might you begin to take them more seriously?

Defending the Critique: Objections and Responses
Responds to objections against the critique, defending the loss of number qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the critique of the fourth division and how they are answered, including the nature of arithmetic and the scope of qualities.

Core Idea
Objections to the critique are met by showing arithmetic deals with number in matter, and the division can accommodate number qualities secondarily.

The critique that the fourth division loses number qualities of immaterial beings invites objections. Does that make arithmetic not a mathematical science? Does it contradict earlier statements? This lesson follows the debate and its resolution, showing how number qualities can be considered as pertaining to bodies in some respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation that present the objection: if numbers occur in immaterial beings, arithmetic would not be a mathematical science because mathematics abstracts from matter.
2
Examine the response
Note the answer: arithmetic only considers number as it exists in association with matter, not absolutely. This preserves its status as a mathematical science.
3
Consider the second objection
Another objection: the division seems to contradict earlier statements that qualities are restricted to material things. The response clarifies that the restriction is primary and essential, not absolute.
4
Understand the final alternative
A possible resolution: perhaps the division intends qualities to either not pertain to bodies without souls at all, or to pertain to them in some way; number qualities like evenness can be seen as pertaining to bodies in some respect, so they are not lost.
This shows a way to salvage the division by broadening the interpretation.

The Objection of Missing Numerical Qualities
First objection about numerical qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Isfahani's defense of the classification of qualities against the objection that it omits qualities of numbers that occur to immaterial entities.

Core Idea
Every quality must be accounted for in the classification, even numerical ones.

In this passage, al-Isfahani addresses an objection raised from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa: the division of qualities seems to miss the qualities specific to numbers, such as evenness, which occur to immaterial entities. This objection challenges the comprehensiveness of the classification. We will see how the response refutes this by clarifying the scope of the category.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and context
Notice that the objection claims that qualities of numbers are not included because they do not occur to bodies.
2
Understand the response from al-Shifa
The response suggests that if such qualities are not included, then the division must be adjusted.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection threatens the classification and how the response maintains consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في تعريفه وأقسامه المحسوسة كلها فاعلة بالتشبيه إلى آخره . ( مع أنه ( مزيف بما ذكر في الشفاء من أنه ( يضيع الكيفية المختصة بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات فإن هذه الكيفية كالزوجية مثلا . غير مندرجة في التقسيم لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام. قوله: (يضيع الكيفية الخ) في الشفاء : فإن لم يدخل تلك الكيفيات في هذه المقولة وكانت الكيفيات ما يعرض للجواهر الجسمانية فيجب أن ينقسم على نحو ما قلنا . قوله : ( بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات) قيل عليه : إِذا ثبت عروض العدد للمجردات لم يكن علم الحساب الباحث عن أحوال العدد من الرياضيات لتصريحهم بأن البحث فيها عن أحوال ما يستغني عن المادة في الذهن لا في الخارج. أجيب : بأن الحساب ليس ينظر فيه في العدد مطلقاً بل من حيث لا يوجد إلا في العدد المقارن للمادة كما يدل عليه تتبع مباحثه . قوله : ( لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام فإن قلت : هذا مناف لما سبق من تخصيص الشارح في أول المرصد للكيفيات بالماديات . قلت : قد نبهناك في أوائل مباحث الكم أن المراد عدم عروضها للمجردات أولاً . وبالذات ويمكن أن يقال : في دفع الاعتراض بضياع الكيفية المذكورة أن المراد إما أن لا تتعلق بالأجسام بدون النفس أصلاً، أو تتعلق بها في الجملة، وإن لم تختص به وكيفيات العدد كذلك فلا تضيع .
The Third Observatory - First Aim: On its definition and divisions. All sensible [qualities] are active by way of similification, etc. (While it is refuted by what is mentioned in al-Shifa: that it misses the quality specific to numbers that occur to immaterial entities; for this quality, like evenness, for example, is not included in the division because it does not occur to bodies.) His statement: ('he misses the quality etc.') in al-Shifa: 'For if those qualities are not included in this category, and qualities are what occur to bodily substances, then it must be divided according to what we said.' His statement: ('with numbers occurring to immaterial entities') an objection was raised against him: 'If the occurrence of number to immaterial entities is established, then the science of arithmetic, which investigates the states of number, would not be among the mathematical sciences, because they explicitly state that their investigation is about the states of what dispenses with matter in the mind, not in the external world.' The answer: 'Arithmetic does not consider number absolutely, but rather only insofar as it does not exist except in number that is concomitant with matter, as shown by examining its topics.' His statement: ('because they do not occur to bodies') 'If you say: This contradicts what the commentator earlier in the beginning of the Observatory specified, that qualities pertain to material things. I say: We have alerted you at the beginning of the discussions of quantity that what is meant is, first and primarily, that they do not occur to immaterial entities. And it can be said in repelling the objection about the loss of the mentioned quality: What is meant is either that they do not attach to bodies without a soul at all, or that they attach to them in some way, even if not exclusively. So numerical qualities are likewise and are not lost.'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section defending the classification of qualities. It begins by quoting an objection from al-Shifa that a certain quality (e.g., evenness) is lost in the division because it pertains to numbers, which are immaterial. The response explains that if such qualities are not included, the division would need to be adjusted. A second objection concerns the science of arithmetic: if numbers occur to immaterial entities, then arithmetic would not be a mathematical science (since mathematics deals with what dispenses with matter in the mind). The answer clarifies that arithmetic studies number only insofar as it exists in matter. A third objection raises inconsistency with an earlier statement that qualities are limited to material things. The reply distinguishes between primary occurrence and secondary attachment. Finally, it is suggested that numerical qualities are not lost because they either require a soul or attach to bodies in some way.

The Objection from Arithmetic and Mathematical Sciences
Second objection about arithmetic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if numbers occur to immaterial entities, then arithmetic would not be a mathematical science, and the response clarifying the scope of arithmetic.

Core Idea
Arithmetic studies number only as it exists in matter.

A further objection arises: if numbers occur to immaterial entities, then the science of arithmetic, which studies number, would not be a mathematical science because mathematics deals with things that dispense with matter in the mind. The response clarifies that arithmetic considers number only insofar as it coexists with matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Note the claim that if numbers occur to immaterial entities, arithmetic would not be a mathematical science.
2
Understand the answer
The answer distinguishes between number absolutely and number as it exists in matter.
3
Connect to wider theme
This response preserves arithmetic as a mathematical science while acknowledging the reality of numbers in immaterial entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في تعريفه وأقسامه المحسوسة كلها فاعلة بالتشبيه إلى آخره . ( مع أنه ( مزيف بما ذكر في الشفاء من أنه ( يضيع الكيفية المختصة بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات فإن هذه الكيفية كالزوجية مثلا . غير مندرجة في التقسيم لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام. قوله: (يضيع الكيفية الخ) في الشفاء : فإن لم يدخل تلك الكيفيات في هذه المقولة وكانت الكيفيات ما يعرض للجواهر الجسمانية فيجب أن ينقسم على نحو ما قلنا . قوله : ( بالأعداد العارضة للمجردات) قيل عليه : إِذا ثبت عروض العدد للمجردات لم يكن علم الحساب الباحث عن أحوال العدد من الرياضيات لتصريحهم بأن البحث فيها عن أحوال ما يستغني عن المادة في الذهن لا في الخارج. أجيب : بأن الحساب ليس ينظر فيه في العدد مطلقاً بل من حيث لا يوجد إلا في العدد المقارن للمادة كما يدل عليه تتبع مباحثه . قوله : ( لأنها غير عارضة للأجسام فإن قلت : هذا مناف لما سبق من تخصيص الشارح في أول المرصد للكيفيات بالماديات . قلت : قد نبهناك في أوائل مباحث الكم أن المراد عدم عروضها للمجردات أولاً . وبالذات ويمكن أن يقال : في دفع الاعتراض بضياع الكيفية المذكورة أن المراد إما أن لا تتعلق بالأجسام بدون النفس أصلاً، أو تتعلق بها في الجملة، وإن لم تختص به وكيفيات العدد كذلك فلا تضيع .
The Third Observatory - First Aim: On its definition and divisions. All sensible [qualities] are active by way of similification, etc. (While it is refuted by what is mentioned in al-Shifa: that it misses the quality specific to numbers that occur to immaterial entities; for this quality, like evenness, for example, is not included in the division because it does not occur to bodies.) His statement: ('he misses the quality etc.') in al-Shifa: 'For if those qualities are not included in this category, and qualities are what occur to bodily substances, then it must be divided according to what we said.' His statement: ('with numbers occurring to immaterial entities') an objection was raised against him: 'If the occurrence of number to immaterial entities is established, then the science of arithmetic, which investigates the states of number, would not be among the mathematical sciences, because they explicitly state that their investigation is about the states of what dispenses with matter in the mind, not in the external world.' And it can be said in repelling the objection about the loss of the mentioned quality: What is meant is either that they do not attach to bodies without a soul at all, or that they attach to them in some way, even if not exclusively. So numerical qualities are likewise and are not lost.'

The Objection on the Materiality of Qualities
Third objection about materiality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Isfahani resolves the apparent contradiction between the classification's focus on material qualities and the inclusion of numerical qualities.

Core Idea
Numerical qualities are not lost because they relate to bodies in some way.

The final objection points out an inconsistency: earlier it was said that qualities pertain to material things, but numerical qualities seem immaterial. The response distinguishes between primary and secondary occurrence, and suggests that numerical qualities either require a soul or attach to bodies in some manner, thus preserving the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Notice the objection claims inconsistency; the response offers two possible understandings.
2
Analyze the distinction
The response distinguishes between primary occurrence (to immaterial entities) and secondary attachment (to bodies).
3
Appreciate the consistency
This shows that numerical qualities are not lost; they can attach to bodies in some way, preserving the classification's focus on material qualities.

Proximity in Quantity Indicates Proximity in Power
Explains why approximation in quantity indicates approximation in power due to elemental simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proximity in quantity indicates proximity in power, and why the text uses 'proximate' instead of 'equal' due to the absence of true equilibrium.

Core Idea
Proximate, not equal: similarity in power is inferred from closeness in quantity, not exact equality.

This passage explains a key principle: when two things are close in quantity, it points to a similar level of power because the underlying elements are simple. The text deliberately avoids saying 'equal' because true equilibrium never exists in reality. The commentator also clarifies two related points: rotational motion as a consequence of equal forces, and the use of 'permissible' despite the outcome being necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the three distinct parts: the main explanation, the gloss on rotational motion, and the gloss on 'permissible'.
2
Understand why 'proximate' is used instead of 'equal'
The commentator explains that 'proximate' is used because a true equilibrium (al-muʿtadil al-ḥaqīqī) does not exist. Ask yourself: why is true equilibrium impossible in the elements?
The simplicity of the elements means powers are similar, but not exactly equal.
3
Examine the examples: rotational motion and permissibility
The gloss on 'rotational motion' illustrates how equal forces lead to rotation, as seen in gold particles in a crucible. The gloss on 'permissible' clarifies the author's choice of terms, noting that 'permissible' suffices for the argument even though the actual outcome is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( متقاربة في (الكمية التقارب في الكمية دليل التقارب في القوة لكون القوى متشابهة في العناصر لبساطتها وإنما لم يقل متساوية لانتفاء المعتدل الحقيقي سواء قلنا: بامتناعه أو لا . قوله (حركة (دوران) فإنّ كل واحد منها لا يقوى على جذب الآخر على الاستقامة لتعادلهما في القوة، فيجذبه على الدوران ويصعده كما يشاهد في البوتقة ارتفاع أجزاء الذهب في وسطها . قوله : (جائز) أي ليس بممتنع واقتصر على الجواز مع كونه واجباً لكفايته فيما هو المطلوب.
His statement: (Proximate in quantity) – proximity in quantity is evidence of proximity in power because the powers are similar in the elements due to their simplicity. And he only did not say 'equal' because of the absence of true equilibrium, whether we affirm its impossibility or not. His statement: (Rotational motion) – for each of them is not strong enough to attract the other straight on due to their equality in power, so it attracts it in rotation and raises it, as is observed in the crucible of the rising of gold particles in its center. His statement: (Permissible) – i.e., not impossible, and he restricted to permissibility despite it being necessary, because it suffices for what is required.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the base text's phrase 'proximate in quantity.' The commentator explains that approximation in quantity serves as evidence for approximation in power (i.e., the strength of the effect). This is because the powers (quwwā) in the elements are similar, the elements being simple (basīṭa). The commentator notes that the base text deliberately avoided saying 'equal' because a true equilibrium of forces never exists, regardless of whether one considers its complete impossibility. The passage then includes two short additional glosses: one on 'rotational motion' (ḥaraka dawrān), explaining that when two forces are equal, neither can attract the other in a straight line, resulting in rotation as seen in gold particles rising in the center of a crucible. The second gloss addresses the term 'permissible' (jāʔiz), clarifying that it means 'not impossible' and that the base text used 'permissible' even though the actual outcome is necessary, to suffice for the argument at hand.

Key Terms
التقارب
الكمية
القوة
العناصر
البساطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'proximate' and 'equal' are interchangeable and the text could have said 'equal' without loss of meaning.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that 'equal' was avoided because true equilibrium (al-muʿtadil al-ḥaqīqī) does not exist; 'proximate' is used to indicate closeness without implying exact equality.

Checkpoint
Why did the base text use 'proximate' (متقاربة) instead of 'equal' (متساوية) for the quantity?
A) Because true equal quantity is impossible in elements.
B) Because the author made a mistake.
C) Because 'proximate' is a weaker term used for rhetorical effect.
D) Because the elements are not simple.

Key Takeaway
Proximity in quantity serves as evidence for proximity in power because the underlying elements are simple and powers are similar; true equilibrium is impossible, so 'proximate' is used over 'equal'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you measure your spiritual 'quantity' (e.g., prayer, knowledge) against that of someone you admire, do you assume your inner power is correspondingly close? How does the simplicity of your own soul (its elemental nature) affect that relation? Will you examine one area today and accept proximity without envy?
?
The commentator insists on 'proximate' over 'equal' because true equilibrium never exists. In your life, where do you demand perfect balance (in relationships, in character) that is impossible, causing you to stagnate? What would change if you embraced proximity and moved forward?
?
The rotational motion example shows that equal forces cannot pull directly but must go around. In a strained relationship, are you trying to pull the other person in a straight line? What would it look like to 'rotate' — to attract indirectly, trusting that the equality is still real? Will you try that this week?

Why 'Jā'iz' Suffices: Logical Precision in Causal Delay
Defends the use of 'permissible' and explains proximity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator used 'jā'iz' (permissible) instead of 'wājib' (necessary) for the delay of heat's effect and how the concept of proximity addresses remaining cases.

Core Idea
'Jā'iz' means 'not impossible' – a minimal claim that is logically sufficient.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a subtle point of terminology. The base text used the term 'jā'iz' to describe the delay of heat's effect, but one might ask: isn't that delay actually necessary given an obstacle? The commentator explains why 'jā'iz' is sufficient and then addresses a remaining case involving the dominance of fine particles, invoking the principle of proximity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the commentator first defines 'jā'iz' and then addresses a subtle case.
2
Understand the logic of 'jā'iz'
The commentator says 'jā'iz' here means 'not impossible'. Even though the delay is actually necessary, saying it is 'jā'iz' suffices for the argument. Ask yourself: why does 'not impossible' suffice?
The term 'jā'iz' is a logical category, not a judgment of free choice.
3
Examine the proximity argument
The commentator considers the case where fine particles dominate but not extremely. He says this might fall under 'proximity' (taqārub). Proximity in quantity (similar matter) indicates proximity in quality (similar power of heat). This completes the defense.
This shows how the base text's mention of 'greatly' covers most cases, and the rest follows by analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (جائز) أي ليس بممتنع واقتصر على الجواز مع كونه واجباً لكفايته فيما هو المطلوب . قوله : ( وإن غلب اللطيف جداً) بقي أن يكون اللطيف غالباً لا جداً فلعله داخل في التقارب . والكثيفة متقاربة في القوة بعد تأثير الحرارة فيها فكان التقارب في الكمية ينبئ عن التقارب في الكيفية فاكتفى به .
His statement: (Permissible) – i.e., not impossible, and he restricted to permissibility despite it being necessary, because it suffices for what is required. His statement: (And if the fine overcomes greatly) – it remains that the fine is dominant but not greatly, so perhaps it falls under proximity. And the dense things are proximate in power after the effect of heat in them, so proximity in quantity indicates proximity in quality, so he sufficed with it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two points from the base text. First, it explains the meaning of 'permissible' (jā'iz) used in the earlier text: the commentator states that 'permissible' here simply means 'not impossible' (laysa bi-mumtaniʿ). Although the actual non-occurrence of the effect due to an obstacle is necessary (wājib), the base text restricted itself to saying 'permissible' because that level of proof suffices for the argument. Second, it addresses the phrase 'if the fine overcomes greatly' (wa in ghalaba al-laṭīf jiddan). The commentator notes that there remains the case where the fine is dominant but not extremely so; this case might be included under 'proximity' (al-taqārub). He then explains that the dense things become proximate in power after heat affects them, so the proximity in quantity (i.e., similarity in the amount of matter) indicates proximity in quality (i.e., in the power of heat effects), and thus the base text sufficed with mentioning proximity.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممتنع
الواجب
التقارب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'jā'iz' here means 'optional' in the sense of free choice.
Correction: In this context, 'jā'iz' simply means 'not impossible' as a logical category, not 'optional'.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use the term 'jā'iz' (permissible) instead of 'wājib' (necessary) for the delay of heat's effect?
A) Because the delay is actually both permissible and necessary.
B) Because the term 'jā'iz' suffices for the argument and does not require proving necessity.
C) Because the delay is impossible, not necessary.
D) Because the author made an error in terminology.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defends the base text's use of 'jā'iz' by showing it is logically sufficient, and uses proximity to handle the remaining case of moderate dominance of fine particles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your plans face obstacles and results are delayed, do you insist on seeing necessity (wājib) in your efforts, or are you content with Allah's permission (jā'iz) that the outcome is possible? The commentator shows 'jā'iz' suffices—does your trust in Allah's decree suffice when outcomes are not guaranteed?
?
You often seek proximity to Allah through outward acts—but have you considered the 'heat' needed to transform quantity into quality? The passage links proximity in quantity to quality only after heat affects the dense; what 'heat' (sincerity, struggle) might be missing in your worship to make it truly proximate to Allah?
?
Do you treat the logical categories of 'possible' and 'necessary' as abstract ideas, or do they shape how you view your own choices? When you sin, do you assume it was impossible to avoid (because of qadr) or permissible (jā'iz) and thus your responsibility? How does this lesson's precision challenge your self-accountability?

Clarifying Domination Cases in Heat Interaction
Commentary on the logical scope of a negation in heat interaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator logically expands the negation in 'the dense overcomes' to include multiple cases of domination and equality.

Core Idea
A single negation can logically encompass several distinct scenarios.

This passage examines a key phrase from the base text. The commentator unpacks the apparent negation to show that it logically includes several distinct cases of interaction between fine and dense substances under heat. Understanding this analysis helps grasp the comprehensive nature of the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how the commentator unpacks the phrase 'bal yaghlib al-kathīf'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the scenarios covered
List the scenarios that the commentator claims are included under the negation: fine overcoming in general, equality, dense overcoming greatly, dense overcoming in general.
Some are explicitly mentioned, others implied.
3
Reflect on completeness
Consider why some cases are under 'proximity' or unknown. This shows the commentator's thoroughness in covering all logical possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل يغلب الكثيف (الخ ظاهر النفي المتوجه إلى غلبة اللطيف على الكثيف جداً يشمل غلبته في الجملة ويشمل أيضاً صورة التساوي وغلبة الكثيف جداً أو في الجملة فبعض هذه الصور مذكور بحكمه صريحاً، وبعضها إما مندرج في التقارب أو غير معلوم التحقق .
His statement: (But the dense overcomes, etc.) – the apparent negation directed to the fine overcoming the dense greatly includes its overcoming in general and also includes the case of equality and the overcoming of the dense greatly or in general. So some of these cases are mentioned explicitly with their ruling, and some are either included under proximity or are not known to be realized.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the base text's phrase 'bal yaghlib al-kathīf' (but the dense overcomes). The commentator analyzes the logical scope of the negation. He explains that the negation of the fine overcoming the dense greatly actually covers multiple scenarios: it includes the case where the fine overcomes to some degree (but not greatly), the case of equality between fine and dense, and the case where the dense overcomes either greatly or to some degree. Some of these cases are explicitly mentioned with their respective rulings in the base text, while others are either included under the category of 'proximity' (taqārub) or are not known to actually occur. Thus, the base text's classification is comprehensive: we have either mutual proximity, or clear domination of one over the other in varying degrees.

B) Only equality and dense overcoming slightly.
C) The fine overcoming in general, equality, and dense overcoming greatly or in general.
D) Only the dense overcoming greatly.

Key Takeaway
A negation of one extreme logically covers multiple intermediate and opposite possibilities.

Objection and Answer: How Theriac's Heat Joins Innate Heat
Objection and answer about theriac's heat becoming innate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Kalam argument that foreign heat can become innate if it has natural suitability, using the example of theriac and poison.

Core Idea
Theriac's heat integrates with innate heat because of its medicinal suitability, not by mere contact.

This passage presents an objection to the claim that only parts of a compound repel foreign elements. The objector uses theriac, a well-known antidote, to challenge this. The answer reveals a key principle: suitability (munāsaba) determines integration. Understanding this clarifies how substances like theriac can strengthen innate heat.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: the objector's challenge followed by the reply.
The objection uses the example of theriac drunk against poison.
2
Identify the core objection
Find why theriac is a problem: its heat is foreign and temporally later than the poison's heat, yet it repels the poison. Why doesn't the poison's heat become innate first?
The key phrase: 'If this amount of contact were sufficient, then the poison's heat would be more deserving.'
3
Follow the answer's reasoning
See how the answer appeals to 'suitability' (munāsaba): theriac's heat, due to its medicinal composition, has a stronger affinity to innate heat, making integration easier and faster.
The analogy with food digestion helps clarify: some foods like meat are digested faster.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Reflect on how this argument guards a key distinction: not every foreign element becomes part of the innate; only those with natural compatibility do.
This preserves the rule that repelling is done by parts that are essentially part of the compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: 'Rather, in the fact that innate is included in that compound' he meant that it is like a part in being inseparable, not that it is a part in reality, for there is no doubt that it is accidental to the compound. And here is a discussion: it is that the context of his statement indicates that the repeller of the foreign part repels it only because it is a part of the compound. Do you not see his statement: 'even if we suppose the foreign were included' etc. So the objection arises from theriac drunk against poisons, where it repels by its heat the heat of the poison, even though the heat of the theriac has not yet become a part of the innate? How? It is temporally later in joining the innate than the heat of the poison. If this amount of contact with the innate were sufficient for it to become a part of the innate, then the heat of the poisoned person would be more deserving of becoming part of it. It can be answered that the heat of theriac, because of the medicines it contains, is more suitable (more appropriate) than the innate heat, so its attachment to it and its becoming part of it is easier and faster, just as some foods like meat are digested faster and attach to nature than many other foods. Then when the heat of theriac becomes a part of the innate, the innate becomes stronger and does its act of repelling.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objection arises from the example of theriac (a medicine) taken against poisons: theriac's heat repels the poison's heat, but theriac's heat is foreign. The critic argues that if mere contact with innate heat made something part of it, then the poison's heat (which contacts innate heat earlier) would become innate first. The answer explains that theriac's heat has a stronger natural affinity (munāsaba) to innate heat than the poison's heat, due to its medicinal composition, so it integrates more quickly and easily. Once integrated, it strengthens innate heat to repel. This preserves the distinction: not all foreign heat becomes innate, only those with proper suitability.

Key Terms

Correction: Only foreign heat with natural suitability (like theriac's) can integrate; mere contact is insufficient (poison's heat does not integrate).

D) Because theriac is a solid while poison is liquid.

Key Takeaway
The integration of foreign heat into innate heat depends on natural suitability, not mere contact.

Motion Generates Heat: The Objection from Celestial Spheres
Argument that motion generates heat, objection from spheres, and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion generates heat only in receptive matter and how this refutes the objection from celestial spheres.

Core Idea
Motion requires a receptive subject to produce heat.

This passage presents the fourth proof that motion generates heat, supported by empirical observation. The objection from Abu al-Barakat challenges this by pointing to the celestial spheres: if motion always causes heat, the rapidly moving spheres should be extremely hot. The answer refines the principle, showing that heat requires not only motion but also a receptive material. This lesson explores how the scholar defends the causal principle by distinguishing necessary conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument, objection, and answer.
2
Identify the objection
Notice the hypothetical consequence: if motion always heats, then the spheres would heat the elements, turning them into fire.
This objection is attributed to Abu al-Barakat.
3
Examine the two-part answer
First, the matter of the spheres is not receptive to heat. Second, the elements are not moved subsidiarily due to smooth surfaces.
Both parts are necessary to block the objection.
4
Reflect on the principle
Why does heat require both a cause (motion) and a receptive subject? How does this preserve the empirical claim that motion generates heat?
Think about everyday examples where motion produces heat only in certain materials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها أن الحركة تحدث الحرارة والتجربة تحققه. وقد أنكره أبو البركات وإليه الإشارة بقوله: قيل إذا كانت الحركة تحدث الحرارة فيجب أن تسخن الأفلاك سخونة شديدة جداً بواسطة حركاتها السريعة ويتسخن بمجاورتها العناصر الثلاثة التي هي في وسط الأثير والأفلاك بمنزلة القطرة في البحر المحيط فيصير هذه الثلاثة كلها بالتدريج ناراً لاستيلاء سخونة الأفلاك عليها مع مساعدة كرة الأثير إياها في تسخينها والجواب أنَّ مواد الأفلاك لا تقبل السخونة أصلاً ولا بد في وجود الحرارة من المقتضي الذي هو الحركة ومن وجود القابل وحينئذ فلا تسخن الأفلاك بسبب حركتها فلا تسخن العناصر بالمجاورة وليست العناصر متحركة على سبيل التبعية فإنها لملاسة سطوحها لا تتحرك بحركة الأفلاك فتسخن. بالنصب على قوله: وليست العناصر ولو سلم كونها متحركة بالتبعية فالحركة التبعية لا تحدث الحرارة والمراد بالعناصر كلها فيندفع مناقضته لما سيأتي. قوله: فإنها لملاسة سطوحها لا تتحرك إلخ يعني أن سطوحها ملساء فلا يلزم من تحرك بعضها كالنار بتبعية فلك القمر لعلاقة بينهما أن يتحرك جميعها. أنها لم تصر بعد جزءاً من الغريزية كيف؟ وإنها متأخرة زماناً في لحوقها بالغريزية عن حرارة السموم فلو كان هذا القدر الذي حصل لها من الملاقاة مع الغريزية كافياً في صيرورتها جزءاً من الغريزية لكان حرارة المسموم أولى بأن تصير جزءاً منها ويمكن أن يجاب بأن حرارة الترياق بما فيه من الأدوية أشد مناسبة من الحرارة الغريزية فيكون التحاقها بها وصيرورتها جزءاً منها أسهل وأسرع، كما أن بعض الأغذية كاللحم أسرع هضماً والتحاقاً بالطبيعة من كثير من الأغذية ثم إذا صارت حرارة الترياق جزءاً من الغريزية تقوت بها الغريزية وفعلت فعلها في الدفع.
Fourth: that motion generates heat, and experience confirms it. Abu al-Barakat denied it, and the reference to him is in his saying: 'It is said: if motion generates heat, then the celestial spheres must become intensely hot due to their rapid motions, and through their proximity the three elements that are in the middle of the ether and the spheres—which are like a drop in the surrounding ocean—would become hot, and gradually all these three would become fire due to the domination of the spheres' heat over them, with the help of the sphere of the ether in heating them.' The answer: the materials of the spheres do not accept heat at all, and for the existence of heat, both the efficient cause (motion) and a receptive subject are necessary; therefore, the spheres do not become hot due to their motion, nor do the elements become hot through proximity. And the elements are not moved in a subsidiary manner, because their surfaces are smooth, so they are not moved by the motion of the spheres and thus become hot. (End of main argument)
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument that motion generates heat, supported by experience. The objection from Abu al-Barakat is then quoted: if motion generates heat, then the rapidly moving celestial spheres would become extremely hot and would heat the three elements (earth, water, air) by proximity, eventually turning them into fire. The answer refutes this by stating that the matter of the spheres (their material) is not receptive to heat. Heat requires both a generating cause (motion) and a recipient that can accept heat. Since celestial matter cannot accept heat, motion does not produce heat there. Additionally, the elements are not heated by proximity because they are not moved by the spheres (due to smooth surfaces), and even if they were moved subsidiarily, such motion does not generate heat. The gloss explains that smooth surfaces prevent transfer of motion. This defends the principle that motion generates heat only in suitable matter.

الحركة (al-ḥaraka)
الحرارة (al-ḥarāra)
القابل (al-qābil)
المقتضي (al-muqtaḍī)
التبعية (al-tabaʿiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion always generates heat regardless of the material.
Correction: Heat requires both motion (cause) and a receptive subject (material capable of being heated).

Checkpoint
Why do the celestial spheres not become hot despite their rapid motion?
A) Because their motion is not fast enough.
B) Because their matter does not accept heat.
C) Because they are too far from the elements.
D) Because their motion is circular, not linear.

Key Takeaway
Motion generates heat only when the moving object's matter is receptive to heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take to make your heart a receptive subject (قابل) for Allah's grace?
?
The objection seemed logical: if motion causes heat, then fast-moving objects must be hot. But the answer revealed a missing condition: receptive matter. Where in your life have you assumed that because you are doing the right 'motions' (rituals, actions) you will automatically get the expected result, without checking whether your soul is receptive? What is the 'قابل' condition you have been neglecting?
?
The elements are not moved by the spheres because their surfaces are smooth; they are not dragged along. Are there spiritual 'smooth surfaces' in your heart—complacency, pride, hardness—that prevent you from being moved by divine motions? What would it take to roughen that surface so you can be carried by the spheres of divine guidance?

Defining Moisture and Defending the Definition
Author defines moisture and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of moisture as a quality that facilitates ease of accepting and abandoning shapes, and how it is defended against objections involving honey, oil, and air.

Core Idea
Moisture is ease, not stickiness or endurance.

In this passage, the author defines moisture as a quality that brings about ease in accepting shapes and leaving them. He anchors this definition in the observable properties of water and then defends it against objections that might arise from comparing water to honey or oil. He also refutes an alternative definition by Ibn Sina. As you read, pay attention to how the author distinguishes between intensity of adhesion and ease of adhesion—a crucial distinction that saves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage once
Read the Arabic text without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from definition to objections.
The Arabic is dense; take time.
2
Understand the definition
The author states moisture is a quality (كيفية) that necessitates ease in accepting and leaving shapes. Note the two attributes of water that illustrate this.
Water has two aspects: ease of adhesion/separation and ease of shape change.
3
Follow the honey objection and response
The objection claims honey should be moister if moisture is about adhesion. The author clarifies: moisture is about ease, not intensity or endurance.
Honey adheres more firmly but not more easily.
4
Analyze the rebuttal of Ibn Sina
Ibn Sina defined moisture as ease of shaping alone. The author shows that would make air moister than water, which is absurd.
Air is more fluid but not moist.
5
Review the agreed principle
The author ends with the majority agreement that mixing moisture with dry gives cohesion, reinforcing the definition.
This ties back to the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي سهولة أي الرطوبة كيفية تقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال و سهولة تركها وذلك لأن الماء له وصفان أحدهما ما يقتضي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال والثاني ما يقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها ولا شبهة في أن الماء يوصف بأنه رطب باعتبار أحد هذين الوصفين فإذا بطل الأول تعين الثاني قلنا هو أي العسل أدوم التصاقا وأشد التصاقا من الماء لا أسهل التصاقا منه ونحن لم نفسر الرطوبة بنفس الالتصاق حتى يلزم أن يكون ما هو أشد وأقوى في الالتصاق أرطب ولا بدوام الالتصاق حتى يكون الأدوم أكثر رطوبة بل بسهولة الالتصاق فاللازم منه أن يكون الأسهل التصاقا أرطب وليس العسل أو الدهن أسهل التصاقا من الماء بل بالعكس وأيضا قد اعتبر في الرطوبة الانفصال وليس العسل أشد انفصالا من الماء فلا يلزم كونه أرطب ويرد ذلك الاعتراض أيضا في تفسيرها بسهولة قبول الأشكال لأنه إذا كان تشكل الجسم بالأشكال الغريبة لأجل رطوبته لزم أن يكون ما هو أدوم شكلا أرطب وليس كذلك إذ شكلا أيبس فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا وأيضا فسهولة الانفصال معتبرة في حقيقتها والعسل وإن فرضنا أنه سهل اتصاله حتى زاد في سهولة الاتصال على الماء لكن يعسر انفصاله فعلى تقدير كون العسل أسهل التصاقا من الماء لا يلزم أيضا كونه أرطب إذ ليس أسهل انفصالا منه ثم نقول يبطل تفسير أي تفسير ابن سينا للرطوبة بسهولة التشكل وتركه أنه يوجب أن يكون الهواء رطبا بل أن يكون أرطب من الماء لأنه أرق قواما منه وأقبل للتشكلات الغريبة وتركها بسهولة واتفقوا أي الجمهور على أن خلط الرطب باليابس يفيد اليابس استمساكا عن
So it is facility: i.e., moisture is a quality that necessitates facility in accepting shapes and facility in leaving them. And that is because water has two attributes: one that necessitates facility of adhesion and separation, and the second that necessitates facility of accepting shapes and leaving them. There is no doubt that water is described as moist by virtue of one of these two attributes. So if the first is invalidated, the second is established. We say: honey is more enduring in adhesion and more intense in adhesion than water, not easier in adhesion. We did not define moisture by adhesion itself such that what is more intense and stronger in adhesion would be moister, nor by endurance of adhesion such that what is more enduring would be more moist; rather by ease of adhesion. So the necessary consequence is that what is easier in adhesion is moister, and honey or oil are not easier in adhesion than water; on the contrary, the opposite is true. Also, separation is considered in moisture, and honey is not more intensely separating than water, so it does not follow that it is moister. That objection also applies to the interpretation of moisture as ease of accepting shapes, because if the body's shaping by extraordinary shapes occurs due to its moisture, it would follow that what is more enduring in shape is moister, which is not the case, for that shape is drier. So your answer is our answer. Also, ease of separation is considered in its essence. Even if we suppose that honey is easy in adhesion to the extent that it exceeds water in ease of adhesion, yet it is difficult in separation. So on the assumption that honey is easier in adhesion than water, it still does not follow that it is moister, since it is not easier in separation. Then we say: the interpretation of Ibn Sina of moisture as ease of shaping and its abandonment is invalidated, because it would require that air is moist, indeed that it is moister than water, because it is more delicate in consistency and more receptive to extraordinary shapes and their abandonment with ease. And they (the majority) agreed that mixing the moist with the dry benefits the dry cohesion.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained argument defending the definition of moisture as a quality that facilitates ease of accepting shapes and leaving them (سهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها). The author begins by stating the definition: moisture is a quality (كيفية) that necessitates facility in both accepting and abandoning shapes. He then grounds this definition by analyzing water, which is clearly moist, and notes that water has two relevant attributes: one that relates to ease of adhesion and separation, and another that relates to ease of accepting shapes and leaving them. Since water is called moist by one of these, and the first attribute (ease of adhesion and separation) might be thought primary, the author anticipates an objection involving honey and oil, which are more adhesive but not obviously easier in adhesion. The objection goes: if honey and oil are more sticky than water, they should be moister, but this is false; thus the definition fails. The author's response is careful: he did not define moisture by adhesion itself (نفس الالتصاق) or by endurance of adhesion (دوام الالتصاق), but rather by ease of adhesion (سهولة الالتصاق). Honey and oil are more intensely adhesive (أشد التصاقا) and more enduring in adhesion (أدوم التصاقا), but they are not easier in adhesion. In fact, they adhere more firmly and thus are less easy to separate. The author also reminds that moisture involves ease of separation (سهولة الانفصال), and honey is not easier to separate than water. Hence the objection does not refute the definition. Then the author extends the same reasoning to the interpretation of moisture as ease of accepting shapes, because if moisture were defined only by that, then the objection would apply in a parallel way: if a body takes on extraordinary shapes due to moisture, then a substance that remains shaped longer would be moister, but that is not the case. So the response to that objection is the same. Finally, the author refutes Ibn Sina's definition of moisture as ease of shaping (سهولة التشكل) by pointing out that air, being more fluid and receptive to shapes, would then be moister than water, which is false. He also cites an agreement among scholars that mixing the moist (i.e., water) with the dry (e.g., earth) gives the dry cohesion (استمساكا), further supporting the proper definition.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
سهولة الالتصاق
سهولة الانفصال
سهولة قبول الأشكال
الالتصاق
الانفصال
التشكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moisture is defined by intensity or endurance of adhesion, so stickier substances like honey are moister.
Correction: Moisture is defined by ease of adhesion and separation, not by intensity or endurance. Honey is more adhesive but not easier to adhere or separate.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author makes in defining moisture when responding to the honey objection?
A) Moisture is defined by the intensity of adhesion, not ease of adhesion.
B) Moisture is defined by ease of adhesion and separation, not by intensity or endurance of adhesion.
C) Moisture is defined solely by endurance of adhesion, not by ease.
D) Moisture is defined by the ability to accept shapes, not by any property of adhesion.

Key Takeaway
Moisture is defined by ease of accepting and leaving shapes, not by intensity or endurance of adhesion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that moisture is not about how strongly something sticks, but about how easily it can change shape. What in your life is 'sticky'—not because it is easy, but because you hold it tightly? How might redefining it as 'ease of leaving' change your struggle?
?
Ibn Sina defined moisture only by ease of shaping, but the author insists on including ease of leaving. In your spiritual practices, do you focus only on 'shaping' yourself (adopting new habits) without considering how easily you abandon them when tested? What does this lesson reveal about your need for both accepting and letting go?

5 min
Distinction Between Wetness and Fluidity
Al-Iji defines fluidity and distinguishes it from wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fluidity (al-sayalān) is defined as the pushing of parts (tadāfuʿ al-ajzāʾ) and is distinct from wetness, as dry substances like sand can flow.

Core Idea
Fluidity is the pushing of parts, and it does not require wetness.

In this passage, al-Iji clarifies a key distinction in natural philosophy: wetness and fluidity are not the same. He provides a precise definition of fluidity—the pushing of parts—and shows that even dry sand can flow. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications for understanding the nature of water and other flowing substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Iji states his claim and then defends it with examples.
The passage begins with a statement that wetness is different from fluidity.
2
Identify the definition of fluidity
Find the phrase 'tadāfuʿ al-ajzāʾ' (pushing of parts) in the Arabic. Understand that this is the core definition. Note that it applies whether parts are actually separate or continuous.
The definition is given right after 'fa innahu ʿibāratun ʿan tadāfuʿ al-ajzāʾ'.
3
Study the examples and implications
See how al-Iji uses flowing sand (dry) and flowing water (wet) to show that fluidity can exist without wetness. Then consider the consequence from al-Mulakhkhas that water, according to philosophers, would not be fluid because it is one continuous whole.
This highlights the technical nature of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وثانيها ) أي ثاني المباحث ( أنَّ الرطوبة مغايرة للسيلان فإنه عبارة عن تدافع الأجزاء سواء كانت متفاصلة في الحقيقة متواصلة في الحس أو كانت متواصلة في الحقيقة أيضاً. وقد يوجد السيلان بهذا التفسير فيما ليس يرطب كالرمل السيال مع كونه يابساً بالطبع ويوجد أيضاً فيما هو رطب كالماء السائل وفي الملخص أن السيلان عبارة عن حركات توجد في أجسام متفاصلة في الحقيقة متواصلة في الحس يدفع بعضها بعضاً، وعلى هذا التفسير يلزم أن لا يوجد السيلان في الماء على رأي الحكماء لأنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة
The second discussion: that wetness is different from fluidity, for it (fluidity) is an expression of the pushing of parts, whether they are actually separate [in reality] while continuous in sense, or also continuous in reality. And fluidity may exist, by this interpretation, in something that is not wet, like flowing sand, despite being naturally dry, and also exists in something wet like flowing water. And in al-Mulakhkhas, fluidity is an expression of motions existing in bodies that are actually separate [in reality] while continuous in sense, some of them pushing others. According to this interpretation, it follows that fluidity does not exist in water according to the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), because it is one continuous whole in reality.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines fluidity (al-sayalān) and distinguishes it from wetness (al-rutūba). The definition of fluidity is the pushing (tadāfuʿ) of parts, regardless of whether the parts are actually separate or continuous. This definition allows that dry substances like sand can flow, while water, being continuous in reality according to the philosophers, would not truly be fluid. The passage thus establishes that fluidity is not a necessary property of wetness; they are distinct qualities.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
السيلان
تدافع الأجزاء
متفاصلة في الحقيقة
متواصلة في الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fluidity is the same as wetness or necessarily implies wetness.
Correction: Fluidity is defined specifically as the pushing of parts, and can exist in dry substances like sand, proving it is independent of wetness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is fluidity (al-sayalān)?
A) The ease of accepting shapes.
B) The pushing of parts, whether actually separate or continuous.
C) The same as wetness.
D) The thinness of consistency.

Key Takeaway
Fluidity is defined as the pushing of parts and is not dependent on wetness; dry sand can flow, and even water may not be considered fluid under certain philosophical definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson emphasizes the importance of precise definitions in theology. Think of a term related to your faith that you use loosely without a clear definition. How might a lack of precision in that term lead to confusion or error in your understanding? Commit to looking up its correct definition from a reliable source this week.
?
Al-Iji distinguishes between fluidity and wetness, showing that one can exist without the other. In your spiritual life, are there actions or practices that appear 'wet' (spiritually meaningful) but are actually 'dry' (lacking true substance)? Reflect on one such area and ask Allah to give you sincerity and depth.
