Fluidity as Mutual Repulsion of Parts
Al-Jurjani defines fluidity and distinguishes it from wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam defines fluidity as the mutual repulsion of parts and why it is conceptually distinct from wetness.

Core Idea
Fluidity is about repulsion, not wetness; dry sand can flow.

This passage tackles a common confusion in natural philosophy: equating fluidity with wetness. Al-Jurjani, following earlier mutakallimūn, defines fluidity precisely as the mutual repulsion of parts, setting the stage for a deeper discussion of motion, continuity, and the nature of elements. The discussion draws on both atomist and philosophical positions, making it a rich exercise in comparative ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of fluidity
Start with the core definition: fluidity is the mutual repulsion (tadāfuʿ) of parts. Notice that the definition does not mention wetness at all.
The Arabic term 'sīlān' is derived from 'sāla' (to flow), but here it is formally defined.
2
Examine the two interpretations of 'parts'
The definition covers two metaphysical views: parts that are actually separate (atomist) and parts that are continuous (philosopher). Each leads to different consequences.
Al-Jurjani quotes al-Mulakhkhaṣ for the atomist view.
3
Consider the counterexample of sand
Flowing sand is dry but exhibits fluidity. This shows that fluidity does not require wetness, confirming the conceptual separation.
4
Analyze the objection about water
On the philosopher's view, water is a single continuum, so it should not be fluid by this definition. This is a key problem discussed in the commentary.
Think about how continuous bodies can still have internal repulsion.
5
Reflect on the stone-thrown-upwards puzzle
The passage ends with a subtle point: if fluidity requires mutual repulsion of parts, does a falling stone qualify? The answer touches on the cause of motion.
This shows the precision required in kalam definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون النار رطباً فضلاً عن كونها أرطب العناصر . ( وثانيها ) أي ثاني المباحث ( أنَّ الرطوبة مغايرة للسيلان فإنه عبارة عن تدافع (الأجزاء سواء كانت متفاصلة في الحقيقة متواصلة في الحس أو كانت متواصلة في الحقيقة أيضاً. وقد يوجد) السيلان بهذا التفسير ( فيما ليس يرطب كالرمل السيال) مع كونه يابساً بالطبع ويوجد أيضاً فيما هو رطب كالماء السائل وفي الملخص أن السيلان عبارة عن حركات توجد في أجسام متفاصلة في الحقيقة متواصلة في الحس يدفع بعضها بعضاً، وعلى هذا التفسير يلزم أن لا يوجد السيلان في الماء على رأي الحكماء لأنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة. قوله : ( فلا يلزم كون النار رطباً لا النار الصرفة ولا النار التي عندنا إذ ليس في طبيعتها سهولة قبول التشكل وأن فرض حصولها في نار عندنا بواسطة مخالطة الهواء وإنما قلنا : وإن فرض لأن المشاهدة تدل على تشكلها بشكل ما توقد فيها وأما سهولة التشكل فغير معلوم فإنه بمجرد الإيقاد يحصل شكل صنوبري فإذا بولغ وملئ ما توقد فيه بالوقود وسد المخارج وبولغ في النفخ يحصل لها شكل ما يحويه. قوله : ( متفاصلة في الحقيقة إلخ) كما هو عند القائلين بالجزء . قوله : (أو كانت متواصلة في الحقيقة عند الحكماء والتواصل لا ينافي التدافع لأنه إنما يقتضي وجود الأجزاء انفصالها فالتدافع بينها حاصل مع الاتصال وبذلك تتحرك الأجزاء عن أمكنتها بالذات فلا يلزم أن يكون الحجر الهابط سيالاً على ما وهم ثم ذلك التدافع الموجب للحركة قد يكون طبيعياً كما في الماء المنحدر ، وقد يكون قسرياً كما في الرمل . قوله : ( متفاصلة في الحقيقة) وهو الأظهر لأن تدافعها مما لا شبهة فيه الهواء قبول تلك الإحالة فكيف يتصور أن تداخل النار الهواء وتبقى على صورتها النوعية؟ فيفيد النار سهولة قبول الأشكال على أن مداخلة الأجزاء الأرضية للنار التي عندنا ربما يدعى أنها أكثر من مداخلة الهواء على تقدير ثبوتها كما هو الظاهر. فكيف لا تكون تلك المداخلة ليبوسة تلك الأجزاء المداخلة مانعة عن قبول الأشكال ؟ فليتأمل . قوله : ( فإنه عبارة عن تدافع الأجزاء كلام الملخص الذي نقله الشارح يدل على أن مراد المصنف حركة بسبب التدافع . قوله : ( أو كانت متواصلة في الحقيقة فإن قلت المتواصلة في الحقيقة الأجزاء لها بالفعل بل لها أجزاء فرضية فتدافعها أيضاً فرضي . فكيف يكون سبباً للحركة الخارجية الثابتة للمجموع؟ قلت : أجيب بأن ذوات الأجزاء محققة، وإن كانت جزئيتها فرضية، وذلك يكفي في كون تدافعها خارجياً مبدأ للحركة الخارجية بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أنه يلزم أن يكون هبوط الحجر المرمي إلى فوق سيلاناً . اللهم إلا أن يقال : في التدافع إشارة إلى أن سبب الحركة مدافعة هو البعض البعض حتى لو انفرد جزء أصغر ما يكون لم يتحرك لكن يلزم على هذا أن لا تكون حركة الماء إلى المكان المنحدر سيلاناً .فتأمل. قوله: (لأنه متصل واحد في الحقيقة إلخ) في كون السيال متصلاً واحداً في الحقيقة نظر لجواز أن يكون السيلان سبباً للانفكاك بين الأجزاء نعم الماء الراكد متصل .
That fire is wet, let alone being the wettest of elements. (And the second of them) i.e., the second of the investigations (that wetness is different from fluidity, for it (fluidity) is an expression of the mutual repulsion of parts, whether they are actually separate [mutafāṣila] in reality continuous in perception [al-ḥiss], or also continuous in reality. And fluidity, by this interpretation, occurs in what is not wet, like flowing sand despite it being naturally dry, and also occurs in what is wet, like flowing water. And in al-Mulakhkhaṣ, fluidity is movements occurring in bodies that are actually separate in reality, continuous in perception, some pushing others. According to this interpretation, it follows that fluidity does not exist in water according to the view of the philosophers, because it is a single continuum in reality. His saying: ('So it does not follow that fire is wet, neither pure fire nor the fire we have, since its nature does not have ease of accepting shape, and if we suppose its occurrence in our fire through admixture of air, we said 'if we suppose' because observation indicates its assuming a shape of some sort in which it is kindled, but ease of shaping is unknown, for upon kindling it immediately acquires a pinecone shape; if it is intensified and the vessel is filled with fuel, the vents sealed, and blowing is intensified, it acquires the shape of what contains it.') His saying: ('Actually separate in reality etc.') as according to the proponents of the atom. His saying: ('Or continuous in reality according to the philosophers; and continuity does not negate repulsion because it only requires the existence of the parts? their separation? so repulsion between them occurs along with continuity, and thereby the parts move from their places essentially; so it does not follow that the falling stone is fluid, as was imagined; then that repulsion causing motion may be natural, as in descending water, or coercive, as in sand.') His saying: ('Actually separate in reality') and it is the more apparent because their repulsion is without doubt. (The air accepts that alteration; how then can it be imagined that fire penetrates air while remaining in its specific form? That would give fire ease of accepting shapes; moreover, the admixture of earthy parts in our fire might be claimed to be greater than the admixture of air, if it is established, as is apparent. So how would that admixture, due to the dryness of those admixed parts, not prevent the acceptance of shapes? So let it be considered.) His saying: ('For it is an expression of mutual repulsion of parts' – the words of al-Mulakhkhaṣ that the commentator transmitted indicate that the author intended movement due to repulsion. His saying: ('Or continuous in reality' – if you say: The parts of that which is continuous in reality are not actual; rather it has only hypothetical parts, so their repulsion is also hypothetical. How then can it be a cause of the external motion established for the whole? I say: It is answered that the essences of the parts are real, even if their partiality is hypothetical, and that suffices for their repulsion to be external, a principle for external motion.) There remains here a discussion, namely that it would require that the descent of a stone thrown upwards be fluidity. Unless it is said: In repulsion there is an indication that the cause of motion is the mutual pushing of parts, such that if a smallest part were isolated, it would not move. But this entails that the movement of water to a descending place would not be fluidity. So reflect. His saying: ('Because it is a single continuum in reality etc.') – there is consideration regarding the flowing thing being a single continuum in reality, because it is possible that fluidity is the cause of separation between the parts. Yes, still water is connected.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction. It begins with the second investigation of the section on wetness and dryness, which asserts that wetness (ruṭūba) is different from fluidity (sīlān). The author defines fluidity as the mutual repulsion (tadāfuʿ) of parts, whether those parts are actually separate in reality but continuous in perception (as the atomists hold) or also continuous in reality (as the philosophers hold). He then notes that by this definition, fluidity can exist in non-wet things like flowing sand (which is naturally dry) and in wet things like water. He cites al-Mulakhkhaṣ, which defines fluidity as movements in bodies that are actually separate but perceptually continuous, pushing one another. Following this definition, water would not exhibit fluidity on the philosophers' view because it is a single continuous reality. The subsequent commentary (قوله) addresses various objections: first, that fire might be considered wet; the commentator argues that fire's shape is determined by kindling conditions, not by inherent ease of shaping. Next, the discussion of parts: for atomists, parts are separate; for philosophers, continuity does not prevent repulsion, as parts can still push each other while connected, producing natural or forced motion. The commentator favors the view of actual separation. A further note clarifies that the author's reference to 'mutual repulsion of parts' indicates motion due to repulsion. The problem of continuous bodies' hypothetical parts is raised: their repulsion would be hypothetical, not a real cause of external motion. The answer is that the essences of the parts are real even if their partiality is hypothetical, making the repulsion external and a valid principle for motion. A remaining issue: under the definition, a stone thrown upward would have to be fluid (since it has repulsion?), but the response restricts fluidity to cases where the cause of motion is mutual pushing of parts such that no single part would move alone. However, this would exclude water flowing downhill, which seems problematic. The final note suggests reconsidering whether a flowing thing is truly a single continuum, since fluidity may cause separation.

رطوبة (ruṭūba)
سيلان (sīlān)
تدافع (tadāfuʿ)
متفاصلة (mutafāṣila)
متواصلة (mutawāṣila)
حكماء (ḥukamāʾ)
جزء (juzʾ)
ملخص (Mulakhkhaṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fluidity is the same as wetness; a fluid must be wet.
Correction: The passage shows that dry sand flows, so fluidity is defined by mutual repulsion of parts, not by wetness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of fluidity (sīlān)?
A) It is the ease of accepting shapes.
B) It is the mutual repulsion of parts, whether actually separate or continuous.
C) It is the wetness of a body.
D) It is the movement of water only.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that fluidity is a kinematic property (mutual repulsion of parts) not tied to wetness, as demonstrated by dry flowing sand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that flowing sand is fluid despite being dry. What surface-level 'wetness' in your spiritual life might be hiding a deeper 'dryness' that needs attention? How can you distinguish between outward appearances and inner reality?
?
If fluidity is defined by mutual repulsion of parts, consider your own actions: Are you moved by internal conviction (like natural motion) or external pressure (like coercive motion)? Which type of motion defines your obedience to Allah?
?
This passage forces a re-examination of a basic physical concept. What basic belief about your faith have you accepted without scrutiny, assuming it to be self-evident? How can you apply the same critical method here to deepen your certainty?

Dispute on the Essence of Wetness in Different Substances
Sanusi presents two opinions on whether wetness of water, oil, and mercury differs in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam debate on whether wetness has a single essence or multiple species, and why al-Razi considered both views plausible.

Core Idea
Wetness may be one species or many—both views are defensible.

The nature of physical qualities like wetness was debated among theologians and philosophers. This passage from Sanusi presents two opposing positions on whether the wetness of water, oil, and mercury are essentially the same or different. Imam al-Razi's neutral verdict invites us to reflect on the limits of rational inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style used to present both opinions.
2
Identify the two opinions
The first opinion states wetness differs in essence across substances, making it a genus with species. The second states wetness is one species, with differences due to mixing with dry.
Contrast the structure: genus+species vs. one species + external factor.
3
Examine al-Razi's conclusion
He says both opinions are possible. Consider why he does not choose one. In kalam, when proof is lacking, multiple positions can be accepted.
4
Reflect on the significance
This debate shows that even basic physical properties can be ambiguous under rational scrutiny. It highlights the humility of kalam in the face of empirical complexity.
Connect this to broader themes of the course.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعم بعضهم أن رطوبة الماء مخالفة بالماهية لرطوبة الدهن المخالفة لرطوبة الزئبق. فالرطوبة جنس تحتها أنواع وزعم آخرون أن ماهيتها واحدة بالنوع والاختلاف، بسبب اختلاط اليابس بالرطب . قال الإمام الرازي: كلا القولين محتمل .
Some claimed that the wetness of water is essentially different from the wetness of oil, which is different from the wetness of mercury. Thus wetness is a genus with species under it. Others claimed that its essence is one in species, and the difference is due to the mixing of dry with wet. Imam al-Razi said: Both opinions are possible.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the reality of wetness in different substances. Some philosophers argue that the wetness of water, oil, and mercury are essentially different, making 'wetness' a genus with multiple species. Others argue that wetness is a single species, and the apparent differences arise from mixture with dry elements. Imam al-Razi states that both views are tenable, leaving the matter open.

Key Terms
رطوبة
ماهية
جنس
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's statement shows indecision or uncertainty.
Correction: In kalam, acknowledging the rational possibility of both views is a valid position when evidence is inconclusive. It reflects careful scholarship, not indecision.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two opinions on the wetness of water, oil, and mercury?
A) They are different in essence; they are identical in species.
B) They are identical in essence; they are different in species.
C) They are different in essence; they are identical in genus.
D) They are identical in genus; they are different in species.

Key Takeaway
The wetness of different substances may be considered either a single species or multiple species, and kalam scholars accept such ambiguity when reason cannot decide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difference of opinion among scholars, do you feel compelled to pick one side or can you accept that Allah may have left some matters ambiguous? How does this affect your humility before knowledge?
?
Think of a recent disagreement you had with someone. Did you focus on superficial differences or try to understand the essence of their position? How can the lesson of wetness—being one essence or many—help you approach disagreements in your own life?
?
This passage uses everyday things—water, oil, mercury—to discuss deep questions of existence. When you use these substances daily, do you ever pause to reflect on their reality as signs of Allah's creation? What might change if you did?

The Possibility of an Intermediate Quality Between Wetness and Dryness
Sanusi discusses the unknown status of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is not known with certainty, and that its possibility remains doubtful from an essential perspective.

Core Idea
The existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is not known, but remains intellectually possible.

After discussing the nature of wetness and dryness, Sanusi raises a subtle question: Could there be an intermediate quality that contradicts both, like redness between black and white? This passage presents the author's cautious position that we cannot confirm or deny such a quality, but its essential possibility is not ruled out.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by an answer.
The passage combines both the original text and the commentary.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage uses the analogy of redness between black and whiteness. This helps illustrate what an intermediate quality would be like.
Analogies are common in kalam to clarify abstract concepts.
3
Grasp the key phrase
Focus on the phrase 'إمكان وجودها مشكوك فيه'. It means its possibility is doubtful, not impossible. The commentator clarifies this refers to essential possibility.
Essential possibility means the quality is not inherently impossible for reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: هل توجد كيفية متوسطة بين الرطوبة واليبوسة تنافيهما كالحمرة بين السواد والبياض أو لا توجد الحق أنه غير معلوم ، وأنَّ إمكان وجودها مشكوك فيه . قوله : ( وإنَّ إمكان وجودها إلخ) أي الإمكان الذاتي وإن كانت ممكناً عند العقل.
Fourth: Is there an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness that contradicts both, like redness between blackness and whiteness, or is there not? The truth is that it is not known, and that its possibility is doubtful. His saying: 'and that its possibility etc.' means essential possibility, even if it is possible according to reason.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether an intermediate quality exists between wetness and dryness, analogous to redness between black and white. The author states that the truth is not known—neither existence nor non-existence can be confirmed. The commentator clarifies that 'possibility' here refers to essential possibility (possibility in itself), not preponderance, and that it remains intellectually possible.

Key Terms
كيفية متوسطة
رطوبة
يبوسة
إمكان ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubtful means impossible
Correction: The phrase 'مشكوك فيه' does not assert impossibility; it indicates that the mind cannot determine due to lack of evidence. The commentator explicitly states that essential possibility remains.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness?
A) It exists necessarily.
B) It does not exist.
C) Its existence is not known, and its possibility is doubtful.
D) Its existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is not known, but its essential possibility is not rejected by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the existence of an intermediate quality between wetness and dryness is not known, but its possibility is not rejected by reason. Are there areas of your faith where you have assumed certainty about something that is actually unknown? How does acknowledging intellectual possibility while withholding judgment affect your humility before Allah's creation?
?
You might be tempted to insist either that the intermediate exists or does not. The lesson teaches that the truth is we do not know. When have you insisted on a position in religious matters without evidence? What would it look like for you to remain in a state of 'not known' about something, trusting that Allah knows best?
?
The concept of essential possibility (imkān dhātī) allows the mind to consider what is not inherently impossible. Consider a matter of Islamic theology that you find difficult. Can you distinguish between what is inherently impossible and what is merely unknown? How does this distinction give you intellectual humility?

The Regress Argument on Defining Wetness
Sanusi presents the regress argument on defining wetness and refutes a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining wetness as receptivity to shapes leads to an infinite regress and how the privative interpretation avoids it.

Core Idea
Defining wetness as a real quality leads to infinite regress; only privative definition stops it.

This lesson examines a key argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya regarding the definition of wetness. Sanusi presents the argument that interpreting wetness as receptivity to shapes either results in an infinite regress of receptivities or forces a privative (non-existent) interpretation. He then refutes a proposed solution, clarifying the principles of attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'al-Khamis' indicating it is the fifth discussion.
2
Identify the two interpretations of wetness
The passage presents two possible definitions: wetness as receptivity to shapes, and wetness as the cause of receptivity. See how each leads to regress.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator rejects the idea of ending at a privative receptivity, using a principle about attribution of privative qualities. Grasp why this refutation is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : ذكر في المباحث المشرقية أن الرطوبة إن فسرت بقابلية الأشكال كانت عدمية وإلا احتاجت إلى قابلية أخرى فيتسلسل، وإن فسرت بعلة القابلية فكذلك لأن الجسم لذاته قابل للأشكال. فلا تكون هذه القابلية معللة بعلة زائدة على ذات الجسم وإن سلم. قوله : (فيتسلسل وما قيل : يجوز الانتهاء إلى قابلية عدمية فمدفوع بما مر في الأمور العامة بأن كل ما من شأنها الوجود العيني فالاتصاف به فرع وجوده فلا يجوز الاتصاف بقابلية عدمية إلا أن يقال باختلاف القابليات بالماهية. قوله : ( وإن فسرت إلخ ) هذا الترديد بالنظر إلى ما وقع في الشفاء حيث فسرها بالقابلية، ثم قال : إنه قول مجازي والمراد ما يوجب القابلية. قوله : ( بعلة القابلية) أي بكيفية. قوله : ( فكذلك ) أي عدمية إذ لا شيء سوى الجسم يقتضي القابلية المذكورة . ثم قال: كونها وجودية على تفسيرهم فالأشبه أنها ليست محسوسة لأن الهواء رطب لا محالة بذلك المعنى. فلو كانت الرطوبة محسوسة لكانت رطوبة الهواء المعتدل الساكن محسوسة، فكان الهواء دائماً محسوساً فكان يجب أن لا يشك الجمهور في وجوده ولا يظنوا أن الفضاء الذي بين السماء والأرض خلاء صرف وإذا فسرناها بالكيفية المقتضية لسهولة الالتصاق . فالأظهر أنها وجودية محسوسة وإن كان للبحث فيه مجال.
Fifth: It is mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that wetness, if interpreted as receptivity to shapes, is privative; otherwise it would need another receptivity, leading to regress. And if interpreted as the cause of receptivity, likewise, because the body in itself is receptive to shapes. So this receptivity is not caused by an attribute superadded to the body itself, even if conceded. His saying: 'leads to regress, and it was said: it is permissible to end at a privative receptivity, but it is refuted by what has passed in general matters that everything that is subject to external existence is contingent upon its existence, so it is not permissible to attribute a privative receptivity unless one posits a difference in the essences of receptivities.' His saying: 'if interpreted etc.' This alternation is with reference to what occurred in al-Shifa', where he interpreted it as receptivity, then said: it is a metaphorical statement, and what is meant is that which necessitates receptivity. His saying: 'by cause of receptivity' means a quality. His saying: 'likewise' means privative, since nothing besides the body necessitates the mentioned receptivity. Then he said: According to their interpretation, its being positive—the more likely is that it is not sensible, because air is inevitably wet in that sense. If wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be sensible, and then air would always be sensible, and it would be necessary that the public not doubt its existence and not think that the space between heaven and earth is pure void. And if we interpret it as the quality necessitating ease of adhesion, then the more apparent is that it is positive and sensible, although there is room for investigation.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part analysis of the definition and nature of wetness. First, it presents an argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: if wetness is defined as the receptivity to shapes, it is privative (non-existent quality); otherwise an infinite regress of receptivities results. If defined as the cause of receptivity, the same regress occurs because the body itself is already receptive to shapes, so no superadded cause is needed. The commentator then addresses an objection about ending at a privative receptivity, rejecting it based on the principle that attribution of a privative quality is invalid without difference in essences. The commentator also clarifies that the interpretation from al-Shifa' treats 'receptivity' metaphorically, meaning the cause of receptivity. Finally, the discussion turns to whether wetness is a positive sensible quality. On the interpretation as mere receptivity, wetness would be non-sensible, as air is wet in that sense yet not sensed, leading to the false conclusion that air is always sensed. On the interpretation as a quality causing ease of adhesion, wetness appears to be positive and sensible, though the author notes room for debate.

Key Terms
قابلية
علة القابلية
عدم
وجود
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining wetness as a privative attribute is a valid way to avoid regress without further justification.
Correction: The passage shows that a privative attribute cannot be attributed to something unless there is a difference in the essences of receptivities, which is not the case here.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of interpreting wetness as receptivity to shapes?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It proves wetness is a positive quality.
C) It makes wetness sensible.
D) It shows wetness is not a quality.

Key Takeaway
Defining wetness as a positive quality leads to infinite regress; the only way to avoid it is to accept it as a privative attribute, but that too is problematic without additional assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own beliefs about Allah's attributes, do you ever rely on 'common sense' definitions that might hide a hidden regress? Will you commit to examining each attribute with the same logical scrutiny Sanusi applies to wetness?
?
This lesson shows that even a simple concept like 'wetness' can lead to deep philosophical problems. Have you ever held a theological position that, when pushed, reveals an infinite regress? Are you willing to reject that position even if it feels comfortable?
?
If you cannot define wetness without falling into contradiction, how much more careful must you be when defining matters of faith? What step will you take today to deepen your understanding of kalam and logic to avoid such errors?

Is Wetness Sensible? Two Interpretations
Sanusi discusses the interpretation from al-Shifa' and the sensibility of wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of wetness from al-Shifa' and the arguments for and against wetness being a sensible quality.

Core Idea
Wetness as receptivity is insensible; wetness as ease of adhesion is sensible.

This lesson continues from the regress argument and explores a different interpretation of wetness from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa'. Sanusi considers two ways to define wetness: as the cause of receptivity (a quality) or as the quality that causes ease of adhesion. He then examines whether wetness under these definitions is a sensible quality, using the example of air to argue against sensibility, and then presenting an alternative that is sensible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Note the two interpretations mentioned.
The passage begins with a reference to al-Shifa' where wetness is initially defined as receptivity but then said to be metaphorical.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation is 'what necessitates receptivity' (a quality). The second is 'the quality necessitating ease of adhesion'. Note how each leads to a different conclusion about sensibility.
3
Analyze the argument about air
The author argues that if wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be sensed, but people do not sense air, so wetness cannot be sensible under that interpretation.

Source Text

الخامس : ذكر في المباحث المشرقية أن الرطوبة إن فسرت بقابلية الأشكال كانت عدمية وإلا احتاجت إلى قابلية أخرى فيتسلسل، وإن فسرت بعلة القابلية فكذلك لأن الجسم لذاته قابل للأشكال. فلا تكون هذه القابلية معللة بعلة زائدة على ذات الجسم وإن سلم. قوله : (فيتسلسل وما قيل : يجوز الانتهاء إلى قابلية عدمية فمدفوع بما مر في الأمور العامة بأن كل ما من شأنها الوجود العيني فالاتصاف به فرع وجوده فلا يجوز الاتصاف بقابلية عدمية إلا أن يقال باختلاف القابليات بالماهية. قوله : ( وإن فسرت إلخ ) هذا الترديد بالنظر إلى ما وقع في الشفاء حيث فسرها بالقابلية، ثم قال : إنه قول مجازي والمراد ما يوجب القابلية. قوله : ( بعلة القابلية) أي بكيفية. قوله : ( فكذلك ) أي عدمية إذ لا شيء سوى الجسم يقتضي القابلية المذكورة . ثم قال: كونها وجودية على تفسيرهم فالأشبه أنها ليست محسوسة لأن الهواء رطب لا محالة بذلك المعنى. فلو كانت الرطوبة محسوسة لكانت رطوبة الهواء المعتدل الساكن محسوسة، فكان الهواء دائماً محسوساً فكان يجب أن لا يشك الجمهور في وجوده ولا يظنوا أن الفضاء الذي بين السماء والأرض خلاء صرف وإذا فسرناها بالكيفية المقتضية لسهولة الالتصاق . فالأظهر أنها وجودية محسوسة وإن كان للبحث فيه مجال.
Fifth: It is mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya that wetness, if interpreted as receptivity to shapes, is privative; otherwise it would need another receptivity, leading to regress. And if interpreted as the cause of receptivity, likewise, because the body in itself is receptive to shapes. So this receptivity is not caused by an attribute superadded to the body itself, even if conceded. His saying: 'leads to regress, and it was said: it is permissible to end at a privative receptivity, but it is refuted by what has passed in general matters that everything that is subject to external existence is contingent upon its existence, so it is not permissible to attribute a privative receptivity unless one posits a difference in the essences of receptivities.' His saying: 'if interpreted etc.' This alternation is with reference to what occurred in al-Shifa', where he interpreted it as receptivity, then said: it is a metaphorical statement, and what is meant is that which necessitates receptivity. His saying: 'by cause of receptivity' means a quality. His saying: 'likewise' means privative, since nothing besides the body necessitates the mentioned receptivity. If wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be sensible, and then air would always be sensible, and it would be necessary that the public not doubt its existence and not think that the space between heaven and earth is pure void. Commentary
This passage is a multi-part analysis of the definition and nature of wetness. First, it presents an argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: if wetness is defined as the receptivity to shapes, it is privative (non-existent quality); otherwise an infinite regress of receptivities results. Is Wetness a Sensible Quality? Arguments and Responses
Analyzes whether wetness is a sensible quality through objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments about whether wetness is a sensible quality, including the role of the sense of touch and the distinction between different definitions.

Core Idea
Wetness may not be sensed in air due to compatibility, but it is sensible in water.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif examines a subtle question in natural philosophy: is wetness (al-rutuba) a quality we can directly sense, or is it inferred? The author presents objections and responses, analyzing both the physical and perceptual aspects. You will see how kalam reasoning navigates between empirical observation and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of wetness
Notice that the author first mentions an interpretation where wetness is 'existent' but not sensible, and then another where wetness is 'the quality requiring ease of adhesion.' This distinction is crucial for the arguments.
The first definition leads to wetness being insensible; the second makes it sensible.
2
Trace the argument from air
The author argues that if wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be sensed, but it is not. Follow the logic and see why this is problematic.
This argument is similar to one about heat and cold.
3
Understand the objections and responses
The author refutes the argument by showing that the same reasoning would make heat and cold insensible, but air can be moderate. Also, lack of sensation of one instance does not disprove the existence of the quality.
Pay attention to the role of the sense of touch and compatibility.
4
Consider the reconciliation of Ibn Sina's views
The passage ends with Ibn Sina's apparent contradiction and a possible resolution based on two meanings of wetness. Reflect on how this illustrates the nuanced nature of kalam discussions.
This shows that philosophical debates often hinge on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونها وجودية على تفسيرهم فالأشبه أنها ليست محسوسة لأن الهواء رطب لا محالة بذلك المعنى. فلو كانت الرطوبة محسوسة لكانت رطوبة الهواء المعتدل الساكن محسوسة، فكان الهواء دائماً محسوساً فكان يجب أن لا يشك الجمهور في وجوده ولا يظنوا أن الفضاء الذي بين السماء والأرض خلاء صرف وإذا فسرناها بالكيفية المقتضية لسهولة الالتصاق . فالأظهر أنها وجودية محسوسة وإن كان للبحث فيه مجال
قوله : ( فلو كانت الرطوبة محسوسة لكانت إلخ) فيه بحث إما أو لا فإنه يستلزم أن لا تكون الحرارة محسوسة لأن الهواء لا يخلو عنها لكونها مقتضى طبعه فلو كانت الحرارة محسوسة لكانت حرارة الهواء المعتدل الساكن محسوسة
قوله : ( فكان الهواء دائماً محسوساً إلخ وكذا لو قيل ببرودة الهواء فاندفع ما قيل في جوابه، عدم خلو الهواء عن الحرارة والبرودة ممنوع، لأنه قد يكون معتدلاً بحيث لا يكون فيه حر ولا برد . كيف والاعتدال يقتضي مرتبة متوسطة لا الخلو عنهما ؟ وأما ثانياً فلان عدم إحساس فرد من أفراد الرطوبة لا يقتضي عدم إحساسها مطلقاً لجواز أن يكون ذلك بواسطة عدم انفعال اللامسة بذلك الفرد إما لضعفه أو لموافقته العضو اللامس، أو لاستمرار إحساسه. كيف وانتفاء الإدراك لشيء لا يدل على انتفائه في نفسه؟
قوله : ( فالأظهر أنها وجودية محسوسة) لأنه لا شك في إحساس شيء عند التصاق الماء الذي لا حر فيه ولا برد وليس ذلك نفس الالتصاق لأنه من الإضافة المعقولة ولا ذات الجسم لأنه جوهر فهو شيء آخر، وهو المعنى بالكيفية المقتضية .
الجسم . قلت : يجوز أن يكون علة السهولة هي الصورة النوعية فلا يثبت كيفية زائدة . قوله : ( فالأشبه أنها ليست محسوسة لأن الهواء إلخ قد يجاب عن ذلك : بأن الهواء الساكن إنما لا يحس به لموافقته للبدن بالمجاورة ومصداق ذلك أن الهواء المجاور إذا زال عن البدن وجاء مكانه هواء جديد أحس البدن به لمخالفته، وإن رطوبة الهواء إنما لا يحس به لأن إحساس اللامسة إنما هو بآلات صلبة كما عرف في موضعه ولا يتأثر تلك الامن مؤثر قوي في التأثير، وليس الهواء المعتدل الساكن يقوي برطوبته على التأثير فيها، وهذا لا يدل على أن كيفية الرطوبة ليست محسوسة أصلاً كما أن عدم إبصار واحد من المبصرات لانتفاء شرط من شرائط الرؤية لا يدل على أنه ليس من المبصرات هذا فإن قلت لو تم ما ذكره الإمام لدل على أن الحرارة والبرودة أيضاً غير محسوسة لأن الهواء لا يخلو عنهما فيلزم أن يكون الهواء على تقدير كونهما محسوسين محسوساً دائماً فكان يجب أن لا يشك الجمهور في وجوده . قلت : عدم خلو الهواء عن الحرارة والبرودة ممنوع، لأنه قد يكون معتدلاً بحيث لا يكون فيه حر ولا برد صرح به الإمام في المباحث المشرقية.
قوله: (فالأظهر أنها وجودية (محسوسة) لأنا إذا غمسنا الأصبع في الماء أحسسنا فيه كيفية بها يحكم بالتصاقه وسهولته ومجال البحث أن يقال لعله من قبيل إدراك وحدة
وقد قال ابن سينا في فصل الاسطقسات من الشفاء إلى أنها غير محسوسة وفي كتاب النفس منه إلى أنها محسوسة ولعله أراد أن الرطوبة بمعنى سهولة قبول الأشكال غير محسوسة وبمعنى الالتصاق محسوسة هذا محصول كلامه فعليك بالتدبر فيه والاطلاع على ما يحتويه .
That it (wetness) is existent according to their interpretation, so the more appropriate is that it is not sensible because air is necessarily wet in that sense. So if wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be sensible, and thus air would always be sensible, and thus it would be necessary that the public not doubt its existence and not think that the space between heaven and earth is absolute void. And if we interpret it as the quality requiring ease of adhesion, then the more apparent is that it is an existent sensible quality, although there is room for investigation. His statement: (If wetness were sensible, then etc.) There is investigation into it, first: because it would entail that heat is not sensible, because air is never free from it since it is required by its nature; so if heat were sensible, then the heat of moderate still air would be sensible. His statement: (Thus air would always be sensible etc.) And likewise if one said with the coldness of air, then what was said in its answer is refuted: that air is not free from heat and cold is denied, because it may be moderate such that there is neither heat nor cold in it. How so? Moderation requires an intermediate degree, not absence of both. And secondly, because the non-sensation of one individual of wetness does not necessitate its absolute non-sensation, for it is possible that this is due to the lack of affection of the sense of touch by that individual, either because of its weakness or its compatibility with the touching organ, or because of the continuity of its sensation. How so? The absence of perception of a thing does not indicate its non-existence in itself. His statement: (The more apparent is that it is an existent sensible quality) because there is no doubt about sensing something when water that has neither heat nor cold sticks, and that is not the adhesion itself because it is a relative relation, nor the substance of the body because it is a substance; it is something else, and it is what is meant by the quality requiring (ease). The body. I say: It is possible that the cause of ease is the specific form, so an additional quality is not established. His statement: (The more appropriate is that it is not sensible because air etc.) One may answer that: still air is not sensed only because of its compatibility with the body by adjacency; the proof of that is that when the adjacent air leaves the body and new air takes its place, the body senses it due to its difference. And the wetness of air is not sensed because the sense of touch perceives only with solid organs as known in its place, and it is not affected except by an effective influence, and moderate still air is not strong enough with its wetness to affect it. This does not indicate that the quality of wetness is not sensible at all, just as the non-seeing of one visible object due to the absence of a condition of vision does not indicate that it is not among visible objects. If you say: If what the Imam mentioned were complete, it would indicate that heat and cold are also not sensible because air is never free from them, so it would follow that air, on the assumption that they are sensible, would always be sensible, and thus the public would not doubt its existence. I say: The claim that air is never free from heat and cold is denied, because it may be moderate such that there is neither heat nor cold in it, as the Imam stated in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya. His statement: (The more apparent is that it is an existent sensible quality) because when we dip our finger in water, we sense a quality by which we judge its adhesion and ease, and there is room for investigation in that one might say it is perhaps of the type of perception of a unity. And Ibn Sina said in the chapter on elements from al-Shifa that it is not sensible, and in his Book of the Soul that it is sensible; perhaps he meant that wetness in the sense of ease of accepting forms is not sensible, and in the sense of adhesion is sensible. This is the gist of his words, so it is upon you to reflect on it and understand what it contains.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument about whether the quality of wetness (al-rutuba) is a sensible quality (mah-susa) or not. The discussion is structured as a series of objections and responses. The author first presents a view that wetness, according to one interpretation, is more likely not sensible because air is always wet in that sense; if wetness were sensible, then the wetness of moderate still air would be felt, but it is not, and people even doubt the existence of air itself. Then the author considers another definition: wetness as the quality that causes ease of adhesion. Under this definition, wetness appears to be an existent sensible quality, though there is still room for debate. The text then examines objections to the claim that wetness is not sensible. One objection shows that if the original argument were sound, it would also imply that heat is not sensible, which is absurd. Another objection argues that the lack of sensation of one instance does not disprove the existence of the quality, as there may be reasons like weakness or compatibility. The author also responds to a possible counter-argument about air being always hot or cold, citing the Imam's view that air can be moderate. Finally, the passage mentions Ibn Sina's apparent inconsistency between his works and suggests a reconciliation based on two meanings of wetness. The lesson ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wetness is always directly sensed.
Correction: Wetness may not be sensed in air due to compatibility and organ type, but it is sensible in water.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for why wetness of moderate still air is not sensed?
A) Because air is not wet at all
B) Because of compatibility with the body and lack of strong effect on the sense of touch
C) Because wetness is a relative relation
D) Because the sense of touch requires solid organs and air is not solid

Key Takeaway
Whether wetness is sensible depends on its definition; under the adhesion definition, it is sensible, as evidenced by touch in water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth simply because you couldn't perceive it directly, like the public doubting the existence of air? What unseen realities might you be ignoring, and what would it cost you to acknowledge them?
?
When you encounter contradictory statements from respected scholars (like Ibn Sina on wetness), do you seek a deeper resolution or do you choose one and discard the other? What does your approach reveal about your intellectual humility and pursuit of truth?
?
This lesson shows that definitions shape what we consider real and sensible. What definitions in your own life—about success, faith, or relationships—might be hiding deeper truths? Are you willing to reconsider them, even if it unsettles you?

Inclination: Definition and Theological Dispute
Defines inclination and presents the theological dispute over its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of inclination (i'timad) as the cause of resistance and the theological disagreement over its real existence.

Core Idea
Inclination is a metaphysical cause of resistance, and theologians debated whether it truly exists.

This lesson introduces a key concept in natural philosophy: inclination (i'timad), also called tendency. The passage defines it through Ibn Sina's lens as the cause of resistance, then reports a sharp disagreement among theologians: some deny it exists, while others affirm it as a necessary truth of experience. Understanding this dispute reveals deeper assumptions about the nature of bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the passage noting Ibn Sina's definition of inclination as what causes resistance to motion. Also notice the alternative view that inclination is identical to resistance.
The definition is from Ibn Sina's al-Hudud.
2
Identify the disputants and their positions
Find who denied and who affirmed the existence of inclination. Al-Isfarayini and his followers denied; the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris like al-Qadi affirmed.
Affirmation is said to be necessary and denying it is stubbornness against sense.
3
Analyze the argument of the deniers
Al-Isfarayini's argument: substances are all similar; differences in heaviness are due to quantity of parts, not an accident like inclination. So there is no such accident.
This shows the denial is based on a metaphysical principle, not ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث في الاعتماد وهو الميل ] ( في الاعتماد ) وهو المسمى بالميل عند الحكماء كما سيأتي ( وفيه مباحث : أحدها الاعتماد على ما ذكره ابن سينا في الحدود ( ما يوجب للجسم المدافعة لما يمنعه الحركة إلى جهة ما من الجهات وهذا تصريح منه بأن الاعتماد علة للمدافعة ( وقيل : هو نفس المدافعة ) المذكورة ( وقد اختلف فيه ) أي في وجود الاعتماد ( المتكلمون فنفاه الأستاذ أبو إسحاق ( الإسفرائيني وأتباعه ) وأثبته المعتزلة وكثير من أصحابنا كالقاضي بالضرورة ) أي قالوا : ثبوته ضروري ( ومنعه مكابرة للحس ) فإنَّ من حمل حجراً ثقيلاً
الشفاء
قوله : (فنفاء الإسناد وقال : إِنَّ الجواهر متماثلة ولا تفاوت بينها بالخفة والثقل إنما التفاوت في الأجسام بكثرة الأجزاء ،وقلتها فليس عرض في الجسم يسمى بالمدافعة أو بمبدئها .
[The Third Objective: On Inclination, which is Tendency] (On Inclination) It is called tendency by the philosophers, as will come. (It contains investigations: one of them: Inclination according to what Ibn Sina mentioned in al-Hudud: That which causes the body to resist whatever prevents its motion towards a certain direction. This is a declaration from him that inclination is the cause of resistance. (And it is said: It is the resistance itself) mentioned. (And the theologians differed) concerning its existence. (Thus Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and his followers denied it, and the Mu'tazila and many of our companions like al-Qadi affirmed it as necessary), i.e., they said its existence is necessary. (And its denial is stubbornness against sense) because whoever carries a heavy stone... al-Shifa. His statement: (Thus the denial of [its] attribution and he said: Substances are similar and there is no difference between them in lightness and heaviness; difference is only in bodies by abundance or paucity of parts, so there is no accident in the body called resistance or its principle.)
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of inclination (al-i'timad) also called tendency (al-mail) by the philosophers. It begins with a definition from Ibn Sina's al-Hudud: inclination is that which causes the body to resist (al-mudafa'a) whatever prevents its motion towards a certain direction. This makes inclination the cause of resistance, though some say it is exactly resistance itself. The passage then reports a dispute among theologians about whether inclination has real existence. Al-Isfarayini and his followers deny it, while the Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris like al-Qadi affirm it as known by necessity; they say denying it is stubbornness against sense evidence, because a person carrying a heavy stone feels resistance. The text then includes a gloss: Abu Ishaq's denial argued that substances are all similar, and differences in lightness and heaviness are due to the quantity of parts, not to an accident like inclination. Thus there is no accident called resistance or its principle. This sets up the debate between those who affirm inclination as a real quality and those who reduce apparent resistance to quantitative differences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying inclination is denying obvious physical experience.
Correction: The denial is based on a metaphysical principle that substances are similar; perceived heaviness is due to quantity of parts, not an accident.

Checkpoint

A) It is the motion itself

C) It is the weight of the body

Key Takeaway
Inclination is defined as the cause of resistance, but its real existence is disputed; al-Isfarayini denies it by arguing that all substances are essentially similar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Six Directions from Human Anatomy
Sanusi defines the six spatial directions in relation to human limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the six spatial directions are defined by reference to the human body, not as absolute cosmological categories.

Core Idea
Directions are relative to human anatomy — right, left, front, back, above, below.

This passage from Sanusi’s commentary explains an important point about how common people understand spatial directions. Rather than being abstract cosmic axes, the six directions are taken from the human body’s own orientation. Let’s see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's straightforward style.
The Arabic text is slightly truncated at the end due to OCR.
2
Identify the six directions
Find the names of the six directions listed in the passage: front, back, right, left, above, below.
These are given as ‘qudam’, ‘khalf’, ‘yamin’, ‘yasar’, ‘fawq’, ‘taht’.
3
Understand the anatomical basis for right and left
The passage explains that the right side is the one that is stronger in most cases and from which movement begins. The left is its opposite.
This shows that directions are grounded in human experience.
4
Reflect on the significance for kalam
Consider why the author begins with this conventional definition. It sets the stage for discussing attributes like direction in relation to Allah.
In kalam, such definitions avoid absolute spatial thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّ الجهات ) على ما اشتهر بين الناس ( ست أخذها العامة من جهات الإنسان ) وأطرافه ( التي هي القدام والخلف واليمين والشمال والفوق والتحت). فإنَّ الإنسان يحيط به جنبان عليهما اليدان وظهر وبطن ورأس وقدم فالجانب الذي هو أقوى في الغالب ومنه ابتداء الحركة يسمى يميناً وما يقابله يساراً، وما [؟]
And know that the directions (al-jihaat), according to what is well-known among people, are six: the common people take them from the directions of man and his limbs, which are front, back, right, left, above, and below. For man is surrounded by two flanks on which are the two hands, a back, a belly, a head, and a foot. So the side that is stronger in most cases and from which motion begins is called the right, and what faces it is the left, and what [؟]
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines the six spatial directions (jihaat) by relating them to the human body. It states that the commonly recognized directions are six: front (qudam), back (khalf), right (yamin), left (yasar), above (fawq), and below (taht). These are derived from the human's own orientation. The text describes the human body: it is surrounded by two sides (the hands), a back, a belly, a head, and a foot. The side that is generally stronger and from which motion begins is called the right (yamin), and the opposite side is called the left (yasar). The passage is cut off before completing the explanation of front and back, but the method is clear: spatial directions are not absolute but are anchored in human anatomy and experience.

The General Definition of the Six Directions
Al-Jurjani defines the common notion of the six directions and their application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the common notion of the six directions is defined relative to the human body and then generalized to animals and other objects.

Core Idea
The six directions are relative to the body, not absolute.

Al-Jurjani begins by explaining the common understanding of the six directions, rooted in the human body's orientation. This passage shows how these relative definitions are then extended to animals and even inanimate objects. Understanding this relativity is key to later discussions of direction in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the key terms: قدام (front), خلف (back), فوق (above), تحت (below).
2
Identify how front and back are defined for humans
Notice that front is defined by facing the face, natural movement, and the sense of sight. Back is its opposite.
This definition is specific to humans.
3
See how the definition is applied to animals
Common people applied the same six directions to animals but adjusted above and below relative to their spines.
Front and back remain based on movement.
4
Understand the generalization to all bodies
The definition was further generalized to non-animal moving bodies, where front is the direction of motion.
For non-animal bodies, front and back are not fixed; they change when motion changes.
5
Reflect on the relativity of directions
Consider how this relative understanding contrasts with an absolute view of space.
This relativity becomes important in later theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : الاعتماد وهو الميل يحاذي وجهه وإليه حركاته بالطبع وهناك حاسة الإبصار يسمى قداماً، وما يقابله خلفاً وما يلي رأسه بالطبع يسمى فوقا وما يقابله تحتا. ولما لم يكن عند العامة سوى ما ذكر وقعت أوهامهم على هذه الجهات الست واعتبروها في سائر الحيوانات أيضاً لكنهم جعلوا الفوق مايلي ظهورها بالطبع والتحت ما يقابله ثم عمموا اعتبارها في سائر الأجسام وإن لم يكن لها أجزاء متمايزة على الوجه المذكور. قوله : ( ثم عمموا اعتبارها إلخ ) بأن شبهوها بالإنسان بوجه من الوجوه إلا أن اعتبار القدام والخلف للحيوان حاصل حال حركته وسكونه بخلاف الأجسام المتحركة غير الحيوان فإنَّ اعتبارها فيها حين كونها متحركة فإنَّ الجهة التي تتحرك إليها قدامها والمتروكة خلفها وإن تغيرت حركتها تغير قدامها وخلفها كذا في الشفاء.
The Third Observatory - The Third Objective: Dependence (al-i‘timād) and it is inclination (al-mayl). It faces his face and to it his movements naturally, and there is the sense of sight, so it is called front (qudam); what is opposite it is back (khalaf); what is attached to his head naturally is called above (fawq); what is opposite it is below (taht). Since the common people have nothing beyond what was mentioned, their illusions (awhām) fell upon these six directions, and they considered them in all other animals as well, but they made above what attaches to their backs naturally and below what is opposite it. Then they generalized their consideration to all other bodies, even if they do not have distinct parts in the aforementioned manner. His saying: (Then they generalized their consideration, etc.) meaning they analogized them to man in some way, except that the consideration of front and back for animals obtains in both states of motion and rest, unlike non-animal moving bodies, for their consideration in them is when they are moving: the direction toward which they move is their front, and the one left behind is their back. If their motion changes, their front and back change. Thus in al-Shifā’.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the common (general) definition of the six directions. It begins by defining front (qudam) as that which faces the face and toward which movement naturally tends, given the sense of sight. Back (khalaf) is the opposite. Above (fawq) is that which is above the head naturally, and below (taht) is its opposite. Then it states that common people only consider these six directions and apply them to animals, making front and back relative to movement, and then generalize to all bodies even without distinct parts. The commentary explains that for animals, front and back are determined by movement and rest, while for non-animal moving bodies, they are determined by the direction of motion.

Correction: The passage shows they are relative to the body and its motion.

The Specialist Definition of Directions Based on Three Dimensions
Al-Jurjani explains the specialist view that directions are determined by the six ends of three perpendicular dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specialist definition of directions refines the common view by grounding the six directions in the three perpendicular dimensions of a body.

Core Idea
The six directions are the ends of three intersecting dimensions.

Al-Jurjani now presents the technical definition of directions used by specialists. Unlike the common people who simply assign directions based on body parts, the specialists define directions as the six ends of the three dimensions (length, width, depth) that intersect at right angles. This definition provides a consistent, mathematical basis for understanding directions in any body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Jurjani builds the definition step by step.
2
Identify the three dimensions and their direction pairs
The passage names three dimensions: longitudinal, width, and remaining (depth). For each, it gives the two direction ends: above/below, right/left, front/back. Reflect on how your own body maps to these.
The dimension names are tied to a standing human figure for clarity.
3
Compare the specialist and common approaches
The passage explains that the specialist consideration includes the common one plus the intersection of dimensions. The commentary adds that common people first assign directions to extremities, then measure dimensions, while specialists do the reverse. Consider the significance of this refinement.
This shows how the specialist view is more systematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أطراف الأبعاد الجسمية المتقاطعة على الزوايا القائمة فإن كل بعد منها له طرفان هما جهتان فلكل جسم جهات ست إلا أنَّ امتياز بعضها عن بعض هاهنا يتوقف على اعتبار الأجزاء المتميزة في الجسم فطرفا الامتداد الطولي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار طول قامته حين هو قائم فالفوق والتحت وطرفا الامتداد العرضي يسميهما باعتبار عرض قامته باليمين والشمال وطرفا الامتداد الباقي يسميهما باعتبار ثخن قامته بالقدام والخلف فالاعتبار الخاصي يشتمل على الاعتبار العامي مع زيادة هي تقاطع الأبعاد، فإنَّ العامة غافلون عنها وإن أمكن تطبيق ما اعتبروه عليها. قوله : ( فلكل جسم جهات ست إلخ) هي ما يحاذي الأطراف الستة . قوله : ( يتوقف على اعتبار (الأجزاء ولذا لا امتياز للجهات في الكثرة إلا بعد فرض الامتياز بين أبعادها الثلاثة . قوله : (يسميها) على صيغة التأنيث والضمير راجع إلى الخاصة . قوله : ( فالاعتبار الخاصي يشمل إلخ حيث اعتبروا في تميز الجهات الأجزاء المتميزة في الجسم وهي الأطراف . قوله : ( وإن أمكن إلخ) بناء على أن الأبعاد الواصلة بين الأطراف متقاطعة على زوايا قوائم : وفرق آخر بين الاعتبارين أنَّ العامة اعتبروا الأطراف وعينوا الجهات بإزائها ثم اعتبروا الأبعاد الواصلة بينها. فقالوا : طول الإنسان من رأسه إلى قدمه وعرضه من يمينه إلى يساره وعمقه من قدامه إلى خلفه والخاصة اعتبروا الأبعاد المتقاطعة أولاً ، ثم اعتبروا أطرافها بإزائها الجهات كذا يستفاد من الشفاء.
From the ends of the bodily dimensions intersecting at right angles. For each dimension has two ends which are two directions, so every body has six directions, except that the distinction of some from each other here depends on considering the distinct parts in the body. So the two ends of the longitudinal extension—man calls them with respect to the length of his stature when he is upright—are above and below. The two ends of the width extension he calls with respect to the width of his stature: right and left. And the two ends of the remaining extension he calls with respect to the thickness of his stature: front and back. Thus the specialist consideration includes the common consideration with an addition: the intersection of the dimensions, which the common people are heedless of, although what they consider can be applied to it. His saying: (For every body has six directions, etc.) meaning that which faces the six extremities. His saying: (depends on considering the parts) so there is no distinction of directions in a simple body except after assuming a distinction between its three dimensions. His saying: (he calls) in feminine form, and the pronoun refers to the specialists. His saying: (Thus the specialist consideration includes, etc.) meaning where they considered for the distinction of directions the distinct parts in the body, which are the extremities. His saying: (And although possible, etc.) based on the fact that the dimensions connecting the extremities intersect at right angles. And another difference between the two considerations: the common people considered the extremities and assigned directions opposite to them, then considered the connecting dimensions; they said: man's length from his head to his foot, his width from his right to his left, and his depth from his front to his back. The specialists first considered the intersecting dimensions, then considered their extremities as directions. Thus it is understood from al-Shifā’.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the specialist (khāṣṣ) definition of directions. It states that directions are the six ends (aṭrāf) of the three bodily dimensions that intersect at right angles. Each dimension has two ends, giving six directions. However, distinguishing these ends depends on assuming distinct parts (ajzā') in the body. For a human standing upright, the ends of the longitudinal dimension are above and below; the ends of the width dimension are right and left; the ends of the remaining (depth) dimension are front and back. The specialist consideration includes the common one but adds the intersection of dimensions, which common people are unaware of—though their assignments can be mapped onto this. The commentary then explains that the common people first consider extremities and then dimensions, while the specialists first consider intersecting dimensions and then assign directions to their extremities. This shows the refinement by the specialists.

Why Six Directions Are an Illusion
Al-Jurjani argues that the limitation of directions to six is an illusion and can be multiplied infinitely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that the commonly accepted limitation of directions to six is a false illusion and how he demonstrates that such directions are not fixed in essence.

Core Idea
Common directions are relative, not absolute; only real directions like above and below are fixed.

Al-Jurjani challenges a widely held view among both the common people and the elite: that there are exactly six directions. He declares this limitation to be an illusion (wahm bāṭil). The passage explains that the basis for this limitation is a consideration that does not yield distinct essences, leading to interchangeable and infinite possibilities. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the strong opening statement calling the limitation a false illusion.
The commentary will help clarify technical terms like 'ghayru munnawwa‘'.
2
Identify the core claim
Al-Jurjani states that the limitation to six is an illusion. Find the phrase 'wahm bāṭil' and see how he dismisses the common explanation as 'not anything'.
His claim is radical: what seems obvious is actually mistaken.
3
Analyze the interchange example
The passage uses examples of lying on one's back or front to show how directions like right/left and front/back swap. Understand why this proves they are not distinct in essence.
This is the key evidence—if directions were real, they would not change when you change posture.
4
Grasp the consequence of infinity
If the common consideration truly established real directions, there would be an infinite number of directions, not just six. This refutes the idea of a fixed limit.
This logical consequence undermines the claim of exclusivity.
5
Distinguish real from relative directions
The commentary notes that above and below are real, fixed directions, unlike the others. Understand that not all six are equal; only some are genuine.
This nuance is important for a correct understanding of directions in kalam.

And that—i.e., the limitation of directions to six—is a false illusion (wahm bāṭil), even if it is well known and accepted among the common people and the elite. And what they mentioned in explaining that limitation is nothing. As for the first (common) aspect, it is a consideration that is not specific (ghayru munnawwa‘), because the directions obtained from it are not distinct in essence (māhiyya). Therefore, the directions may interchange: right becomes left and vice versa, front becomes back and vice versa—this is obvious. And if a person lies on his back, his above becomes front and his below becomes back, and the situation reverses when he lies on his front. So the directions obtained from this consideration are not real differences. And if the mentioned consideration were truly to establish a direction—i.e., to posit a real direction—then there would be infinite (ghayru mutanāhiya) directions, i.e., not limited. His saying: (Because it is a consideration that is not specific, so the judgment of their limitation to six is not valid.) The consideration of front and back in the aforementioned manner, and that their consideration relative to that toward which movement is and that from which it is, is not necessary. His saying: (And if a person lies on his back, etc.) This is a reinforcement of the text (matn) statement, otherwise he will verify that above and below are among the real directions that do not change at all. Yes, another attribute accompanies them.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage critiques the common notion that directions are limited to six. It begins by stating that this limitation is an illusion (wahm) and false, despite being widely accepted. The first (common) consideration is not specific (ghayr munnawwa‘) because the resulting directions are not distinct in essence; they interchange: right becomes left, front becomes back. For example, when a person lies on his back, what was above becomes front, and below becomes back. Hence, these directions are not real, fixed differences. Moreover, if the common consideration truly established a real direction, it would yield infinite directions, not just six. The commentary explains that the interchange shows the limitation is invalid, and clarifies that above and below are real, permanent directions—they do not interchange—unlike the others. This reinforces that the common limitation is not substantive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Directions are absolutely limited to six, and all six are equally real and fixed.
Correction: The common six are not all real; only above and below are fixed. The others are relative and interchangeable, and the limitation is an illusion.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani argue that the limitation of directions to six is an illusion?
A) Because the directions from the common consideration are not distinct in essence and can interchange, so they are not fixed.
B) Because the six directions are not mentioned in the Quran.
C) Because animals have different directions.
D) Because the specialist view shows there are only three directions.

Key Takeaway
The common limitation of directions to six is an illusion because the directions are not distinct in essence and would imply an infinite number if taken as real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many 'six directions' do you hold in your life—beliefs or habits that everyone around you accepts as absolute, but which you have never critically examined? What would it cost you to question one of them today, and what might you find on the other side?
?
The passage shows that above and below are real and unchanging, while the other directions shift with perspective. In your relationship with God, what are the absolute, non-negotiable truths (like above and below) that you must anchor to, no matter how your situation changes? Are you treating something relative as if it were fixed, or neglecting something fixed as if it were relative?
?
Al-Jurjani exposes an illusion accepted by both the common people and the elite. Is there a comfortable falsehood in your own community—perhaps in how you understand success, worship, or justice—that you have been too timid to examine? Will you have the courage to probe it before Allah asks you why you followed without seeking truth?

Why the Six Directions Are Not Real
Refutes the common view on bodily directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the common view that every body has exactly six real directions is false, and how the specific consideration (with intersecting dimensions) corrects it.

Core Idea
Directions that swap when you move cannot be real — true directions are fixed.

The idea that every body has six directions (above/below, right/left, front/back) seems obvious, but is it really true? In this passage, the author shows that if directions were real, they wouldn't change when you lie down or turn around. He argues that the common basis for identifying directions is arbitrary and leads to infinite directions. Instead, real directions are rooted in the intersecting dimensions of the body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the common view
Read the description of how people assign six directions based on standing posture, using the body's length, width, and depth.
Notice the dependence on human anatomy and posture.
2
See the refutation
Observe how directions like right/left and front/back swap when the body moves, proving they are not fixed essences. Also note the argument that if these were real directions, there would be infinite directions.
The key point: direction exchange shows lack of essence.
3
Grasp the specific view
The author hints that above and below are truly real and unchanging, unlike the other directions.
This is the positive teaching from the specific consideration.

The third observatory - the third objective: on reliance (i'timad) which is inclination. From the extremities of the bodily dimensions intersecting at right angles, for each dimension has two extremities which are two directions, so every body has six directions, except that the distinction of some from others here depends on considering the distinct parts in the body. So the two extremities of the longitudinal extension are called, by man by virtue of the length of his stature when he is standing, the above and the below; the two extremities of the latitudinal extension are called by virtue of the breadth of his stature, the right and the left; the two extremities of the remaining extension are called by virtue of the thickness of his stature, the front and the back. So the specific consideration includes the common consideration with the addition of the intersection of the dimensions, for the common people are heedless of it, although it is possible to apply what they considered to it. And that (i.e., the restriction of directions to six) is an illusion, false, even if it is famous and accepted among the common people and the elite. And what they mentioned in explaining that restriction is nothing. As for the first, the common, aspect: it is because it is a consideration that does not prevent (diversity), since the directions resulting from it are not different in essence; therefore directions may exchange, so the right becomes left and vice versa, the front becomes back and vice versa—and that is apparent. And if a man lies on his back, his above becomes front and his below becomes back, and the situation is reversed if he prostrates. So the directions resulting from this consideration are not different realities. And if the mentioned consideration were a verifier of a direction (i.e., establishing a real direction), there would be infinite directions, i.e., unlimited. He says: (For every body has six directions, etc.) They are what are opposite the six extremities. He says: (It depends on considering the parts) so there is no differentiation of directions in multiplicity except after assuming differentiation between its three dimensions. He says: (He calls them) in the feminine form; the pronoun refers to the specific (al-khassa). He says: (So the specific consideration includes, etc.) Where they, in distinguishing directions, considered the distinct parts in the body, which are the extremities. He says: (And if it is possible, etc.) Based on the fact that the dimensions connecting the extremities intersect at right angles. Another difference between the two considerations is that the common people considered the extremities and designated the directions opposite them, then considered the dimensions connecting them, so they said: the length of a man from his head to his foot, his breadth from his right to his left, and his depth from his front to his back. The specific people considered the intersecting dimensions first, then considered their extremities as directions opposite them. This is learned from al-Shifa'. He says: (So it is a consideration that does not prevent; so it is not valid to judge that they are restricted to six.) The consideration of front and back in the mentioned manner, and that considering them in relation to that toward which motion is and that from which it is is not necessary. He says: (And if a man lies on his back, etc.) This is support for the text's statement; otherwise, he will verify that the above and the below are among the real directions that do not change at all. Yes, another attribute accompanies them.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage refutes the common view that each body has exactly six real directions. It begins by explaining the common consideration: directions are derived from the three dimensions (length, width, depth) of the human body when standing, giving six directions (above/below, right/left, front/back). However, the author argues that this is not a real distinction because directions can exchange; for example, when lying down, 'above' becomes 'front'. If the consideration were to establish a real direction, then infinite directions would exist due to different positions. The commentary reinforces this by noting that the distinction depends on assuming distinct parts, and that the specific consideration adds the intersection of dimensions. The passage concludes that the above and below are truly real and unchanging, contrary to the common view.

جهات حقيقية (jihat haqiqiyya)

Misconception: That every body has exactly six fixed, real directions (above/below, right/left, front/back).
Correction: These six are not all real because they exchange with posture; only above and below are truly real and unchanging.
