Refuting the Specific View on Directions with the Cube Example
Refutes the specific view using cube and circle arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that directions are limited to three orthogonal dimensions is false, and how the cube example demonstrates that the number of directions depends on what aspects (surfaces, lines, points) are counted.

Core Idea
Directions are not fixed; a cube alone can have up to 26 directions.

The specific view insists that directions arise from three axes at right angles. al-Razi refutes this by examining a simple cube: depending on whether you count its surfaces, lines, or points, the number of 'directions' changes from 6 to 18 to 26. This lesson unpacks his argument and also addresses the special case of circles and spheres, which seem to lack actual directions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the specific view being refuted
Read the first few lines where the view is stated: that directions require three dimensions intersecting at right angles. Note why the author finds this problematic.
2
Follow the cube example enumeration
Read the passage where al-Razi counts the directions of a cube: by surfaces alone (6), by surfaces and lines (18), by surfaces, lines, and points (26). Notice how this multiplication undermines a fixed count.
3
Consider the objection about circles and spheres
The passage mentions that circles and spheres are said to have no actual directions. Read the objection and the response that they do have actual extremities (circumference/surface).
4
Reflect on the implication for the specific view
Ask yourself: if directions can be counted in multiple ways even in a simple cube, how can the specific view claim a fixed set of six?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني الخاصي فلأنه ليس في الجسم بعد بالفعل لما مر من أنه ليس فيه عندنا إلا الأجزاء التي هي الجواهر المفردة والأبعاد المفروضة لا نهاية لها وعلى تقدير وجود البعد في الجسم فليس اعتبار التقاطع على قوائم أمراً واجباً في تحقق الجهات . وحينئذ نقول ففي المكعب وهو ما يحيط به سطوح ستة مربعات ستة وعشرون بعداً أي طرفا وجهة بحسب سطوحه الستة وخطوطه الاثني عشر ونقط زواياه الثماني. قال الإمام الرازي: لما كانت الأبعاد متناهية المقدار كما ستعرفه وجب أن يكون للامتداد الخطي طرفان هما جهتان له وللامتداد السطحي إذا كان مربعاً أطراف أربعة هي خطوطه المحيطة به، وإن اعتبرت النقط مع الخطوط كان أطرافه التي هي جهاته ثمانية وعلى هذا قياس المخمس والمسدس وغيرهما من السطوح والحال في الأجسام على قياس السطوح فللمكعب مثلاً سطوح ستة وخطوط اثنا عشر ونقط ثمان، فإن اعتبرت السطوح فقط كانت جهاته ستا، وإن اعتبرت معها الخطوط كانت ثماني عشرة، وإن اعتبرت معهما النقط كانت ستاً وعشرين قال ولا جهة بالفعل للدائرة والكرة وجهاتهما بالقوة غير متناهية ورد عليه بأن الدائرة لها طرف بالفعل هو المحيط بها وكذا للكرة طرف بالفعل هو سطحها المستدير المحيط بها فوجب أن الخط المستدير
قوله : ( اعتبار التقاطع على قوائم إلخ) وعلى تقدير اعتبار انحصار التقاطع على زوايا قوائم في أبعاد ثلاثة إنما هو إذا فرض امتداد واحد أصلاً، ووضع وضعاً من غير أن يكون الطبع موجبه فترتبت عليه المقاطعات بالقوائم ولو فرض مكان ذلك الامتداد الأول الواحد غيره مما ليس موازياً له لوقعت ثلاث مقاطعات أخرى على قوائم غير ذلك بالعدد ووقعت جهات غير ذلك بالعدد كذا الشفاء.
قوله : ( متناهية المقدار دون الوضع كالدائرة والكرة .
قوله : ( وخطوطه الاثني عشر هذا على اعتبار التداخل في الخطوط والنقط وإلا فالخطوط أربع وعشرون والنقط ثمانية وأربعون . قوله : (وجب أن يكون للامتداد الخطي طرفان أراد الامتداد الخطي الغير المستدير كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ورد عليه بأن الدائرة إلخ) فإن قلت : الدائرة قد تطلق على محيطها وقد صرح في بحث نفي الجزء من شرح المقاصد بإطلاق الكرة على محيطها أعني سطحها أيضاً. فحينئذ لا يرد الرد. قلت : الكلام الذي نقله الشارح عن الإمام نقلاً بالمعنى مذكور في الملخص وفي
As for the second, the specific, aspect: it is because there is no actual dimension in the body, according to what has passed that in our view there is nothing in it except the parts which are the simple atoms, and the assumed dimensions are infinite; and assuming the existence of dimension in the body, considering intersection at right angles is not necessary for the realization of directions. Then we say: in the cube (muk'aab), which is what is surrounded by six square surfaces, there are twenty-six dimensions (ab'ad), i.e., extremities and directions, according to its six surfaces, its twelve lines, and its eight corner points. Imam al-Razi said: Since the dimensions are finite in magnitude, as you will know, it is necessary that the linear extension has two extremities which are two directions for it, and for the surface extension, if it is square, there are four extremities which are its surrounding lines; and if the points are considered along with the lines, its extremities which are its directions are eight. And upon this is the analogy of the pentagon, hexagon, and other surfaces. And the situation for bodies is analogous to surfaces. For the cube, for example, there are six surfaces, twelve lines, and eight points. If only the surfaces are considered, its directions are six; if the lines are considered along with them, they become eighteen; if the points are considered along with both, they become twenty-six. He said: There is no actual direction for the circle and sphere; their directions in potentiality are infinite. It was objected to him that the circle has an actual extremity, which is its circumference; likewise the sphere has an actual extremity, which is its curved surface surrounding it; so it is necessary that the curved line... He says: (Considering intersection at right angles, etc.) And assuming the restriction of intersection to right angles in three dimensions only occurs if a single extension is assumed originally, placed in a position without nature necessitating it, then the intersections at right angles follow from it. And if instead of that first single extension another is assumed not parallel to it, three other intersections at right angles different in number would occur, and directions different in number would occur. This is from al-Shifa'. He says: (Finite in magnitude but not in position, like the circle and sphere.) He says: (And its twelve lines) this is according to considering overlap in lines and points; otherwise the lines are twenty-four and the points forty-eight. He says: (It is necessary that the linear extension has two extremities) he intends the non-curved linear extension, as is not hidden. He says: (And it was objected to him that the circle, etc.) For if you say: the circle may be applied to its circumference, and it has been stated in the discussion of negation of the part from Sharh al-Maqasid that the sphere is applied to its circumference, i.e., its surface. So then the objection does not stand. I say: the statement that the commentator transmitted from the Imam in paraphrase is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas and in...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage refutes the specific view that directions are determined by three dimensions intersecting at right angles. It first argues that there is no actual dimension in a body (only atoms), so assumed dimensions are infinite. Even if dimension existed, intersection at right angles is not necessary. Then it uses the cube example to show that if we consider surfaces, lines, and points, the cube has 26 directions, not 6—illustrating that directions multiply. It also cites Imam al-Razi on the linear and surface extensions. Then it addresses the objection that the circle and sphere have no actual directions, responding that they do have actual extremities (their circumference/surface), so they also have actual directions. The commentary clarifies details about lines, points, and the source of the objection.

Key Terms
بعد (bu'd)
مكعب (muk'aab)
زوايا قوائم (zawaya qawa'im)
جهات بالقوة (jihaat bi-al-quwwa)
دائرة (da'ira)
كرة (kura)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That directions are limited to six (up, down, left, right, front, back) for any object.
Correction: The cube example shows that when we count surfaces, lines, and points, there can be up to 26 directions. Directions are relative to how we define them.

Checkpoint
How many directions does a cube have when considering surfaces, lines, and points together?
A) 6
B) 18
C) 26
D) 12

Key Takeaway
Directions are not fixed in reality; they depend on what aspects of an object we choose to consider, so the specific view that they are limited to six is false.

When was the last time you mistook a conventional understanding (like 'six directions') for an absolute truth about Allah's creation? Ask yourself: how can I humbly hold my beliefs while remaining open to the possibility that Allah's reality is far more complex?
?
The passage shows that even a simple cube can have 26 directions if we consider its lines and points. What does this teach you about the limits of human categorization when trying to understand divine truths? Are there areas in your faith where you rigidly define something (like Allah's attributes) that might actually be more multifaceted? The Cube's Twenty-Six Directions
Al-Razi enumerates the actual directions of a cube as twenty-six.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi derives the twenty-six actual directions of a cube from its geometric extremities: surfaces, lines, and points.

Core Idea
A cube's twenty-six directions follow necessarily from its finite surfaces, lines, and points.

This passage shows how kalam uses geometry to reason about physical reality. Imam al-Razi demonstrates that the number of actual directions a cube has is not arbitrary but follows from the finite nature of its dimensions. By counting surfaces, lines, and corner points, we arrive at twenty-six directions. This illustrates the kalam method of basing claims on necessary structural properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice that the passage first states the conclusion, then quotes al-Razi's reasoning.
2
Identify the three geometric features
The cube has six surfaces, twelve lines, and eight points. Each contributes to the count of directions.
These correspond to faces, edges, and vertices.
3
Follow al-Razi's reasoning from line to square to cube
Al-Razi starts with a line (2 extremities), then a square (4 extremities as lines, 8 with points), then applies analogously to the cube.
The principle: finite extensions have actual extremities that are directions.
4
Verify the cumulative count
Count surfaces only: 6 directions. Add lines: +12 = 18. Add points: +8 = 26. This confirms the total.
The order of counting is important: surfaces first, then lines, then points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحينئذ نقول ففي المكعب وهو ما يحيط به سطوح ستة مربعات ستة وعشرون بعدا أي طرفا وجهة بحسب سطوحه الستة وخطوطه الاثني عشر ونقط زواياه الثماني. قال الإمام الرازي: لما كانت الأبعاد متناهية المقدار كما ستعرفه وجب أن يكون للامتداد الخطي طرفان هما جهتان له وللامتداد السطحي إذا كان مربعا أطراف أربعة هي خطوطه المحيطة به، وإن اعتبرت النقط مع الخطوط كان أطرافه التي هي جهاته ثمانية وعلى هذا قياس المخمس والمسدس وغيرهما من السطوح والحال في الأجسام على قياس السطوح فللمكعب مثلا سطوح ستة وخطوط اثنا عشر ونقط ثمان، فإن اعتبرت السطوح فقط كانت جهاته ستا، وإن اعتبرت معها الخطوط كانت ثماني عشرة، وإن اعتبرت معهما النقط كانت ستا وعشرين
And then we say: in the cube, which is surrounded by six square surfaces, there are twenty-six dimensions, i.e., extremities and directions, according to its six surfaces, its twelve lines, and its eight corner points. Imam al-Razi said: Since dimensions are finite in magnitude as you will come to know, it is necessary that the linear extension has two extremities, which are two directions for it; and for the surface extension, if it is square, it has four extremities, which are its encompassing lines; and if the points are considered together with the lines, its extremities which are its directions become eight; and the same applies to pentagons, hexagons, and other surfaces. The case for bodies is analogous to surfaces. For example, the cube has six surfaces, twelve lines, and eight points. If only the surfaces are considered, its directions are six; if the lines are considered along with them, they become eighteen; and if the points are considered along with both, they become twenty-six.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. It first states the conclusion: a cube has twenty-six directions (extremities) based on its six surfaces, twelve lines, and eight corner points. It then quotes Imam al-Razi's explanation. Al-Razi argues that because dimensions are finite, a linear extension necessarily has two extremities (directions). A square surface has four extremities (its bounding lines), and if the corner points are also considered, the extremities become eight. This pattern scales analogously for bodies: a cube has six surfaces (six directions), twelve lines (adding twelve makes eighteen), and eight points (adding eight makes twenty-six). The proof follows the principle that actual directions correspond to the actual extremities of finite extensions.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
بعد (dimension)
مكعب (cube)
امتداد خطي (linear extension)
امتداد سطحي (surface extension)
طرف (extremity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The number of directions of a cube is arbitrary or conventional.
Correction: Al-Razi shows it follows necessarily from the finite nature of dimensions and the actual geometric extremities.

Checkpoint

A) 6
B) 18
C) 26
D) 8

Key Takeaway
The actual directions of a body are determined by the number of finite extremities (surfaces, lines, points) that constitute its dimensions.

The Circle and Sphere Have One Actual Direction
Refutation of the claim that a circle and sphere have no actual direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a circle and sphere each have one actual direction, based on their actual extremity.

Core Idea
A round shape still has a definite direction toward its boundary.

In kalam discussions about direction (jiha), some argued that circles and spheres have no actual direction because they are round and symmetric. However, this passage presents a refutation using the concept of an actual extremity (taraf). Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the concise style of the objection and refutation.
2
Identify the two claims
Find the original claim: 'no actual direction, potential directions infinite.' Then find the refutation: 'circle has actual extremity, sphere has actual extremity.'
3
Understand key terms
Note the difference between actual direction (jiha bil-fi'l) and potential direction (jiha bil-quwwa). Actual extremity (taraf) is a concrete boundary.
These terms are central to kalam physics.
4
See why extremity implies one direction
The extremity is a real boundary, so toward that boundary there is one actual direction. This refutes the claim of no actual direction.
The refutation is straightforward: having an extremity gives direction.

Source Text

This passage presents an objection and its refutation. Someone claimed that circles and spheres lack actual direction, possessing only infinite potential directions. The refutation argues that both the circle and sphere have an actual extremity—the circle's circumference and the sphere's spherical surface—and therefore each has one actual direction (toward that extremity). The argument hinges on the principle that any bounded figure has at least one actual extremity, which constitutes an actual direction.

Distinguishing Absolute Directions from Direction in General
Answer to the objection that direction as a property of the body prevents movement toward it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between absolute directions and direction in general resolves the objection that a body's direction being inherent in it would prevent movement toward that direction.

Core Idea
Direction can be absolute (fixed) or general (inherent in a body) — both are needed for motion.

The objection raised here is a common one: if a direction is a property of the body itself, how can the body move toward that direction? The answer introduces a crucial distinction between two senses of 'direction': absolute directions that are the endpoints of motion, and 'direction in general' that refers to the extremities inherent in each body. Understanding this distinction clarifies how rectilinear motion is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Note the two parts of the objection: (1) movement toward a direction seems impossible if direction is in the body, (2) directions would change with rotation, contradicting fixed above/below.
2
Identify the answer's two components
Find how the author distinguishes between absolute directions (jihāt muṭlaqa) and 'direction in general' (muṭlaq al-jihāt). Understand each definition.
3
Trace the resolution
See how this distinction answers both parts of the objection: absolute directions are fixed endpoints, while direction in general refers to inherent extremities that can be ends of motion relative to each body.
4
Reflect on the significance
Understand why this distinction matters for the concept of direction in kalam and how it preserves both fixed directions and the possibility of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا الكلام يدل بصريحه على أنَّ جهة الجسم قائمة به فكيف يتصور حركة الجسم إلى الجهة للوصول إليها والقرب منها، كما سيأتي ذكره . وأيضاً يلزم من هذا أن تكون جميع جهات الجسم متبدلة وهو مناف لكون الفوق والتحت جهتين حقيقيتين على ما قال بل الحق أن الجهة الحقيقية فوق وتحت لا غير . قلنا : إنَّ لنا جهات مطلقة ومطلق الجهات، أما الجهات المطلقة فهي منتهى الإشارات ومقصد الحركات المستقيمة على ما ستقف عليه، وأما مطلق الجهات فيتناول الأطراف القائمة بكل جسم إذ يمكن اعتبار انتهاء الإشارة والحركة إليها وهي واقعة بإزاء الجهات المطلقة فتسمى بأسمائها
If it is said: This statement explicitly indicates that the direction of a body is subsistent in it; so how can the movement of a body toward a direction be conceived for the purpose of reaching it and becoming near to it, as will be mentioned later? Also, it follows from this that all directions of the body are changeable, which contradicts the fact that the above and below are two real directions, as has been said. Rather, the truth is that the real directions are only above and below. We say: For us there are absolute directions (jihāt muṭlaqa) and 'direction in general' (muṭlaq al-jihāt). As for absolute directions, they are the ultimate termini of indications and the goal of rectilinear motions, as you will come to know. As for 'direction in general', it encompasses the extremities subsistent in every body, since it is possible to consider the termination of indication and motion to them, and they are opposite to the absolute directions, so they are called by their names.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection has two parts: (1) If directions are properties of the body itself, movement toward a direction would be impossible because the direction is in the body; (2) directions would change as the body rotates, contradicting the fixed reality of above and below as real directions. The answer distinguishes between two senses: (a) absolute directions (jihāt muṭlaqa) — the ultimate ends of indications and rectilinear motions, which are fixed (like above and below relative to the cosmos); and (b) 'direction in general' (muṭlaq al-jihāt) — the extremities subsisting in each body, which can be considered as ends of indication and motion in relation to that body. These latter are named after the absolute directions by correspondence. This distinction resolves the objection: direction in the body is 'direction in general', while movement toward absolute directions is meaningful.

Key Terms
جهات مطلقة (absolute directions)
مطلق الجهات (direction in general)
فوق (above)
تحت (below)
حركة مستقيمة (rectilinear motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If direction is a property of the body, then movement toward that direction is impossible and directions would change when the body rotates.
Correction: By distinguishing absolute directions (fixed termini) from direction in general (inherent extremities), the objection is resolved: movement toward direction in general is possible relative to each body, while absolute directions remain fixed.

Checkpoint

C) Between real directions and imaginary directions.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between absolute directions (fixed endpoints) and direction in general (inherent extremities) is essential to understanding how motion toward a direction is possible.

Absolute Directions vs. The Absolute of Directions
Sanusi presents an objection and defines two types of direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute directions (jihāt muṭlaqa) and the absolute of directions (muṭlaq al-jihāt) and how this distinction resolves an objection about the changeability of directions.

Core Idea
Absolute directions are universal termini; the absolute of directions are relative extremities.

This passage addresses a key objection in physics and metaphysics: if each body has a single actual direction, how can it move toward that direction without contradiction? The response introduces two subtly different concepts of 'direction' that resolve the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that claims the previous statement leads to contradictions: direction as inherent attribute and changeability of directions.
Notice the two problems raised.
2
Identify the two definitions
In the response, find and note the definitions of 'absolute directions' and 'the absolute of directions'.
Contrast the scope: universal vs. bodily.
3
See how the objection is resolved
Understand how the distinction allows fixed reference directions (e.g., above/below) while accounting for changing bodily extremities.
The absolute directions are fixed; bodily directions are named after them.
4
Reflect on importance
Consider why this distinction matters for motion and spatial reasoning in classical cosmology.
It preserves the reality of directions while explaining motion.

المباحث المشرقية وليس فيهما ذكر الكرة بل الدائرة ولا وجه لحملها على محيطها، لأن أسلوب کتابه مانع عن ذلك ودل على أن مراده من الدائرة معناها المعروف أعني سطحاً يحيط به خط مستدير. قال في الملخص: السطح إن كان مربعاً واعتبرت نهاياته التي الخطوط كانت أربعة وإن اعتبر جميعها حتى النقط صارت ثمانية وإن كان مسدساً أو مسبعاً أو غير ذلك من المضلعات فله بحسب كل حد جهة لأنه لا معنى للجهة إلا الطرف والدائرة لا جهة لها بالفعل وأما بالقوة فجهاتها غير متناهية إذ لا نقطة أولى بها من غيرها والحال في الجسم كالحال في السطح هذه عبارته في الملخص وعلى هذا أسلوب كلامه في المباحث المشرقية، فليتأمل. قوله : (فهي منتهى الإشارات ومقصد الحركات المستقيمة فبالنظر إلى الأول قيل: إن جهة الفوق هي بحسب الفلك الأعظم لأنه منتهى الإشارات الحسية ومقطعها أو بالنظر إلى الثاني
The third observation - the third purpose: concerning inclination, which is leaning. Each of them has actually one direction. If it is said: This statement clearly indicates that the direction of the body is subsistent in it, so how can the body's motion toward the direction be conceived for reaching it and approaching it, as will be mentioned. Also, it follows from this that all directions of the body are changeable, which contradicts that above and below are two real directions, as he said, rather the truth is that the real direction is only above and below. We say: Indeed, we have absolute directions (jihāt muṭlaqa) and the absolute of directions (muṭlaq al-jihāt). As for the absolute directions, they are the limit of indications and the destination of straight motions, as you will come to know. As for the absolute of directions, it encompasses the extremities subsisting in every body, since it is possible to consider the termination of indication and motion to them, and they are opposite to the absolute directions, so they are called by their names. And we ruled that... His saying: (that the circle, etc.) in al-Shifa': As for the circle, it has only one actual direction. His saying: (this statement, etc.) means what I transmitted from the Imam, but the author's statement does not indicate that. His saying: (indicates explicitly, etc.) since he applied directions to the extremities of extensions. And if it were said: There is looseness in his statement, and the meaning is that they are determinants of directions, so the meaning of his saying: the two are two directions, they are determinants of two directions, and analogize to that, then he would not need in refuting it to divide directions into absolute directions and the absolute of directions. And what we say is indicated by the expression of al-Shifa' where he said: And if all types of the finite are considered, even to the angle, then it has eight directions: four to the lines and four to the angles. Perhaps in his saying 'explicitly' there is an indication of what we said. His saying: (that all directions of the body are changeable) because the extremities change with the change of the body's positions. His saying: (absolute directions) means that their consideration is not in relation to one body rather than another. His saying: (and the absolute of directions) means that it is a direction in general. His saying: (they are the limit of indications and the destination of motions) he indicated by the plural form the absence of specificity. His saying: (since it is possible to consider the termination of indication, etc.) so they are the termination of an indication and a motion occurring in the extension of that body. His speech in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya: In it there is no mention of the sphere, but rather the circle, and there is no justification for interpreting it as its circumference, because the style of his book prevents that and indicates that his intention from 'circle' is its well-known meaning, i.e., a surface surrounded by a circular line. He said in al-Mulakhkhas: The surface, if it is square and its terminations are considered as lines, they are four; and if all are considered, even the points, they become eight. And if it is hexagonal or heptagonal or other polygons, then according to every limit it has a direction, because there is no meaning of direction except the extremity. And the circle has no actual direction; but potentially its directions are infinite, since no point has priority over another. And the case for the body is like the case for the surface. This is his expression in al-Mulakhkhas, and on this is the style of his speech in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya. So let it be contemplated. His saying: (they are the limit of indications and the destination of straight motions) for with regard to the first it is said: Indeed, the direction of 'above' is according to the highest sphere because it is the limit of sensory indications and their termination, or with regard to the second.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an answer. The objector claims that the statement that each thing has one actual direction implies that direction is a subsistent attribute of the body, which would make motion toward that direction problematic, and also that all directions would change, contradicting the reality of above and below. The author responds by distinguishing between 'absolute directions' (jihāt muṭlaqa) and 'the absolute of directions' (muṭlaq al-jihāt). Absolute directions are the limits of indications and destinations of straight motions, not relative to any particular body. The absolute of directions refers to the extremities that subsist in each body, to which indication or motion can terminate; these are labeled according to the absolute directions they face. The commentary then explains specific phrases from the objection and answer, clarifying nuances, citing al-Shifa' and al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya, and discussing how the direction of 'above' is defined by the highest sphere as the termination of sensory indications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direction is a fixed attribute of every body, such that 'above' and 'below' are inherent and unchanging for all bodies.
Correction: The text distinguishes fixed absolute directions (like the universal 'above') from relative extremities that change with body orientation, thus resolving the objection. Only absolute directions are fixed; bodily directions vary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are 'absolute directions' (الجِهَاتُ المُطْلَقَة)?
A) The extremities of each body, considered in themselves
B) The limits of indications and destinations of straight motions
C) The two real directions: above and below
D) The directions that change with body positions

Key Takeaway
The distinction between absolute directions (universal termini) and the absolute of directions (relative bodily extremities) is essential to understanding motion and spatial reference in Islamic philosophy.

Why Only Above and Below Are Real Directions
The author proves that only above and below are real directions and addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs that above and below are the only real directions and how to resolve an objection about their relativity.

Core Idea
Above and below are absolute, not relative—they are rooted in natural inclination.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that above and below are not merely conventional but have a real basis in nature. He provides two arguments: one from the natural tendencies of elements, and another from invariability. Then he addresses a subtle objection involving two persons on opposite ends of the earth, clarifying that 'by nature' refers to the relation, not the body parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument: definition, two proofs, objection, answer.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the two reasons why above and below are real directions: (1) natural inclination of elements, (2) invariability of these directions even when the body is inverted or reclining.
The first proof is based on how different elements behave (fire rises, water falls). The second proof shows that inverting a person does not change what is above and below.
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if 'above' and 'below' are defined relative to head and foot, they would change when two persons stand on opposite ends of the earth. Identify the example and the apparent problem.
4
Grasp the answer
The author explains that 'by nature' is not a property of head and foot themselves but describes the natural relation between the body part and the direction. In the objection case, placing one person's foot where the other's head would be is an unnatural inversion, so it does not affect the absolute nature of above and below.
5
Connect to natural inclination
The passage ends by linking the two real directions to natural inclination (الاعتماد الطبيعي). Reflect on why this matters for understanding motion and orientation in the cosmos.

The third observation - the third purpose: concerning inclination, which is leaning. Above and below, meaning from among the absolute directions, are two real directions, because they are two directions naturally distinct. For some elemental bodies by nature seek above and flee from below, like fire and air, and some are opposite, like earth and water. Also, these two directions do not change at all. For if a standing person becomes inverted, what is next to his head does not become above and what is next to his foot below; rather his head becomes below and his foot above, and above and below remain as they were. And what is mentioned of the case of the reclining person does not remove above or below from being above or below; rather his face becomes toward above and his back toward below. Yes, above and below then become qualified by two other accidental descriptions, namely being front and back. As for the remaining directions, there is no natural distinction between them, and they change according to assumption, as previously. And it may be said: If above and below are interpreted as what is next to the sky and the earth, then change is not conceivable for them, unlike if they are interpreted as what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, for then they change, as when two persons stand on opposite ends of one diameter of the earth, for the head and foot of each are on the natural course, yet the side that is next to the head of one is next to the foot of the other, so that side is 'above' relative to the first and 'below' relative to the second. And it is answered: Our saying 'by nature' is not a description of the foot and head, rather it is attached to the mentioned action, and its meaning is that the head and foot of each person have a natural relation with the direction in closeness and proximity. There is no doubt that if we suppose the foot of one of these two persons to be where the head of the other is, it would not be on the natural course; rather that would be an inversion for him. And if it is established that the real directions are two, then natural inclination (al-iʿtimād al-ṭabīʿī) is also [two]. His saying: (not a description of the foot and head) such that it is a stable adverb taking the place of the state for them. His saying: (rather it is attached, etc.) i.e., an idle adverb indicating that nearness and proximity are natural. His saying: (and if it is established, etc.) is an explanation of the connection of his saying: 'and know' to his saying: 'rather the truth' with what preceded from the explanation of the rulings on inclination. It is said: it is the concavity of the sphere of the moon. And the first is correct, because if the indication passes beyond the sphere of the moon, it is definitely toward the direction of above, since it is proceeding from the direction of below heading toward what is opposite it. His saying: (since it is possible to consider the termination of indication and motion to them) for if you point to the extremity of a cube, such as one of its surfaces, your indication ends at it, and if we suppose a motion of a body in it, then when the mover penetrates it and moves, his motion extending from that surface penetrating into it ends at its opposite surface. His saying: (and its meaning that for the head of each person, etc.) It is said: The correct expression according to this interpretation is to say: what is next to a person's head and foot by nature, so let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage proves that only above (al-fawq) and below (al-taḥt) are real directions. The author provides two main proofs: (1) natural inclination: elemental bodies naturally seek above (like fire and air) or flee from it (like earth and water), showing a real distinction; (2) invariability: these directions do not change even when a person is inverted or reclining—above remains above, below remains below, though they may acquire accidental descriptions like front/back. Other directions are not naturally distinct and change with assumption. The passage then addresses an objection: if above and below are interpreted relative to the sky and earth, they are invariable, but if interpreted relative to a person's head and foot by nature, they seem to change (as in the example of two persons on opposite ends of the earth, where the same direction is above for one and below for the other). The answer clarifies that 'by nature' does not describe the head and foot themselves but rather the natural relation (naturally based proximity and distance) each has with the direction. In the example, if one person's foot were where the other's head is, that would be an inversion, not a natural situation. Thus, above and below remain absolute. The passage concludes that if real directions are two, then natural inclination (al-iʿtimād al-ṭabīʿī) is also two. The commentary explains the nuanced terms and connects this to previous discussions on inclination.

The Disagreement on I'timad: One Quality or Many?
Al-Jurjani reports the disagreement on i'timad and presents al-Qadi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions on the nature of i'timad and why al-Qadi Abu Bakr argued for a single quality with relational names.

Core Idea
Inclination is one reality; direction gives it different names.

The nature of i'timad (inclination) was a debated topic in kalam. Some argued that each direction has a distinct inclination, while others asserted a single quality with relational names. This passage introduces the disagreement and presents al-Qadi Abu Bakr's view that multiplicity is only in naming, not in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two groups
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two groups of scholars: one claiming i'timad in each direction is distinct, the other claiming it is one in each body. Mark their arguments.
2
Understand al-Qadi's position
Pay attention to al-Qadi Abu Bakr's view. He says the difference is only in naming. The same quality is called 'weight' when directed downward and 'lightness' when upward. This allows six inclinations to coexist in one body.
3
Consider the logical implications
Reflect on the consequence: if inclinations were multiple and contradictory, they could not coexist. The unified view avoids this problem. This shows how kalam arguments turn on logical consistency.
This connects to the learning objective.

۲۰۱
اثنان أعني الصاعد والهابط وما عداهما اعتمادات غير طبيعية ( وجعلها القاضي ) هذا قسيم لقوله : له أنواع بحسب أنواع الحركة أي وجعل القاضي الاعتمادات بحسب الجهات ) أمراً واحداً فقال : الاختلاف في التسمية ) فقط ( وهي كيفية واحدة ) بالحقيقة ( فسمى) تلك الكيفية الواحدة ( بالنسبة إلى السفل ثقلا وإلى العلو خفة ) وقس على ذلك حالها بالنسبة إلى سائر الجهات ( وقد يجتمع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد قال الآمدي : ( القائلون بالاعتماد من أصحابنا اختلفوا فقال : بعضهم . الاعتماد في كل جهة هو غير الاعتماد في جهة أخرى والاعتمادات إما متضادة أو اعتمادان في جسم واحد إلى جهتين إذ هما ضدان فلا يجتمعان ولا إلى جهة واحدة إذ هما مثلان فامتنع اجتماعهما أيضا وقال آخرون : الاعتماد في كل جسم واحد والتعدد في التسمية دون المسمى وعلى هذا يجوز اجتماع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد من غير تضاد وهو اختيار القاضي أبي بكر (و) هذا هو ) الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا القائلين بوجود الاعتماد (إذ لو قلنا بتضاد الاعتمادات ( المتفرع على تعددها كما ذهب إليه الطائفة الأولى (لما اجتمعت ) متماثلة فلا يتصور
قوله : (أمراً واحداً) أي بالنوع يتحقق في كل جسم واحد من أفراده فلا اجتماع للضدين ولا للمتماثلين وما قيل : إِنَّ المراد أنه واحد بالشخص فوهم لأنَّ الغرض يتعدد بحسب المحل فكيف يكون واحداً بالشخص في جميع الأجسام.
قوله : ( الاختلاف في التسمية) أي تسمية ذلك النوع بحسب الاعتبارات .
قوله : ( وقد يجتمع الاعتمادات الست المتخالفة بالاعتبار الواحدة بالذات .
قوله : ( وهو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا ) من القول بالتعدد لم يذكر الشارح قدس سره تلك الأصول وما وقفت عليها حتى يظهر وجه الأشبهية ولعل عند غيري بيانها .
النوعي
قوله : أمراً واحداً مقابلته بقوله : له أنواع يشعر بأنَّ المراد بالأمر الواحد الواحد بالنوع وإن تعدد الأشخاص وهو المفهوم من بعض كلامه أيضاً. فإنَّ التضاد إنما يتفرع على التعدد لا الشخصي وفيه أنه حينئذ يلزم اجتماع المثلين على تقدير اجتماع أفراد ذلك النوع. والحق كما هو المفهوم من قوله : الاختلاف في التسمية فقط أنَّ المراد الواحد بالشخص والوحدة الشخصية تستلزم انتفاء التعدد النوعي وبهذا الاعتبار تستقيم المقابلة تم الحكم بالأشبهية بالنظر إلى القول بالتعدد النوعي، فلا إشكال في حديث التفرع أيضاً فعلى هذا معنى قوله : وقد الاعتمادات الست جواز أن يعرض لذلك الأمر الشخصي الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافات إلى الجهات الست .
تجتمع
قوله: (وهذا هو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا ) نوقش في العبارة بأن الأشبهية تدل على صحة القول بتعدد الاعتمادات وتضادها في الجملة مع أنَّ من جملة الأصول اجتماعها، فلا يستقيم
The third observatory – the third purpose: on i'timad, which is inclination. Two: the ascending and the descending, and the rest are non-natural inclinations. (And al-Qadi made them) – this is a counterpart to his saying: it has types according to types of motion – that is, al-Qadi made the inclinations according to the directions a single thing, and said: the difference is only in naming (only), and it is a single quality (in reality). So he named that single quality, relative to the lower, weight, and relative to the upper, lightness. And analogize that to its state relative to the other directions. (And the six inclinations may coexist in one body). Al-Amidi said: those who affirm i'timad among our companions disagreed: some said i'timad in each direction is other than i'timad in another direction, and the inclinations are either contradictory, or two inclinations in one body toward two directions are two opposites and thus cannot coexist, nor toward one direction because they are similar, so their coexistence is impossible. Others said: i'timad in each body is one, and multiplicity is in naming not in the named; on this, the six inclinations may coexist in one body without contradiction, and this is the choice of al-Qadi Abu Bakr. (And) this is (the more consistent with the principles of our companions who affirm the existence of i'timad) (for if we affirmed the contradiction of inclinations (branching from their multiplicity as the first group went) (they would not coexist) being similar. His saying: (a single thing) – i.e., in type, it is realized in each body among its individuals, so there is no coexistence of two opposites nor of two similars. And what was said that it is intended to be one in person is an error, because the purpose multiplies according to the locus, so how could it be one in person in all bodies? His saying: (the difference is in naming) – i.e., naming that type according to considerations. His saying: (and the six inclinations may coexist) – differing by consideration, one in essence. His saying: (and this is the more consistent with the principles of our companions) – regarding the statement of multiplicity, the commentator did not mention those principles, and I have not come across them until the aspect of being more consistent appears; perhaps others have an explanation. Type-based. His saying: 'a single thing' being contrasted with his saying 'it has types' suggests that what is meant by 'a single thing' is one in type, even if individuals are multiple; this is understood from some of his statements. For contradiction branches only from multiplicity, not from personal unity. And there is an objection that then it would entail the coexistence of similars if individuals of that type were to coexist. The truth, as understood from his saying 'the difference is only in naming', is that what is meant is one in person, and personal unity entails the negation of type multiplicity, and with this consideration the contrast is sound, and the judgment of being more consistent is made with regard to the statement of type multiplicity, so there is no problem in the discussion of branching. On this, the meaning of his saying 'the six inclinations may coexist' is the possibility that that personal entity may have different considerations and relations to the six directions. His saying: (and this is the more consistent with the principles of our companions) – an objection was made to the phrasing, that 'more consistent' indicates the validity of the statement of multiplicity of inclinations and their contradiction in general, while among the principles is their coexistence, so it is not sound.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the nature of i'timad (inclination) – whether it is a single reality or multiple. The author begins by stating that only two inclinations are natural (ascending and descending); the rest are non-natural. He then reports the view of al-Qadi Abu Bakr, who made all inclinations a single thing, with differences only in naming: the same quality is called 'weight' when directed downward and 'lightness' when directed upward, and analogously for other directions. On this view, the six inclinations can coexist in one body as different relations of the same quality. The passage then cites al-Amidi's account of a disagreement among the proponents of i'timad. One group says i'timad in each direction is a distinct entity; these are either contradictory (if toward opposite directions) or similar (if toward the same), so they cannot coexist. Another group says i'timad is one in each body, with multiplicity only in name, so the six can coexist without contradiction; this is the Qadi's choice. The author then remarks that this view is more consistent with the school's principles, because if inclinations were multiple and contradictory, they could not coexist. The extensive commentary that follows discusses whether 'a single thing' means one in type or one in person, with the conclusion that the correct interpretation – aligning with the Qadi's view – is one in person, since personal unity avoids both contradiction and similarity issues.

Unity in Person vs. Unity in Type: Resolving the I'timad Debate
Al-Jurjani's commentary clarifies the nature of unity in i'timad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between unity in type and unity in person, and why the latter is the correct interpretation of al-Qadi's view.

Core Idea
Unity in person avoids both contradiction and similarity issues.

The commentary on al-Qadi's view delves into the precise meaning of 'a single thing'. Is it one in type (species) or one in person? The resolution affects whether inclinations can truly coexist. This passage examines the arguments, concluding that personal unity is the sound interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two kinds of unity
Read the passage carefully. Notice the distinction between 'one in type' (واحد بالنوع) and 'one in person' (واحد بالشخص). Understand what each means in this context.
2
Grasp the objection to type-unity
If i'timad is one in type but multiple in individuals, then two inclinations in the same body would be similar (مثلان) and cannot coexist. This is a logical problem. Find this argument in the text.
3
See why personal unity is preferred
The text argues that 'a single thing' in the Qadi's statement should be taken as one in person. This personal unity negates any multiplicity, allowing the six inclinations to be mere relations of the same entity. Follow the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل
۲۰۱
اثنان أعني الصاعد والهابط وما عداهما اعتمادات غير طبيعية ( وجعلها القاضي ) هذا قسيم لقوله : له أنواع بحسب أنواع الحركة أي وجعل القاضي الاعتمادات بحسب الجهات ) أمراً واحداً فقال : الاختلاف في التسمية ) فقط ( وهي كيفية واحدة ) بالحقيقة ( فسمى) تلك الكيفية الواحدة ( بالنسبة إلى السفل ثقلا وإلى العلو خفة ) وقس على ذلك حالها بالنسبة إلى سائر الجهات ( وقد يجتمع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد قال الآمدي : ( القائلون بالاعتماد من أصحابنا اختلفوا فقال : بعضهم . الاعتماد في كل جهة هو غير الاعتماد في جهة أخرى والاعتمادات إما متضادة أو اعتمادان في جسم واحد إلى جهتين إذ هما ضدان فلا يجتمعان ولا إلى جهة واحدة إذ هما مثلان فامتنع اجتماعهما أيضا وقال آخرون : الاعتماد في كل جسم واحد والتعدد في التسمية دون المسمى وعلى هذا يجوز اجتماع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد من غير تضاد وهو اختيار القاضي أبي بكر (و) هذا هو ) الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا القائلين بوجود الاعتماد (إذ لو قلنا بتضاد الاعتمادات ( المتفرع على تعددها كما ذهب إليه الطائفة الأولى (لما اجتمعت ) متماثلة فلا يتصور
قوله : (أمراً واحداً) أي بالنوع يتحقق في كل جسم واحد من أفراده فلا اجتماع للضدين ولا للمتماثلين وما قيل : إِنَّ المراد أنه واحد بالشخص فوهم لأنَّ الغرض يتعدد بحسب المحل فكيف يكون واحداً بالشخص في جميع الأجسام.
قوله : ( الاختلاف في التسمية) أي تسمية ذلك النوع بحسب الاعتبارات .
قوله : ( وقد يجتمع الاعتمادات الست المتخالفة بالاعتبار الواحدة بالذات .
قوله : ( وهو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا ) من القول بالتعدد لم يذكر الشارح قدس سره تلك الأصول وما وقفت عليها حتى يظهر وجه الأشبهية ولعل عند غيري بيانها .
النوعي
قوله : أمراً واحداً مقابلته بقوله : له أنواع يشعر بأنَّ المراد بالأمر الواحد الواحد بالنوع وإن تعدد الأشخاص وهو المفهوم من بعض كلامه أيضاً. فإنَّ التضاد إنما يتفرع على التعدد لا الشخصي وفيه أنه حينئذ يلزم اجتماع المثلين على تقدير اجتماع أفراد ذلك النوع. والحق كما هو المفهوم من قوله : الاختلاف في التسمية فقط أنَّ المراد الواحد بالشخص والوحدة الشخصية تستلزم انتفاء التعدد النوعي وبهذا الاعتبار تستقيم المقابلة تم الحكم بالأشبهية بالنظر إلى القول بالتعدد النوعي، فلا إشكال في حديث التفرع أيضاً فعلى هذا معنى قوله : وقد الاعتمادات الست جواز أن يعرض لذلك الأمر الشخصي الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافات إلى الجهات الست .
تجتمع
قوله: (وهذا هو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا ) نوقش في العبارة بأن الأشبهية تدل على صحة القول بتعدد الاعتمادات وتضادها في الجملة مع أنَّ من جملة الأصول اجتماعها، فلا يستقيم
The third observatory – the third purpose: on i'timad, which is inclination. Two: the ascending and the descending, and the rest are non-natural inclinations. (And al-Qadi made them) – this is a counterpart to his saying: it has types according to types of motion – that is, al-Qadi made the inclinations according to the directions a single thing, and said: the difference is only in naming (only), and it is a single quality (in reality). So he named that single quality, relative to the lower, weight, and relative to the upper, lightness. And analogize that to its state relative to the other directions. (And the six inclinations may coexist in one body). Al-Amidi said: those who affirm i'timad among our companions disagreed: some said i'timad in each direction is other than i'timad in another direction, and the inclinations are either contradictory, or two inclinations in one body toward two directions are two opposites and thus cannot coexist, nor toward one direction because they are similar, so their coexistence is impossible. Others said: i'timad in each body is one, and multiplicity is in naming not in the named; on this, the six inclinations may coexist in one body without contradiction, and this is the choice of al-Qadi Abu Bakr. (And) this is (the more consistent with the principles of our companions who affirm the existence of i'timad) (for if we affirmed the contradiction of inclinations (branching from their multiplicity as the first group went) (they would not coexist) being similar. His saying: (a single thing) – i.e., in type, it is realized in each body among its individuals, so there is no coexistence of two opposites nor of two similars. And what was said that it is intended to be one in person is an error, because the purpose multiplies according to the locus, so how could it be one in person in all bodies? His saying: (the difference is in naming) – i.e., naming that type according to considerations. His saying: (and the six inclinations may coexist) – differing by consideration, one in essence. His saying: (and this is the more consistent with the principles of our companions) – regarding the statement of multiplicity, the commentator did not mention those principles, and I have not come across them until the aspect of being more consistent appears; perhaps others have an explanation. Type-based. His saying: 'a single thing' being contrasted with his saying 'it has types' suggests that what is meant by 'a single thing' is one in type, even if individuals are multiple; this is understood from some of his statements. For contradiction branches only from multiplicity, not from personal unity. And there is an objection that then it would entail the coexistence of similars if individuals of that type were to coexist. The truth, as understood from his saying 'the difference is only in naming', is that what is meant is one in person, and personal unity entails the negation of type multiplicity, and with this consideration the contrast is sound, and the judgment of being more consistent is made with regard to the statement of type multiplicity, so there is no problem in the discussion of branching. On this, the meaning of his saying 'the six inclinations may coexist' is the possibility that that personal entity may have different considerations and relations to the six directions. His saying: (and this is the more consistent with the principles of our companions) – an objection was made to the phrasing, that 'more consistent' indicates the validity of the statement of multiplicity of inclinations and their contradiction in general, while among the principles is their coexistence, so it is not sound.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the nature of i'timad (inclination) – whether it is a single reality or multiple. The author begins by stating that only two inclinations are natural (ascending and descending); the rest are non-natural. He then reports the view of al-Qadi Abu Bakr, who made all inclinations a single thing, with differences only in naming: the same quality is called 'weight' when directed downward and 'lightness' when directed upward, and analogously for other directions. On this view, the six inclinations can coexist in one body as different relations of the same quality. The passage then cites al-Amidi's account of a disagreement among the proponents of i'timad. One group says i'timad in each direction is a distinct entity; these are either contradictory (if toward opposite directions) or similar (if toward the same), so they cannot coexist. Another group says i'timad is one in each body, with multiplicity only in name, so the six can coexist without contradiction; this is the Qadi's choice. The author then remarks that this view is more consistent with the school's principles, because if inclinations were multiple and contradictory, they could not coexist. The extensive commentary that follows discusses whether 'a single thing' means one in type or one in person, with the conclusion that the correct interpretation – aligning with the Qadi's view – is one in person, since personal unity avoids both contradiction and similarity issues.

Key Terms

واحد بالنوع
التضاد
المثلان

Checkpoint
What problem arises if i'timad is interpreted as one in type (واحد بالنوع)?
A) It cannot account for different directions.
B) If two instances exist in one body, they would be similar and cannot coexist.
C) It contradicts al-Qadi's statement about naming.
D) It requires the inclinations to be contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The correct interpretation of 'a single thing' is personal unity, which avoids both contradiction and similarity issues, allowing the six inclinations to coexist as relations of one entity.

The Unity of the Six Directional Tendencies
Presents the two views on i'timādāt and the Qadi's choice of unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among Ashʿarī scholars on the six directional tendencies (i'timādāt) and why al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr's view that they are one in type is considered more consistent with Ashʿarī principles.

Core Idea
The six tendencies are one reality, differing only by direction.

This passage presents a key debate within the Ashʿarī tradition over the nature of directional tendencies (i'timādāt). Al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr argues that the six tendencies are a single quality, named differently by direction, while others maintain they are distinct. The commentary explains the meaning of 'one thing' and addresses an objection to the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms: i'timād, ṣāʿid, hābiṭ, taḍād, tamāthul.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two views
Find the two positions: 1) Tendencies are distinct in each direction, either opposite or similar, causing impossibility of coexistence. 2) Tendency is one per body, multiplicity in name only, allowing all six to coexist.
3
Understand al-Qāḍī's position
Al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr holds that the six tendencies are one quality (kayfiyya wāḥida) in reality, differing only in terminology based on direction (e.g., heaviness downward, lightness upward).
His view is the second one mentioned.
4
Clarify 'one thing'
The commentary clarifies that 'one thing' means one in type (nawʿ), not one in individual (shakhṣ). This avoids the error of thinking the tendencies are numerically identical.
5
Examine the objection
The phrase 'more in accordance' is objected to because it seems to imply that the view of multiplicity and opposition is still correct, whereas the school's principles require coexistence of all six.
Reflect on how the objection is addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل. اثنان أعني الصاعد والهابط وما عداهما اعتمادات غير طبيعية. وجعلها القاضي أمراً واحداً فقال: الاختلاف في التسمية فقط وهي كيفية واحدة بالحقيقة. فسمى تلك الكيفية الواحدة بالنسبة إلى السفل ثقلا وإلى العلو خفة وقس على ذلك حالها بالنسبة إلى سائر الجهات. وقد يجتمع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد. قال الآمدي: القائلون بالاعتماد من أصحابنا اختلفوا فقال بعضهم: الاعتماد في كل جهة هو غير الاعتماد في جهة أخرى والاعتمادات إما متضادة أو اعتمادان في جسم واحد إلى جهتين إذ هما ضدان فلا يجتمعان ولا إلى جهة واحدة إذ هما مثلان فامتنع اجتماعهما أيضا. وقال آخرون: الاعتماد في كل جسم واحد والتعدد في التسمية دون المسمى وعلى هذا يجوز اجتماع الاعتمادات الست في جسم واحد من غير تضاد وهو اختيار القاضي أبي بكر. وهذا هو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا القائلين بوجود الاعتماد. قوله: أمراً واحداً أي بالنوع. وقيل إن المراد أنه واحد بالشخص فوهم. قوله: الاختلاف في التسمية أي تسمية ذلك النوع بحسب الاعتبارات. قوله: (وهو الأشبه بأصول أصحابنا) نوقش في العبارة بأن الأشبهية تدل على صحة القول بتعدد الاعتمادات وتضادها في الجملة مع أن من جملة الأصول اجتماعها، فلا يستقيم.
The Third Observatory - The Third Objective: On inclination, which is the tendency. Two, namely the upward and the downward, and what is other than them are non-natural tendencies. And the Judge (al-Qāḍī) made them one thing, so he said: the difference is only in the name, while it is one quality in reality. So he named that one quality with respect to downward as heaviness and with respect to upward as lightness, and similarly for the other directions. And the six tendencies may be combined in one body. Al-Āmidī said: Those who affirm tendency among our companions differed: some said the tendency in each direction is different from the tendency in another direction, and tendencies are either opposite, or two tendencies in one body to two directions are impossible because they would be opposites, and to one direction also impossible because they would be similar. Others said: the tendency in every body is one, and the multiplicity is in the name, not the named, and on this it is permissible for the six tendencies to be combined in one body without opposition, and this is the choice of Judge Abū Bakr. And this is more in accordance with the principles of our companions who affirm tendency. His statement: 'one thing' meaning one in type. And it was said that it means one in individual, which is a mistake. His statement: 'the difference in naming' means naming that type according to considerations. His statement: 'and this is more in accordance with the principles of our companions' – the expression is objected to because 'more in accordance' implies the correctness of the view of multiplicity and opposition in general, whereas among those principles is their conjunction, so it is not consistent.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the two main positions among Ash'arī scholars on the nature of the six directional tendencies (i'timādāt: up, down, front, back, right, left). The Judge (al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī) holds that they are one reality called by different names depending on the direction, so they can coexist in one body. Others hold that each direction's tendency is a distinct entity, and they are either opposites (so two cannot coexist to opposite directions) or similars (so two cannot coexist to the same direction). The commentator then explains that the Qadi's 'one thing' means one in type (naw'), not one in individual (shakhṣ), and argues that this is more consistent with the principles of the Ash'arī school. A possible objection is raised: the claim 'more consistent' seems to imply that multiplicity with opposition is still a possible position, whereas the school's principles actually require that the six tendencies can coexist, which militates against opposition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'one thing' means the tendencies are numerically identical (one in individual).
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'one thing' means one in type (one in definition/essence), not one in individual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr regarding the six i'timādāt?
A) They are six distinct opposites.
B) They are one in reality, differing only in name.
C) They are one in name but multiple in reality.
D) They cannot coexist in one body.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr's position that the six directional tendencies are one in type (not individually distinct) is considered more consistent with Ashʿarī principles, as it allows all six to coexist in a single body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr saw the six tendencies as one reality expressed in different forms. Look at your own acts of worship — salāh, zakāh, fasting, hajj. Do you approach them as separate duties or as manifestations of one submissive heart? How would your practice change if you saw them as one single quality of taqwā showing up in different relationships?
?
The passage includes an objection about careless wording — saying 'more in accordance' when the truth is absolute. Think of a time you softened your speech about a clear matter of faith to avoid conflict. How can you speak with complete conviction without arrogance, and what is one concrete step you can take today to correct a hesitant statement you made?
?
This lesson is about unity underlying apparent multiplicity — a deep echo of tawḥīd. Where in your life do you still treat Allah's commands as fragmented, picking and choosing based on convenience? What would it mean for you to live as though all of life's directions are ultimately one call from one Lord?

The Three Positions on I'timādāt
Summary of the three scholarly views on i'timādāt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on the nature of i'timādāt (directional tendencies) as classified in the text.

Core Idea
I'timādāt are classified into three distinct positions: union, multiplicity with opposition, and multiplicity without opposition.

After an extensive discussion on the nature of directional tendencies (i'timādāt), the author condenses the debate into three core positions. This passage serves as a classificatory summary, presenting the outcomes in a clear and structured manner. Understanding these three positions is essential for grasping the theological disagreements surrounding the nature of created attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage is a concise summary following a prior debate.
The passage is very short; focus on the three terms: الاتحاد، والتعدد مع التضاد، وبدونه.
2
Identify each position
Identify the three positions: union (ittiḥād), multiplicity with opposition (ta'addud ma'a taḍād), and multiplicity without opposition (ta'addud bidūn taḍād). Consider what each means in terms of whether tendencies are one or many, and whether they oppose each other.
The key terms are اتحاد, تعدد, تضاد.
3
Reflect on the classification
Ask yourself why the author presents these as the three and only possibilities. The classification is exhaustive: either all tendencies are one, or they are many, and if many, they either have opposition or not.
This structure is typical of kalam taxonomies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل. فصارت الأقوال في الاعتمادات ثلاثة: الاتحاد، والتعدد مع التضاد، وبدونه.
The Third Observatory - The Third Objective: On inclination, which is the tendency. So the opinions concerning the tendencies became three: union, multiplicity with opposition, and multiplicity without opposition.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a concise classification of the three positions on the i'timādāt (directional tendencies) that have been discussed in the preceding arguments. The first position is 'ittiḥād' (union), which is the view that all directional tendencies are one in reality and differ only in name. The second is 'ta'addud ma'a taḍād' (multiplicity with opposition), which holds that each direction has a distinct tendency and those in opposite directions are opposites and cannot coexist. The third is 'ta'addud bidūn taḍād' (multiplicity without opposition), which holds that there are distinct tendencies but they are not opposites, so they can coexist. This summary encapsulates the doctrinal outcome of the debate.

Key Terms
اتحاد
تعدد
تضاد
اعتماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'multiplicity without opposition' is the same as 'union'.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: union posits a single reality, while multiplicity without opposition holds multiple distinct tendencies that are not opposites.

Checkpoint
How many total positions on i'timādāt are enumerated in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The debate on i'timādāt reduces to three positions: union, multiplicity with opposition, and multiplicity without opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own internal inclinations—toward worship, procrastination, anger, or compassion—do you experience them as a unified drive with varied expressions, as warring opposites, or as independent forces that sometimes align? Which of the three scholarly positions best captures your daily struggle, and what does that reveal about your need for Allah's guidance?
?
The scholars distinguished between tendencies that literally oppose each other and those that merely differ. Consider a moment when you felt torn between two good deeds. Were they truly in conflict, or was the conflict only in your limited capacity? How might seeing them as 'multiplicity without opposition' change how you prioritize your actions for the sake of Allah?

The Three Types of Inclination: Natural, Compulsory, and Psychic
Al-Ījī presents the philosopher’s tripartite classification of inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers classify inclination (mayl) into three types based on the source of the inclination.

Core Idea
Inclination is classified by its cause: external (compulsory), internal with will (psychic), or internal without will (natural).

Al-Ījī begins his discussion of inclination by presenting the philosopher's division. This classification is foundational for understanding motion and change in natural philosophy. The key criterion is whether the cause of inclination is external to the locus or not, and if internal, whether it is accompanied by will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic classification passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the clear tripartite structure.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three types of inclination. Identify each: compulsory (qasrī), psychic (nafsānī), and natural (tabī‘ī). Note the criterion used for each.
The criterion is based on the cause: external vs. internal, and if internal, with or without will.
3
Match examples to categories
The passage gives examples: stone thrown upward (compulsory), human voluntary movement (psychic), stone falling naturally (natural). Confirm you can classify other examples mentally.
4
Reflect on the philosophical significance
Ask yourself why this classification is important. It provides a framework for understanding all motion and change in the physical world according to the philosophers.
This classification is used in later discussions of causality and natural philosophy.

الخشبة المجذوبة ولا يخفى بعده على أنَّ شارح حكمة العين صرح في بحث أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً بأن القاسم امتناع الخلاء فحينئذ يحتاج في دفع الاشكال إلى تعميم الامتياز في الوضع كما أشرنا إليه والله أعلم فإن قلت : الميل الموجود في الأفلاك الثمانية بالنسبة إلى حركاتها العرضية بواسطة المحدد يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل وهو المحدد مع أنهم قالوا : لا قاسر في الأفلاك . قلت : لا نسلم وجود الميل فيها بالنسبة إلى تلك الحركات فإنَّ المراد بالميل هاهنا هو المبدأ القريب للحركات الذاتية أعني المقابلة للحركات العرضية ولا وجود له فيما ذكر . قوله : ( وصادر عن الإرادة فيه تنبيه على أن مجرد المقارنة بالإرادة لا يكفي فيه إذ ليس
Its fifth: The philosopher calls dependence inclination, and divides it into three divisions: natural, compulsory, and psychic. Because it (i.e., inclination) is either due to a cause external to the locus, i.e., due to something distinct from the locus of inclination in position and indication, and it is compulsory inclination, like the inclination of a stone thrown upward; or it is not due to an external cause, and then it is either accompanied by sensation and emanating from will, and it is psychic inclination, like the inclination of a human in his voluntary movement; or it is not, and it is natural inclination, like the natural inclination of a stone. His saying: (The sense contradicts it, and what is in Sharh al-Maqāsid that it is permissible that it is not sensed due to its smallness with extreme mixing with water parts, is something that the intellect contradicts. For how can it sense water parts when the contact between them is extremely small and the distance between them is ten times that?) Together with his saying: (The sense contradicts it. It is said: it can be said that it is not sensed due to extreme smallness with extreme mixing with water parts, and its remoteness is not hidden.) His saying: (and it is compulsory inclination) contains discussion: because when the stone moves upward by the will of the determiner, its upward inclination is compulsory, yet it cannot be described as being due to a cause external to the locus in the sense of distinction in position. And generalizing distinction in position to the extent that the locus of the inclination, when placed without its external cause, is contrary to the apparent. Also, the movement of water upward when sucking a bottle strongly or pressing it on water is compulsory, and the compeller is the impossibility of the void, although it has no position at all. The answer to the first: the division is according to the doctrine of the philosopher, and they do not affirm the determiner (exalted is His station above what they say). And as for the second: the compeller is the nature of air necessitating the contiguity of its surface with the surface of water due to the necessity of the absence of void, but this does not avail in the case of the sprinklers that the commentator mentioned in the discussion of the void, so the matter is problematic. Unless the compeller there is considered to be the pulled wood, and its remoteness is not hidden. And that the commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn explicitly stated in a discussion that between every two movements there is a rest, that the divider is the impossibility of the void. Then in repelling the problem, it is necessary to generalize distinction in position as we indicated, and God knows best. If you say: The inclination existing in the eight spheres with respect to their transverse movements via the determiner applies to it that it is due to an external cause, namely the determiner, although they said: there is no compeller in the spheres. I say: We do not affirm the existence of inclination in them with respect to those movements, for what is meant by inclination here is the proximate principle of essential movements, i.e., opposite to transverse movements, and it does not exist in what was mentioned. His saying: (and emanating from will) contains a warning that mere conjunction with will is not sufficient in it, since it is not...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth point (خامسها) which provides the philosopher's classification of dependence (اعتماد) as inclination (ميل). The classification is tripartite: natural (طبيعي), compulsory (قسري), and psychic (نفساني). The criterion is whether the cause of the inclination is external to the locus or not. If external, it is compulsory (e.g., a stone thrown upward has an inclination contrary to its natural one due to an external force). If not external, it is either accompanied by sensation and will (psychic, e.g., voluntary human movement) or not (natural, e.g., a stone's natural downward inclination). The text then includes commentary labeled 'قوله' that discusses objections. The first two 'قوله' passages comment on a previous statement about the sense contradicting a claim about void; these are asides. The third 'قوله' ('وهو الميل القسري') raises an objection: if a stone moves upward by God's will, its inclination is compulsory but the cause is not 'external' in the sense of being a distinct object. The answer is that the division is according to philosophers who deny a determiner. Another objection about water rising due to void avoidance is answered by appealing to the nature of air, but the commentator notes difficulties with certain cases (e.g., sprinklers). Further objections about heavenly spheres are addressed by distinguishing between essential and transverse movements. The passage ends with a comment on the phrase 'emanating from will', emphasizing that mere conjunction with will is insufficient.

Key Terms
ميل
اعتماد
ميل طبيعي
ميل قسري
ميل نفساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inclination is only about direction (up/down).
Correction: The classification is about the source of inclination, not its direction. A stone thrown upward has compulsory inclination, not natural upward inclination.

Checkpoint
According to the philosopher's classification in this passage, what determines whether an inclination is classified as compulsory (qasrī)?
A) Whether it is accompanied by sensation and will.
B) Whether it is caused by an external factor distinct from the locus.
C) Whether it is directed upward.
D) Whether it is found in animate beings.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher defines three types of inclination: compulsory (external cause), psychic (internal with will), and natural (internal without will).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of your recent actions. Are they driven more by external pressures (compulsory), your own desires (psychic), or ingrained habits (natural)? Which type dominates your daily life, and what does that reveal about your spiritual state? Will you commit to increasing psychic inclination toward actions that please Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes psychic inclination (with will) from natural (without will). Are you aware of moments when your actions are automatic and unthinking, like natural inclinations? How can you bring more conscious will and intention into your worship and dealings?
?
If all your inward inclinations were laid bare, would you find that your psychic inclinations align with what is true and good, or are they often at odds? What one step can you take today to redirect your psychic inclinations toward the path of righteousness?

Objections to the External Cause Criterion for Compulsory Inclination
Al-Ījī discusses objections to the definition of compulsory inclination and the responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the definition of compulsory inclination as requiring an external cause distinct in position, and the responses given.

Core Idea
Compulsory inclination seems to occur even when the external cause is not spatially distinct, challenging the classification criterion.

After presenting the classification, al-Ījī discusses objections to the idea that compulsory inclination always involves an external cause distinct in position. The first objection concerns a stone moved by God's will, and the second involves water rising due to void avoidance. These objections test the limits of the philosopher's definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about divine action
Read the first part where the objector argues that a stone moved by God's will has compulsory inclination, but God is not spatially distinct from the stone. Understand the problem.
The objector is pointing out that the philosopher's definition fails if God is the mover.
2
Read the answer to the first objection
Read the answer: the division is according to the philosophers' doctrine, which denies a voluntary agent (God). So the objection is not relevant within their framework.
This answer shows that the classification is context-dependent.
3
Read the objection about water rising
Read the second objection: water rising due to void avoidance has a compeller (impossibility of void) that has no position, so again the external cause is not spatially distinct.
4
Analyze the answer and its limitations
Read the answer that the compeller is the nature of air, but note the commentator's remark about sprinklers and the need to generalize 'distinction in position'. Consider why the answer is not fully satisfactory.
This shows the complexity of applying the classification to all physical phenomena.
