Commentary
This passage begins the fifth point (خامسها) which provides the philosopher's classification of dependence (اعتماد) as inclination (ميل). The classification is tripartite: natural (طبيعي), compulsory (قسري), and psychic (نفساني). The criterion is whether the cause of the inclination is external to the locus or not. If external, it is compulsory (e.g., a stone thrown upward has an inclination contrary to its natural one due to an external force). If not external, it is either accompanied by sensation and will (psychic, e.g., voluntary human movement) or not (natural, e.g., a stone's natural downward inclination). The text then includes commentary labeled 'قوله' that discusses objections. The first two 'قوله' passages comment on a previous statement about the sense contradicting a claim about void; these are asides. The third 'قوله' ('وهو الميل القسري') raises an objection: if a stone moves upward by God's will, its inclination is compulsory but the cause is not 'external' in the sense of being a distinct object. The answer is that the division is according to philosophers who deny a determiner. Another objection about water rising due to void avoidance is answered by appealing to the nature of air, but the commentator notes difficulties with certain cases (e.g., sprinklers). Further objections about heavenly spheres are addressed by distinguishing between essential and transverse movements. The passage ends with a comment on the phrase 'emanating from will', emphasizing that mere conjunction with will is insufficient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compulsory inclination always involves a visible external object as the cause.
Correction: The cause can be non-spatial, like the impossibility of void, or the will of God (though philosophers deny that).

Checkpoint
What is the first objection raised against the definition of compulsory inclination as requiring an external cause distinct in position?
A) The void has no position, so it cannot be a cause.
B) A stone moved by God's will has compulsory inclination but God is not spatially distinct.
C) The stone moves upward naturally.
D) Water rising due to suction shows inclination is not compulsory.

Key Takeaway
The examples of divine action and void avoidance show that 'external cause' must be interpreted flexibly to include non-spatial causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials, do you automatically think of impersonal causes (e.g., 'it's just random') rather than recognizing Allah's direct hand? How can you shift your perspective to see His will as the primary cause in your life? Commit to pausing before reacting to a difficulty today and saying, 'This is from Allah.'
?
The passage shows that even a definition based on philosophy can be challenged by deeper reflection. In your faith, are there areas where you have settled for comfortable explanations rather than grappling with difficult intellectual questions? Will you take one such question to a scholar or a trustworthy source this week?
?
The commentator ends with 'God knows best.' When you encounter ambiguity in religious knowledge, do you humbly accept that Allah's knowledge is greater, or do you feel anxious for a complete answer? How can you cultivate contentment with divine mystery? Resolve to recite 'God knows best' sincerely when you next face an unanswered question.

Compulsory Inclination in Heavenly Spheres and Clarification on Psychic Inclination
Al-Ījī addresses an objection about heavenly spheres and clarifies the condition for psychic inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why heavenly spheres are not considered to have compulsory inclination despite their motion being caused by an external determiner, and what constitutes psychic inclination.

Core Idea
Heavenly spheres have essential movements, not compulsory ones, and psychic inclination requires more than mere conjunction with will.

The passage concludes with an objection about the heavenly spheres: their transverse movements are caused by a determiner (al-muhaddid), which seems like an external cause, yet philosophers deny a compeller in the heavens. Al-Ījī clarifies by distinguishing essential from transverse movements. Finally, a note on psychic inclination emphasizes that it must emanate from will, not just be accompanied by it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about heavenly spheres
Read the objection: the spheres have transverse movements caused by a determiner (muhaddid), which appears to be an external cause, so why is this not compulsory?
2
Read the answer distinguishing movements
Read the answer: inclination here refers only to essential movements (the proximate principle of essential motion), not transverse movements. The spheres do not have inclination for transverse motions.
This distinction is crucial to understanding the classification's scope.
3
Read the note on psychic inclination
Read the final part: 'and emanating from will' contains a warning that mere conjunction with will is not sufficient; the inclination must actually emanate from will.
This clarifies that psychic inclination requires active will, not just any association with will.

Because it (i.e., inclination) is either due to a cause external to the locus, i.e., due to something distinct from the locus of inclination in position and indication, and it is compulsory inclination, like the inclination of a stone thrown upward; or it is not due to an external cause, and then it is either accompanied by sensation and emanating from will, and it is psychic inclination, like the inclination of a human in his voluntary movement; or it is not, and it is natural inclination, like the natural inclination of a stone. Then in repelling the problem, it is necessary to generalize distinction in position as we indicated, and God knows best. If you say: The inclination existing in the eight spheres with respect to their transverse movements via the determiner applies to it that it is due to an external cause, namely the determiner, although they said: there is no compeller in the spheres. I say: We do not affirm the existence of inclination in them with respect to those movements, for what is meant by inclination here is the proximate principle of essential movements, i.e., opposite to transverse movements, and it does not exist in what was mentioned. His saying: (and emanating from will) contains a warning that mere conjunction with will is not sufficient in it, since it is not...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth point (خامسها) which provides the philosopher's classification of dependence (اعتماد) as inclination (ميل). The classification is tripartite: natural (طبيعي), compulsory (قسري), and psychic (نفساني). The criterion is whether the cause of the inclination is external to the locus or not. If external, it is compulsory (e.g., a stone thrown upward has an inclination contrary to its natural one due to an external force). If not external, it is either accompanied by sensation and will (psychic, e.g., voluntary human movement) or not (natural, e.g., a stone's natural downward inclination). The text then includes commentary labeled 'قوله' that discusses objections. The first two 'قوله' passages comment on a previous statement about the sense contradicting a claim about void; these are asides. The third 'قوله' ('وهو الميل القسري') raises an objection: if a stone moves upward by God's will, its inclination is compulsory but the cause is not 'external' in the sense of being a distinct object. The answer is that the division is according to philosophers who deny a determiner. Another objection about water rising due to void avoidance is answered by appealing to the nature of air, but the commentator notes difficulties with certain cases (e.g., sprinklers). Further objections about heavenly spheres are addressed by distinguishing between essential and transverse movements. The passage ends with a comment on the phrase 'emanating from will', emphasizing that mere conjunction with will is insufficient.

Key Terms
ميل
حركات عرضية
حركات ذاتية
مبدأ قريب
المحدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All movements caused by something external are compulsory inclinations.
Correction: Compulsory inclination applies only to essential movements; transverse movements of spheres are not considered inclination at all.

Checkpoint
Why do the heavenly spheres not have compulsory inclination according to this passage?
A) Because they move by their own nature.
B) Because their transverse movements are not considered essential movements, and inclination here means proximate principle of essential movements.
C) Because the determiner is not really external.
D) Because they have no inclination at all.

Key Takeaway
Inclination in this context is specific to essential movements; heavenly spheres' transverse motions are not considered inclinations, and psychic inclination requires emanating from will, not just accompanying it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever stop to ask whether they truly emanate from your will or are merely coinciding with it? How would your practice change if you ensured every prayer and charity sprang from a deliberate intention, not just habit?
?
The passage warns that mere conjunction with will is insufficient for psychic inclination. Consider your own inclinations: are there areas in your life where you go through the motions without the movement truly coming from your will? What would it take to transform those actions into genuine expressions of your desire for Allah?
?
Heavenly spheres move without compulsion, yet their movements are fixed. In contrast, your will can choose. If your spiritual inclinations were examined today, would they resemble the fixed, essential movements of the spheres, or are they chaotic and externally driven? Write down one intention you will set tonight to ensure tomorrow's actions are truly your own.

Definition of I‘timad as Mayl and Its Three Divisions
Sanusi defines i‘timad as mayl and classifies it into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers define i‘timad as mayl (inclination) and distinguish its three types: natural, forced, and psychic.

Core Idea
I‘timad is mayl, and mayl is either externally caused or not, with or without consciousness.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the fifth proof from the philosophers: they define i‘timad (dependence) as mayl (inclination) and then classify mayl into three categories based on the origin of the movement. This classification is essential for understanding how physical and voluntary motions are explained in kalam and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise definition and the threefold division.
The passage begins with 'خامسها' indicating this is the fifth proof.
2
Identify the three types of mayl
Look for the three types: natural (طبيعي), forced (قسري), and psychic (نفساني). Note the criteria used to distinguish them: external cause and presence of consciousness.
3
Examine the examples
For forced: stone thrown upward. For psychic: voluntary human motion. For natural: stone falling by nature. Understand why each fits its category.
4
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Ask yourself why the philosophers needed this classification. It helps explain different types of motion in terms of causation and will.
This classification will be used in later arguments about the nature of i‘timad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها: الحكيم يسمي الاعتماد ميلاً، ويقسمه إلى ثلاثة أقسام طبيعي وقسري ونفساني لأنّه ) أي الميل (إما ) أن يكون بسبب خارج عن المحل أي بسبب ممتاز عن محل الميل في الوضع والإشارة (وهو) الميل ( القسري ) كميل الحجر المرمي إلى فوق ( أو لا يكون بسبب خارج (فإما مقرون بالشعور) وصادر عن الإرادة (وهو) الميل (النفساني) كميل الإنسان في حركته الإرادية ) أولا) وهو ( الميل ( الطبيعي ) كميل الحجر بطبعه
Fifth: The sage calls i‘timad (dependence) mayl (inclination), and divides it into three types: natural, forced, and psychic. Because it (i.e., inclination) is either due to a cause external to the locus, i.e., due to something distinct from the locus of inclination in position and indication—and it is forced inclination, like the inclination of a stone thrown upward—or it is not due to an external cause, and then it is either accompanied by sensation and issuing from will—and it is psychic inclination, like the inclination of a person in his voluntary motion—or it is not, and it is natural inclination, like the inclination of a stone by its nature.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. Sanusi (the author of the text being commented on) states that the philosophers define i‘timad (dependence) as mayl (inclination). Then he divides mayl into three categories based on its cause. First, forced inclination (al-qasrī): it arises from a cause external to the moving body, where the cause is distinct from the body in position and indication; example: a stone thrown upward. Second, psychic inclination (al-nafsānī): it arises without an external cause but is accompanied by consciousness and will; example: a person moving voluntarily. Third, natural inclination (al-ṭabī‘ī): it arises without an external cause and without consciousness or will; example: a stone falling downward by its nature. The passage thus establishes a threefold classification that will be used in subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
الاعتماد (i‘timad)
الميل (mayl)
طبيعي (natural)
قسري (forced)
نفساني (psychic)
المحل (locus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that i‘timad is only physical inclination, excluding psychic or forced motions.
Correction: The philosophers define i‘timad broadly as mayl, which includes forced, psychic, and natural inclinations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes forced inclination from natural inclination?
A) The presence of consciousness
B) The cause being external to the locus
C) The speed of motion
D) The direction of motion

Key Takeaway
The philosophers classify inclination into three types based on whether its cause is external and whether it involves consciousness and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily actions, how many are driven by external pressures (forced inclination) versus your own conscious will (psychic inclination)? ?
Consider the natural inclinations you have—habits ingrained in your character that operate without thought. Which of these draw you closer to Allah, and which pull you away? Will you confront the negative ones today, or let them continue to move you without your conscious consent?

Objections to Forced Inclination and Their Answers
Sanusi raises and answers two objections to forced inclination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections to the definition of forced inclination—divine action and the impossibility of void—and the philosophical responses to them.

Core Idea
Forced inclination's definition is challenged by divine agency and void, requiring a broader understanding.

Sanusi now critically examines the concept of forced inclination that he previously defined. He raises two powerful objections: one involving the action of a free agent (God) and the other involving the impossibility of void. The answers reveal that the original definition assumes a philosophical framework that excludes the Creator, and that the notion of 'distinction in position' must be generalized to cover cases like the siphon. This discussion sharpens our understanding of how kalam engages with inherited philosophical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the two objections: stone moved by God, and water moved by void.
2
Identify the answers
See how Sanusi answers each objection: first, the definition is from philosophers who don't believe in a free agent; second, the forcing agent is air nature, but that fails for siphons.
3
Reflect on the generalization
Notice that Sanusi suggests generalizing 'distinction in position' to resolve the difficulty. This shows how kalam modifies philosophical concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهو الميل القسري) فيه بحث هو أنه إذا تحرك الحجر إلى فوق بإرادة القادر المختار فميله إلى فوق قسري مع أنه لا يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل بمعنى الامتياز في الوضع وتعميم الامتياز في الوضع إلى أن يكون محل الميل إذا وضع دون سببه الخارج خلاف الظاهر. وأيضاً حركة الماء إلى فوق عند مص القارورة مصاً شديداً أو كبها على الماء إنما هي قسرية والقاسر امتناع الخلاء مع أنه لا وضع له أصلاً والجواب عن الأول أن التقسيم على مذهب . الحكيم ولا يقولون بالقادر المختار تعالى شأنه عما يقولون. وعن الثاني أن القاسر طبيعة الهواء المقتضي لتلازم سطحه سطح الماء لضرورة عدم الخلاء لكن هذا لا يجدي في صورة الزراقات التي ذكرها الشارح في بحث الخلاء فيشكل الأمر. اللهم إلا أن يعتبر القاسر هناك الخشبة المجذوبة ولا يخفى بعده على أنَّ شارح حكمة العين صرح في بحث أن بين كل حركتين سكوناً بأن القاسم امتناع الخلاء فحينئذ يحتاج في دفع الاشكال إلى تعميم الامتياز في الوضع كما أشرنا إليه والله أعلم
His saying: 'And it is forced inclination'—there is a discussion in it, namely that when a stone moves upward by the will of the free agent, its inclination upward is forced, yet it is not true of it that it is due to a cause external to the locus in the sense of distinction in position; and generalizing distinction in position so that the locus of inclination, when placed without its external cause, is contrary to appearance. Also, the motion of water upward when sucking a bottle strongly or turning it over water is forced, and the forcing agent is the impossibility of void, while it has no position at all. The answer to the first is that the division is according to the doctrine of the philosopher, and they do not affirm the free agent—exalted is His status above what they say. And the answer to the second is that the forcing agent is the nature of air requiring the cohesion of its surface with the surface of the water due to the necessity of the absence of void, but this is not beneficial in the case of siphons which the commentator mentioned in the discussion of void, so the matter becomes problematic. Unless the forcing agent is considered there to be the pulled stick, and its improbability is not hidden. Moreover, the commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn stated in a discussion that between every two motions is rest, that the forcing agent is the impossibility of void, and thus to remove the difficulty one needs to generalize distinction in position, as we indicated, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (baḥth) of forced inclination. It raises two objections. First, if a stone moves upward by the will of a free agent (God), its inclination is forced, but the cause (God) is not external in the sense of being distinct in position from the stone, contradicting the definition. Second, water moving upward due to the impossibility of void (e.g., in a siphon) is forced, yet the forcing agent (the impossibility of void) has no position. The answer to the first is that the division of inclination is based on the philosopher's (i.e., Aristotle's) doctrine, which does not affirm a free agent like God. The answer to the second is that the forcing agent is the nature of air, but this fails in siphons; possibly the forcing agent is the pulled stick, but that is weak. The commentator of Hikmat al-‘Ayn states that the forcing agent is the impossibility of void, so to resolve the issue one must generalize the notion of distinction in position. The passage thus critically examines the definition and suggests a modification.

Key Terms
الميل القسري
القادر المختار
امتناع الخلاء
الامتياز في الوضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forced inclination always requires a spatially distinct external cause.
Correction: Divine action and the impossibility of void challenge this; the definition must be generalized to include nonexistent extrinsic causes.

Checkpoint

B) The division is based on philosophers who do not accept a free agent
C) The stone moves by nature

Key Takeaway
Forced inclination's definition faces challenges from divine action and void, necessitating a broader understanding of 'external cause' beyond spatial distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Celestial Spheres and Forced Inclination
Objection and answer on celestial spheres and forced inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the celestial spheres' accidental motions do not involve forced inclination, preserving the principle that there is no forcing agent in the heavens.

Core Idea
Accidental motions do not require inclination; only essential motions do.

The philosophers assert that celestial spheres have no forcing agent. But an objector points out that the eight spheres have accidental motions caused by an external determiner, which would imply forced inclination. In this passage, Sanusi responds by clarifying the definition of inclination and showing that accidental motions do not involve the same kind of inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's argument: the eight celestial spheres have accidental motions due to an external determiner, so their inclination is forced, contradicting the claim of no forcing agent.
2
Note the key point
The objector focuses on the determiner (al-muḥaddad) as an external cause, which would make the inclination forced.
3
Read the answer
Sanusi denies that there is inclination for those accidental motions. He defines inclination as the proximate principle of essential motions only.
4
Understand the distinction
Essential motions are self-motions of spheres; accidental motions are imparted from outside. The term 'inclination' is not used for accidental motions.
5
See the resolution
Since forced inclination is only relevant to essential motions, the objection fails. No forced inclination exists in the heavens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : الميل الموجود في الأفلاك الثمانية بالنسبة إلى حركاتها العرضية بواسطة المحدد يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل وهو المحدد مع أنهم قالوا : لا قاسر في الأفلاك . قلت : لا نسلم وجود الميل فيها بالنسبة إلى تلك الحركات فإنَّ المراد بالميل هاهنا هو المبدأ القريب للحركات الذاتية أعني المقابلة للحركات العرضية ولا وجود له فيما ذكر.
If you say: The inclination existing in the eight celestial spheres with respect to their accidental motions by means of the determiner (al-muḥaddad) is true of it that it is due to a cause external to the locus, namely the determiner, while they said: There is no forcing agent in the celestial spheres. I say: We do not affirm the existence of inclination in them with respect to those motions, for what is meant by inclination here is the proximate principle of essential motions, i.e., those opposite to accidental motions, and it does not exist in what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector notes that the eight celestial spheres (the seven planets and the fixed stars) have accidental motions due to the determiner (the sphere of fixed stars), which is external to each sphere, so their inclination would be forced, contradicting the philosophers' claim that there is no forcing agent in the heavens. The answer denies that there is inclination for those accidental motions. The term 'inclination' here refers to the proximate principle of essential (self-motion) motions, not accidental motions. Therefore, the celestial spheres do not have forced inclination. This preserves the classification.

Key Terms

الميل (inclination)
الحركات الذاتية (essential motions)
الحركات العرضية (accidental motions)
المحدد (determiner)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any motion caused by an external agent is forced and involves inclination.
Correction: In this context, inclination is defined only for essential (self-motion) motions. Accidental motions, even if externally caused, do not involve the same kind of inclination.

Key Takeaway

Refuting Universal Tadādd among I'timādāt
Refutes Abu Ali's claim that all i'timādāt are mutually contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila debate on whether all i'timādāt are contradictory and the two key refutations of Abu Ali's claim.

Core Idea
Effects do not dictate causes: contradictory movements do not prove contradictory inclinations.

This passage addresses a key internal Mu'tazila debate about the nature of i'timādāt (inclinations). Abu Ali al-Jubba'i argued that all i'timādāt are mutually contradictory, citing the contradictory movements they produce. The refutation dismantles his analogy by showing it lacks a commonality and that a single cause can produce opposite effects under different conditions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of i'timādāt
Read the opening lines that categorize i'timādāt into necessary natural (heaviness/lightness) and acquired (other directions). This sets the stage for the debate.
2
Identify Abu Ali's claim
Notice that Abu Ali asserts all i'timādāt are mutually contradictory (mutadādda), drawing a parallel to movements.
His reasoning is an analogy (qiyās tamthīlī).
3
Analyze the first refutation
Examine why the analogy fails: it merely claims similarity (mumāthala) without a shared cause (jāmiʿ). This is a juristic analogy without proper grounds.
The objection and response clarify that similarity here is linguistic, not technical.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Understand the point that contradictory effects do not necessitate contradictory causes. A single nature can produce both movement and rest depending on context.
This directly undercuts Abu Ali's inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادسها في اختلاف المعتزلة في الاعتمادات فمنها أنهم بعد الاتفاق على انقسامها إلى اعتماد لازم طبيعي وهو الثقل والخفة الثابتان للعناصر الثقيلة والخفيفة المقتضيان للهبوط والصعود وإلى مجتلب وهو ما عداهما كاعتماد الثقيل إلى العلو إذا رمي إليه واعتماد الخفيف إلى السفل حال ما حرك إليه وإلى سائر الجهات أعني القدام والخلف واليمين والشمال قد اختلفوا في أنها هل فيها تضاد فقال أبو علي نعم الاعتمادات كلها متضادة كالحركات التي تجب بها ويبطله أنه تمثيل خال عن الجامع فإن مرجعه إلى دعوى المماثلة من غير علة جامعة بينهما وأنى يلزم من تضاد الآثار التي هي الحركات تضاد أسبابها التي هي الاعتمادات فإنه يجوز أن يصدر عن سبب واحد آثار متضادة بحسب شروط مختلفة كالطبيعة المقتضية للحركة بشرط الخروج عن الحيز الطبيعي وللسكون بشرط الحصول فيه
قوله : ( فإن مرجعه إلى دعوى المماثلة ) قيل عليه : لو سلم المماثلة فجعل أحد المتماثلين واحدة سبباً والآخر سبباً ترجيح بلا مرجح وأيضاً لم لا يجوز أن يكون التضاد باعتبار التشخص لا باعتبار الماهية النوعية فكونهما متماثلين بمعزل عن تلك الدلالة؟ وأيد ذلك بأنه لو جوز كون بعض أفراده سبباً والآخر مسبباً فليجوز كون بعضها متضاداً وبعضها غير متضاد، والجواب : إنه ليس المراد بالمماثلة المذكورة الاتحاد في النوع حتى يرد ما ذكر بل المماثلة اللغوية أي المثلية في التضاد كما يقتضيه سياق الكلام وحاصله أنَّ ما ذكره أبو علي قياس فقهي بلا جامع وسنذكر الآن مثله في الوجه الثاني من وجهي الجبائي بعدم بقاء الاعتماد مطلقاً
The sixth [discussion] regarding the differences of the Mu'tazila concerning the i'timādāt. Among them: that after agreeing on their division into necessary natural i'timād, which is heaviness and lightness established for heavy and light elements, necessitating descent and ascent; and into acquired i'timād, which is other than these two, like the i'timād of the heavy upward when thrown, and the i'timād of the light downward when moved there, and to the other directions, i.e., front, back, right, and left — they differed on whether there is tadādd among them. Abu Ali said: Yes, all i'timādāt are mutually contradictory (mutadādda), like the movements they necessitate. And it is refuted by the fact that it is an analogy lacking a commonality, for its basis is a claim of similarity without a common cause. And also, the claim that contradiction of the effects (which are movements) necessitates contradiction of their causes (which are the i'timādāt) is invalid, because it is possible for a single cause to produce contradictory effects according to different conditions, like nature that necessitates movement under the condition of leaving the natural place, and rest under the condition of being in it.
His statement: (For its basis is a claim of similarity). It was objected: Even if similarity is granted, then making one of the two similar things a cause and the other a cause would be a preponderance without a preponderator. Also, why may tadādd not be considered with respect to individual identity rather than specific nature, so that their being similar is irrelevant to that indication? It was supported by: if it is allowed that some of its individuals are a cause and others are an effect, then it is allowed that some of them are contradictory and others not. The answer: The mentioned similarity is not union in species so that what was mentioned applies, but rather linguistic similarity, i.e., likeness in tadādd, as the flow of speech requires. Its gist is that what Abu Ali mentioned is a juristic analogy without a commonality, and we will now mention its like in the second of al-Jubba'i's two arguments regarding the non-endurance of i'timād absolutely.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage refutes Abu Ali al-Jubba'i's claim that all i'timādāt (inclinations) are mutually contradictory (mutadādda). It first states the division of i'timādāt into necessary (lāzim) natural (tabīʿī) – like heaviness and lightness – and acquired (mujtalab) – like the upward inclination of a thrown heavy object. The Mu'tazila agreed on this division but differed on whether there is tadādd among them. Abu Ali claimed tadādd universally, comparing them to movements. The refutation is two-fold. First, his argument is an analogy without a commonality (tamthīl khāl ʿan al-jāmiʿ): it merely claims similarity (mumāthala) but provides no shared cause. Second, he argues that tadādd of effects (movements) requires tadādd of causes (i'timādāt), but this is invalid because a single cause can produce opposite effects under different conditions (e.g., nature causes movement when outside its natural place and rest when inside it). The commentary elaborates on the first point: even if similarity is conceded, one could say that the tadādd is based on individual identity, not species, so similarity doesn't rule out tadādd. The response clarifies that the similarity here is linguistic (mumāthala lughawiyya), meaning simple likeness in tadādd, not a technical commonality, so Abu Ali's reasoning is merely a juristic analogy without a valid commonality.

Key Terms
اعتماد
تضاد
مماثلة
قياس تمثيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If movements are contradictory, then their causes (i'timādāt) must also be contradictory.
Correction: One cause can produce contradictory effects under different conditions; thus, contradictory movements do not entail contradictory i'timādāt.

Checkpoint
What are the two main arguments used to refute Abu Ali's claim that all i'timādāt are contradictory?
A) That i'timādāt are not real and that movements have no causes
B) That his analogy lacks a commonality and that one cause can produce contradictory effects
C) That i'timādāt are all identical and thus cannot be contradictory
D) That movements are not caused by i'timādāt

Key Takeaway
Abu Ali's claim of universal tadādd among i'timādāt is refuted because his analogy lacks a commonality and one cause can produce contradictory effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone commit a sin, do you immediately assume their heart is corrupt, or do you allow for the possibility that a single nature (e.g., good intentions) can produce contradictory actions under different conditions? How does this lesson challenge your judgments of others? Will you commit to pausing before assuming bad intentions from a single action?
?
In your own spiritual life, you may find yourself performing contradictory actions—sometimes obedient, sometimes sinful. Does this mean your inner inclination (i'timād) is contradictory? Or can a single soul produce both? How does this understanding affect your hope in Allah's mercy and your own self-assessment?
?
The refutation relies on the possibility that one cause produces contradictory effects under different conditions. Reflect on a time when you acted one way in one situation and differently in another. Did you assume you were inconsistent, or did you recognize the different conditions? How can this lesson help you reconcile your own contradictions and turn to Allah for consistency?

Difference Between Movements and I'timādāt Regarding Beings in Spaces
Al-Sanusi enumerates the logical difference between movements and i'timādāt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two simultaneous movements are impossible due to requiring two beings in two places, while two i'timādāt do not face this impossibility, thus breaking the analogy.

Core Idea
Movements force a substance into two places; i'timādāt do not—the decisive difference.

In this second refutation, al-Sanusi sharpens the distinction between movements and i'timādāt. He argues that two simultaneous movements toward different directions would compel a single atom to occupy two distinct places at once—an obvious impossibility. However, this necessity does not apply to i'timādāt. Therefore, the analogy between them collapses. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow: first the claim of difference, then the argument for movements, then the absence in i'timādāt.
The commentary adds an important nuance about causal entailment.
2
Understand the impossibility for movements
Two movements toward two directions would force the substance to have two 'beings' (kawnān) in two different spaces (ḥayyizān) at the same time. This is inherently impossible.
The impossibility is not just empirical but conceptual (ḍarūra).
3
See why i'timādāt are different
The same cause of impossibility is absent in i'timādāt because an inclination toward a direction does not entail being located in that direction.
4
Grasp the invalidation of the analogy
Since the analogical argument (qiyās tamthīlī) lacks a shared cause (jāmiʿ) and has a clear point of difference (fāriq), it is invalid.
The commentary clarifies a potential counter-objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالفرق قائم فإن اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين يوجب للجوهر كونين في حيزين فإنه إذا تحرك إلى جهتين أوجب له الحركة إلى كل جهة منهما الحصول في حيز واقع في تلك الجهة غير الأول الذي تحرك عنه فيلزم أن يجتمع له في حالة واحدة كونان في مكانين واقعين من الحيز الأول في تينك الجهتين واجتماع الكونين محال ضرورة فهذه علة استحالة اجتماع الحركتين وهي مفقودة في الاعتمادين فيبطل القياس التمثيل الخالي عن الجامع مع ظهور الفارق
قوله : ( وأيضاً فالفرق قائم إلخ ) نعم لو كان الاعتماد علة ملزومة للحركة اندفع هذا الوجه لأن تضاد اللازمين ملزوم لتضاد الملزومين وقد مر أنه ليس كذلك
Also, a difference stands: the conjunction of two movements toward two directions necessitates for the substance two beings (kawnān) in two spaces. For if it moves toward two directions, the movement toward each direction necessitates being in a space in that direction other than the first space from which it moved. Thus it would be necessary for it in one state to have two beings in two places located from the first space in those two directions. The conjunction of two beings is impossible by necessity. So this is the cause of the impossibility of the conjunction of two movements, and it is absent in the case of two i'timādāt. Thus the analogical argument lacking a commonality is invalidated, with a clear difference.
His statement: (Also, a difference stands, etc.) Yes, if i'timād were a cause necessarily entailing movement, this argument would be refuted, because the tadādd of the two necessary consequents would necessitate the tadādd of the two antecedent causes—but it has been mentioned that this is not the case.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents a second refutation of Abu Ali's analogy between movements and i'timādāt. It first establishes a key difference: two simultaneous movements toward two different directions would require a single substance to occupy two places at once (two 'beings' in two spaces), which is impossible. This is the reason movements cannot coexist. However, this impossibility does not apply to i'timādāt, because an i'timād (inclination) toward a direction does not entail being in a place in that direction. Therefore, the analogical argument (qiyās tamthīlī) fails because there is a clear point of difference (fāriq). The commentary adds a nuance: if i'timād were a cause that necessarily produces movement (i.e., a 'illah malzūmah), then the tadādd of movements would imply tadādd of i'timādāt, but it has already been explained that i'timād is not such a cause.

Key Terms
كون (kawn)
حيز (ḥayyiz)
اجتماع الكونين (ijtimāʿ al-kawnayn)
فارق (fāriq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That i'timādāt entail being in a place just like movements do.
Correction: This passage explicitly denies that i'timādāt have the same implication; they do not require the substance to be located in the direction of inclination.

Checkpoint
Why does the impossibility of two simultaneous movements not apply to two i'timādāt?
A) Because i'timādāt are not real entities
B) Because i'timādāt do not necessitate being in a place in the direction of inclination
C) Because movements are stronger than i'timādāt
D) Because i'timādāt are always compatible

Key Takeaway
Two movements necessitate two beings in two places, an impossibility; i'timādāt lack this necessity, so the analogy fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that not every analogy is valid—a difference in the underlying cause can invalidate a seemingly plausible comparison. Think of a belief you hold about God, yourself, or the world that you may have accepted based on an analogy (e.g., 'God is like a father' or 'my soul is like a computer'). What specific difference might make that analogy flawed? Will you examine that belief today with the same rigorous logic al-Sanusi used?
?
Al-Sanusi shows that movements force a substance to 'be' in two places at once, which is impossible. Where in your own life are you trying to 'be' in two places at once—serving two masters, holding two contradictory values, or dividing your heart? What 'being' must you sacrifice to have one?
?
The passage distinguishes between what is necessary (the impossibility of two movements) and what is not (i'timādāt). Reflect on something you treat as a necessary truth in your faith or practice that may actually be a debatable opinion. Are you willing to reexamine it, even if it means being less certain? What would that require of you today?

Abu Hashim on Tadādd of I'timādāt
Abu Hashim's view on tadādd of i'timādāt with example and hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's distinction between necessary and acquired i'timādāt regarding mutual contradiction (tadādd), and his hesitation on same-type i'timādāt.

Core Idea
Necessary and acquired i'timādāt coexist without contradiction, but two of the same kind may not.

Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'ī, a leading Mu'tazilite theologian, introduced a nuanced view on i'timādāt. While his father Abu Ali held that all i'timādāt are mutually contradictory, Abu Hashim argued that necessary and acquired i'timādāt can coexist without tadādd, as seen in a lifted stone. However, he was unsure about two necessary or two acquired i'timādāt. This lesson examines his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Note the technical terms: لازم, مجتلب, مدافعة, تردد.
The passage uses a clear division: 'أما الأول' and 'أما الثاني'.
2
Identify the two cases
Distinguish between the certain case (no tadādd between necessary and acquired) and the uncertain case (hesitation on two necessary or two acquired).
3
Analyze the stone example
Understand how the stone's natural weight (downward repulsion) is a necessary i'timād, and the lifter's pull (upward repulsion) is an acquired i'timād. Both exist together without contradiction.
The coexistence is possible because the two i'timādāt are in opposite directions.
4
Reflect on the hesitation
Consider why Abu Hashim hesitated on same-type i'timādāt. What might make two necessary i'timādāt contradictory? This shows the complexity of kalam reasoning.
Think about what it means for two forces to oppose each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه: لا تضاد للاعتمادات اللازمة مع المجتلبة وهل يتضاد الاعتمادان اللازمان أو المجتلبان؟ تردد قوله فيه فقال تارة بالتضاد وتارة بعدمه. أما الأول فلما علمت أن الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق فيه مدافعة هابطة يجدها الرافع وهذه اعتماد لازم طبيعي للحجر وفيه مدافعة صاعدة يجدها الرافع له وهذه اعتماد مجتلب للحجر فقد اجتمع فيه اللازم مع المجتلب فلا تضاد بينها. وأما الثاني وهو تردده في أنه هل يتضاد
قوله : ( فقد اجتمع فيه إلخ ) قد عرفت أنَّ المعلوم وجود الاعتمادين فيه وأما أنهما معاً فكلا
And his son said: There is no tadādd between necessary i'timādāt and acquired ones. But are two necessary i'timādāt or two acquired i'timādāt mutually contradictory? He hesitated regarding his view, sometimes affirming tadādd and sometimes denying it. As for the first [part, i.e., the non-tadādd of necessary with acquired], this is because you know that a stone lifted upward has a downward dāfiʿa (repulsive force) that the lifter feels, and this is a natural necessary i'timād of the stone; and it also has an upward dāfiʿa that the lifter feels, and this is an acquired i'timād of the stone. Thus the necessary and the acquired have come together in it, so there is no tadādd between them. As for the second [part], it is his hesitation on whether they are mutually contradictory.
His statement: (Thus the necessary and the acquired have come together in it) You know that the existence of both i'timādāt in it is known; but as for both of them being together [in the same direction], no.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Hashim (the son of Abu Ali) on whether i'timādāt are mutually contradictory. He distinguishes two cases. First, he is certain that there is no tadādd between a necessary (lāzim) i'timād and an acquired (mujtalab) i'timād. He proves this with the example of a stone lifted upward: the stone simultaneously has a downward natural necessary i'timād (its weight) and an upward acquired i'timād (due to the lifting). Both are present together, so they are not contradictory. Second, he is uncertain about whether two necessary i'timādāt (e.g., both downward) or two acquired i'timādāt are contradictory; he sometimes says yes and sometimes no. The commentary points out that while we know both i'timādāt exist in the stone, it is not the case that they are both toward the same direction, which might explain the coexistence.

Key Terms
لازم (lāzim)
مجتلب (mujtalab)
مدافعة (mudāfaʿa)
تردد (taraddud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hashim denied all tadādd among i'timādāt.
Correction: He only denied it between necessary and acquired; he was unsure about same-type pairs.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the lifted stone demonstrate according to Abu Hashim?
A) That all i'timādāt are contradictory
B) That necessary and acquired i'timādāt can coexist without contradiction
C) That i'timādāt do not exist
D) That upward and downward i'timādāt are contradictory

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim demonstrated that necessary and acquired i'timādāt coexist without contradiction, but he remained uncertain about same-type pairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You daily feel opposing forces — the pull of your nature (necessary i'timād) and the push of external expectations (acquired i'timād). Which of these do you assume must cancel each other out? Abu Hashim shows they can coexist. Confront one area where you have denied a part of yourself thinking it contradicts another. What would it mean to let both exist without forcing a resolution?
?
Abu Hashim hesitated: he did not pretend to know whether two necessary i'timādāt can contradict. Where in your faith do you pretend certainty to avoid the discomfort of not knowing? Will you today admit your hesitation to Allah and seek knowledge with humility, or will you keep your false certainty?
?
The stone's necessary i'timād is its natural weight; its acquired i'timād is from the lifter. Which i'timād in your soul is innate (your fitrah) and which is acquired from your environment or upbringing? Are they in conflict? What would it look like to let both be present without insisting one must dominate?

The Disanalogy Between Movements and I'timad
Refutes the analogy between movements and i'timad by showing a key disanalogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposition of movements does not imply the opposition of i'timads, due to the key difference in their relation to spatial location.

Core Idea
Movements require location; i'timad does not — the analogy collapses.

Theologians sometimes argue that since movements oppose each other, their causes (i'timads) must also oppose. This lesson examines a counterargument: while two movements to different directions would force a substance to be in two places at once (impossible), i'timad toward a direction has no such implication. The passage from al-Mawaqif lays out this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Note the technical terms.
Pay attention to the example of nature causing movement or rest under different conditions.
2
Identify the core disagreement
Focus on the sentences that describe what happens when a substance moves in two directions. Why does this lead to impossibility? Then contrast with i'timad.
The key is the requirement of 'kawn' (locating) in a space.
3
Understand why the analogy fails
Consider the commentator's point: the analogy lacks a common ground (jāmi‘). Reflect on how the passage establishes a clear difference that invalidates the comparison.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل الآثار ) التي هي الحركات ( تضاد أسبابها ) التي هي الاعتمادات فإنه يجوز أن يصدر عن سبب واحد آثار متضادة بحسب شروط مختلفة كالطبيعة المقتضية للحركة بشرط الخروج عن الحيز الطبيعي وللسكون بشرط الحصول فيه ( وأيضاً فالفرق قائم فإنَّ اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين ( يوجب للجوهر كونين) في حيزين ( فإنه إذا تحرك ) الجوهر (إلى) جهتين أوجب له الحركة إلى كل جهة منهما ( الحصول في حيز واقع في تلك الجهة (غير) الحيز ( الأول ) الذي تحرك عنه فيلزم أن يجتمع له في حالة واحدة كونان في مكانين واقعين من الحيز الأول في تينك الجهتين ) واجتماع الكونين محال ضرورة) فإنّ البديهة تحكم بأن الجوهر الواحد في حالة أن يكون في حيزين معاً ( فهذه علة استحالة اجتماع الحركتين وهي مفقودة في الاعتمادين) فإنَّ الاعتماد إلى جهة لا يستلزم الحصول في مكان واقع في تلك الجهة ) فيبطل القياس التمثيل الخالي عن الجامع مع ظهور الفارق
Third Observatory - Third Objective: On i'timad, which is inclination. The effects, which are movements, oppose their causes, which are i'timads, for it is possible that from one cause issue opposite effects according to different conditions, like nature that necessitates movement on condition of leaving the natural place and rest on condition of being in it. Also, the difference is established: the conjunction of two movements toward two directions necessitates for the substance two locatings (kawnayn) in two spaces (ḥīzayn). For when the substance moves toward two directions, the movement toward each direction necessitates obtaining in a space located in that direction other than the first space from which it moved; so it follows that in one state it has two locatings in two places located from the first space in those two directions. The conjunction of two locatings is impossible necessarily, for intuition (al-badīha) judges that a single substance cannot in one state be in two spaces simultaneously. So this is the cause of the impossibility of the conjunction of two movements, and it is absent in the two i'timads, for i'timad toward a direction does not necessitate obtaining in a place located in that direction. Thus the analogical argument lacking a common ground with the appearance of difference is invalidated.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the claim that i'timads (inclinations) oppose each other like movements do. It begins by stating that effects (movements) can originate from opposing causes (i'timads) under different conditions, as in the example of nature causing movement when leaving the natural place and rest when within it. Then it presents a key difference: when a substance moves in two directions simultaneously, it would require the substance to occupy two different places (ḥīzayn) at the same time, which is impossible (impossible by intuitive necessity). In contrast, i'timad toward a direction does not imply that the substance occupies a place in that direction; thus the analogy between movements and i'timads fails due to a clear distinction. The conclusion is that the analogical reasoning (qiyās tamthīl) is invalid because it lacks a common ground (jāmi‘).

Key Terms
اعتماد (i'timad)
حركة (haraka)
تضاد (tadadd)
حيز (hīz)
كون (kawn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because movements are opposites, their causes (i'timads) must also be opposites.
Correction: The opposition of movements is due to their requirement of spatial location, which i'timad does not share; therefore the causes need not be opposites.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives to refute the analogy between movements and i'timads?
A) Movements are contradictory by nature, but i'timads are not.
B) The conjunction of two movements requires the substance to occupy two places at once, which is impossible, but i'timad does not have this requirement.
C) Movements are caused by external forces, while i'timads are internal.
D) The author does not refute the analogy; he confirms it.

Key Takeaway
Two movements cannot coexist because they force a single substance to be in two places at once; i'timad does not force such location, so the analogy between them is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed two things are alike because they look similar, but upon deeper inspection they are fundamentally different? Can you identify a belief you hold that relies on a false analogy? What would it cost you to re-examine it with the same rigor used in this passage?
?
The passage says intuition (badīha) judges the impossibility of a substance being in two places at once. Is there an intuitive truth about God's oneness that you have neglected or compromised? How can you affirm tawḥīd more consciously in your daily thoughts?
?
Consider a time you reasoned by analogy but missed a crucial difference (fāriq). How can you train yourself to look for such differences before drawing conclusions, especially in matters of faith?

Abu Hashim: Necessary and Acquired I'timads Do Not Oppose
States Abu Hashim's view that necessary and acquired i'timads do not oppose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that necessary and acquired i'timads can coexist without opposition, using the example of a lifted stone.

Core Idea
Necessary and acquired i'timads coexist without opposition.

Abu Hashim, a key figure in early kalam, examines whether different types of i'timad (inclinations) oppose each other. He is certain that necessary i'timads (like natural heaviness) and acquired i'timads (like the lift) can exist together. In this passage, he uses the concrete example of a stone being lifted to demonstrate their compatibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall claim and example.
Notice the parentheses around Abu Hashim's name and the phrase about his wavering.
2
Identify the stone example
Focus on the stone being lifted. Identify the two i'timads: the downward necessary i'timad (weight) and the upward acquired i'timad (from the lifter). Note that both are present simultaneously.
The lifter feels both repulsions (mudāfa‘a).
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this example prove that necessary and acquired i'timads do not oppose? Why is this significant for kalam physics?
Abu Hashim's conclusion challenges the assumption that all i'timads in one substance must conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه : ) أبو هاشم ( لا تضاد للاعتمادات اللازمة مع المجتلبة وهل يتضاد الاعتمادان اللازمان أو المجتلبان ؟ تردد قوله فيه ) فقال تارة بالتضاد وتارة بعدمه. ( أما الأول ) وهو جزمه بأنه لا تضاد اللازمة مع المجتلبة ( فلما علمت أن الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق فيه مدافعة هابطة يجدها الرافع، وهذه اعتماد لازم طبيعي للحجر ( و ) فيه أيضا مدافعة ( صاعدة يجدها الرافع له أي للرافع وهذه اعتماد مجتلب للحجر فقد اجتمع فيه اللازم مع المجتلب فلا تضاد بينها
And his son, Abu Hashim, said: there is no opposition between the necessary (al-lāzima) i'timads and the acquired (al-mujtalaba). And whether the two necessary or the two acquired i'timads oppose each other – his statement wavered on it, for sometimes he said they oppose and sometimes not. As for the first – which is his assertion that there is no opposition between the necessary and the acquired – it is because you know that the stone which is raised upward has a downward repulsion (mudāfa‘a hābita) that the lifter feels, and this is a necessary natural i'timad of the stone; and it also has an upward repulsion (ṣā‘ida) that the lifter feels for himself, and this is an acquired i'timad of the stone. Thus both the necessary and the acquired coexist in it, so there is no opposition between them.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's position on the opposition (tadadd) of i'timads. He is certain that necessary i'timads (like natural heaviness) and acquired i'timads (like the upward force from lifting) do not oppose each other; they can coexist without mutual exclusion. He is hesitant, however, about whether two necessary i'timads or two acquired i'timads oppose. To support the first point, he gives the example of a stone being lifted upward: the stone has a downward necessary i'timad (its weight) and an upward acquired i'timad from the lifter. Both are present simultaneously in the stone, proving that they are not opposites. The passage lays the foundation for discussions on the nature of i'timad coexistence.

Key Terms
اعتماد لازم (i'timad lāzim)
اعتماد مجتلب (i'timad mujtalab)
تضاد (tadadd)
مدافعة (mudāfa‘a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two i'timads in the same substance must oppose each other.
Correction: Abu Hashim shows that necessary and acquired i'timads can coexist without opposition, as seen in the lifted stone.

Checkpoint
In the stone example, why does Abu Hashim conclude that the necessary and acquired i'timads do not oppose each other?
A) Because they are of different types, one created and one eternal.
B) Because both are felt by the lifter as repulsions in opposite directions, yet they exist together in the stone.
C) Because the stone cannot have two i'timads at once.
D) Because the necessary i'timad is from the stone's essence, and the acquired is external.

Key Takeaway
Necessary and acquired i'timads can coexist without opposition, as illustrated by the stone's simultaneous downward weight and upward lift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that necessary (innate) and acquired (learned) i'timads can coexist without opposition. Think of a struggle you face between a natural tendency (e.g., laziness) and an acquired goal (e.g., discipline). Do you believe these must always be at war, or can they coexist? What would change if you accepted that they can? Will you try to see them as complementary forces rather than opposites this week?
?
Abu Hashim's example of the stone suggests that upward and downward forces can be present simultaneously. In your spiritual life, you may feel both a natural pull toward worldly ease and an acquired push toward spiritual exertion. Have you been viewing these as mutually exclusive? How might the lesson's principle allow you to hold both without despair? Commit to one specific way you will allow both forces to exist without feeling torn.
?
The passage notes that Abu Hashim wavered on whether two necessary or two acquired i'timads oppose. Reflect on your own life: when you face two similar demands (e.g., two necessary obligations or two learned habits), do you immediately assume they conflict? Can you recall a situation where you reconciled two similar demands? What does your assumption reveal about your inner worldview? Will you from now on pause and ask whether the two truly oppose or can coexist?

The Rope Example: Two Acquired I'timads
Examines whether two acquired i'timads can coexist using the rope example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rope example illustrates the dispute over whether two acquired i'timads can coexist in a single substrate.

Core Idea
The rope's stillness does not settle the debate; it depends on whether repulsions are actual or potential.

Abu Hashim's hesitation on the coexistence of two acquired i'timads is illuminated by the physical example of a rope pulled from opposite directions. Sometimes he argues that the rope contains two opposing repulsions, indicating two i'timads; other times he denies this, claiming each pull prevents the other from producing a real i'timad. This passage presents both views, inviting us to weigh the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the contrast between the two positions introduced by 'sometimes he said'.
The rope is the substrate; the pullers are the causes.
2
Understand the first position
Identify the argument for two i'timads: each puller feels the rope's inclination toward the other side, and if one lets go, the rope moves. This suggests a real i'timad in each direction.
The key phrase is 'each of them finds from himself an inclination of the rope toward the opposite of his direction'.
3
Understand the second position
Contrast with the second view: each puller's force prevents the other from creating an i'timad, so the rope is like a stationary object with no combined i'timads.
The rope's stillness is not due to balanced i'timads but to mutual prevention.
4
Reflect on the dispute
Ask yourself: which position seems more convincing? Why does Abu Hashim waver? This example shows how a simple physical scenario can generate a deep kalam problem.
Consider the difference between actual repulsion and mere potential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتمادات اللازمة بعضها مع بعض وكذلك المجتلبة (فللحبل المتجاذب) على سبيل التقاوم حتى سكن كما مر . فتارة قال : فيه مدافعة للجاذبين يجده ) أي يجد الجاذب مدافعة الحبل له ( بالضرورة ) فإن كل واحد منهما يجد من نفسه ميل الحبل إلى خلاف جهته بحيث لولا جذبه إياه إلى جهته لتحرك الحبل ذلك الميل إلى خلاف تلك الجهة بالضرورة. وإليه أشار بقوله : ( إذ لولا جذبه له لتحرك ضرورة ) فقد اجتمع في الحبل اعتمادان مجتلبان ) وتارة قال : لا مدافعة فيه وإنما هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع من التحرك ) فإنَّ كل واحد من الجاذبين يمنع بجذبه أن يحدث الآخر فيه مدافعة إلى جهته فلا اجتماع هناك بین الاعتمادين
The necessary i'timads with each other, and likewise the acquired ones. For the rope pulled from both sides (al-ḥabl al-mutajādhib), on the basis of mutual resistance until it became still, as previously. Sometimes he said: in it there is a repulsion for the two pullers that he finds – i.e., the puller finds the rope's repulsion for him – necessarily, for each of them finds from himself an inclination of the rope toward the opposite of his direction, such that were he not to pull it toward his direction, the rope would necessarily move due to that inclination toward the opposite direction. And to this he pointed by his saying: 'For were he not to pull it, it would move necessarily.' Thus there have combined in the rope two acquired i'timads. And sometimes he said: there is no repulsion in it; rather it is like the stationary thing that refrains from moving. For each of the two pullers prevents by his pulling that the other produce in it a repulsion toward his direction; so there is no combination there between the two i'timads.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage addresses Abu Hashim's hesitation about whether two acquired i'timads (mujtalabān) can coexist. The example is a rope pulled from opposite ends until it becomes still. Abu Hashim sometimes says that the rope has a repulsion (mudāfa‘a) toward each puller, because each puller feels the rope's tendency to go in the other direction. If one puller let go, the rope would move toward the other side. This indicates two acquired i'timads in the rope simultaneously. Other times, he says there is no repulsion; instead, each puller's force prevents the other from producing any i'timad in the rope, so the rope remains like a stationary object with no combined i'timads. Thus Abu Hashim's position wavers on this specific issue.

Key Terms
اعتماد مجتلب (i'timad mujtalab)
مدافعة (mudāfa‘a)
حبل متجاذب (ḥabl mutajādhib)
تقاوم (taqāwum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rope's stillness proves that two i'timads are not present.
Correction: Stillness alone does not settle the issue; one position explains it as two opposing actual i'timads, the other as mutual prevention with no actual i'timads.

Checkpoint
According to the first position presented, what evidence suggests that two acquired i'timads coexist in the rope?
A) The rope moves in both directions simultaneously.
B) Each puller feels the rope's inclination toward the opposite direction, and if he let go, the rope would move.
C) The rope is completely still, showing balance.
D) The pullers are pulling with equal force.

Key Takeaway
The rope example reveals a genuine hesitation in kalam about whether acquired i'timads can coexist; the answer depends on whether the observed repulsions are considered actual or merely potential.

Abu Hashim presents two contradictory statements without resolving them. Are you comfortable sitting with theological uncertainty, or do you demand a decisive answer? How might your need for clarity sometimes lead you to oversimplify complex realities?
?
The rope appears still, yet beneath the surface a subtle debate rages about what is actually there. In your own life, when everything looks calm and settled, do you ever stop to investigate the hidden tensions? What might you be ignoring because the outward stillness feels safer?

Al-Jubba'i's First Argument Against I'timad Persistence
Presents al-Jubba'i's first argument against persistence of i'timads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i uses the concept of the 'most specific attribute' (akhaṣṣ ṣifa) to argue that necessary i'timads cannot persist because that would force persistence of acquired i'timads.

Core Idea
If one i'timad persists, all i'timads sharing its specific attribute must also persist.

Al-Jubba'i denies persistence of i'timads entirely, but his son Abu Hashim distinguishes necessary from acquired i'timads, allowing persistence for the former. To undermine this distinction, al-Jubba'i offers two arguments. This lesson unpacks his first argument, which leverages the deep identity between necessary and acquired i'timads when they share the same direction—the most specific attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the contrasts: al-Jubba'i denies persistence absolutely vs. his son's distinction.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the two types of i'timad
Al-Jubba'i's son distinguishes between necessary i'timad (i'timad lāzim) and acquired i'timad (i'timad mujtalab). Understand each: necessary is inherent to the body, acquired arises from external force.
The argument focuses on the downward direction example.
3
Understand the shared attribute argument
Al-Jubba'i argues that if a necessary i'timad in a given direction persisted, then any acquired i'timad in the same direction would also persist because they share the 'most specific attribute' (akhaṣṣ ṣifa)—the direction itself.
The logical link is that identity in the most specific attribute entails identity in all properties relevant to persistence.
4
See why the conclusion follows
Since acquired i'timads are known to be non-persistent (they come and go with the external force), the consequence that they would persist is unacceptable. Therefore, necessary i'timads cannot persist either.
This refutes the son's position that necessary i'timads persist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي ومن اختلافاتهم أنَّ الاعتمادات هل تبقى؟ فمنعه الجبائي من غير تفصيل وواقفه ابنه في المجتلبة دون اللازمة فإنها باقية عنده للجبائي في عدم بقاء الاعتماد مطلقاً وجهان . الأول : لو بقي الاعتماد اللازم في جهة السفل مثلاً بقي المجتلب في تلك الجهة أيضاً، كالاعتماد الحاصل للحجر المتحرك إلى السفل بسبب دفع الإنسان إياه إليه لأنه يشاركه في أخص صفة النفس جهة السفل مثلاً وهو أعني الاشتراك في الأخص يوجب وهو كونه اعتمادا
And among them – i.e., among their differences – is whether i'timads persist (tabqā). Al-Jubba'i denied it absolutely, without detail, and his son agreed with him concerning the acquired (al-mujtalaba) but not the necessary (al-lāzima), for they persist according to him. For al-Jubba'i there are two arguments against the persistence of i'timad absolutely. The first: if the necessary i'timad in the downward direction, for example, persisted, then the acquired i'timad (al-mujtalab) in that same direction would also persist, like the i'timad occurring for the stone moving downward due to a person's push towards it, because it [the acquired] shares with it [the necessary] in the most specific attribute of the self – the downward direction, for example – and that is, the sharing in the most specific [attribute] necessitates... and it being an i'timad [؟].
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage introduces the dispute over whether i'timad is persistent (bāqī) across time. Al-Jubba'i denies persistence entirely, while his son Abu Hashim distinguishes between necessary i'timads (which persist) and acquired i'timads (which do not). Al-Jubba'i presents two arguments; the first is given here: if a necessary i'timad persisted in a given direction (e.g., downward), then any acquired i'timad in that same direction would also have to persist because it shares with the necessary i'timad in the most specific attribute (the direction). But al-Jubba'i holds that acquired i'timads are transient (like movements), so this consequence is unacceptable, thereby refuting the persistence of necessary i'timads. The argument is cut off but the core idea is clear.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
اعتماد لازم (i'timad lāzim)
اعتماد مجتلب (i'timad mujtalab)
أخص صفة (akhaṣṣ ṣifa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Jubba'i's argument directly proves that no i'timad can persist because persistence itself is impossible.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i's argument does not assert that persistence is impossible per se; rather, it shows that if necessary i'timads persisted, then acquired i'timads would also have to persist due to the shared specific attribute, but since acquired i'timads are non-persistent, the assumption of persistence of necessary i'timads is false.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-Jubba'i's first argument against the persistence of i'timads?
A) Necessary and acquired i'timads cannot coexist.
B) If a necessary i'timad persisted, then an acquired i'timad in the same direction would also have to persist because they share the same specific attribute, but acquired i'timads are known to be non-persistent.
C) I'timads are like movements, and movements do not persist.
D) The son's position is contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's first argument refutes the persistence of necessary i'timads by showing that it would entail the persistence of acquired i'timads, which are indisputably transient.

The argument shows that if a necessary inclination persists, then related acquired inclinations persist too. What would it mean for your repentance if your deeper inclinations also need transformation?
?
Al-Jubba'i's son distinguished between transient acquired and eternal necessary i'timads, but his father refuted that by showing a logical connection. Two Cases of Acquired I'timad: The Rope Example
Explains two possible cases of acquired i'timad using the rope-pulling example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible cases of acquired i'timad illustrated by the rope-pulling example and the conceptual distinction between them.

Core Idea
Two acquired i'timads can either coexist in resistance or not, depending on force balance.

The rope pulled from both sides provides a vivid illustration of how acquired i'timads behave under opposing forces. This passage presents two scenarios: one where the rope resists both pullers, showing coexistence of i'timads in mutual resistance, and another where the rope remains still like a stationary object, indicating no true coexistence. By understanding these cases, you will grasp a key nuance in the kalam theory of i'timad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall context of the rope example.
Notice the use of 'tara...' and 'tara...' to separate the two cases.
2
Identify the two cases
Look for the phrases 'فتارة قال' and 'وتارة قال' to delineate the two scenarios of acquired i'timad.
3
Reflect on the conceptual distinction
Consider that in the first case there is mutual resistance (مدافعة) and in the second there is not. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for understanding the nature of i'timad.
The distinction hinges on whether the rope's inclination is fully countered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل. الاعتمادات اللازمة بعضها مع بعض وكذلك المجتلبة (فللحبل المتجاذب) على سبيل التقاوم حتى سكن كما مر . فتارة قال : فيه مدافعة للجاذبين يجده ) أي يجد الجاذب مدافعة الحبل له ( بالضرورة ) فإن كل واحد منهما يجد من نفسه ميل الحبل إلى خلاف جهته بحيث لولا جذبه إياه إلى جهته لتحرك الحبل ذلك الميل إلى خلاف تلك الجهة بالضرورة. وإليه أشار بقوله : ( إذ لولا جذبه له لتحرك ضرورة ) فقد اجتمع في الحبل اعتمادان مجتلبان ) وتارة قال : لا مدافعة فيه وإنما هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع من التحرك ) فإنَّ كل واحد من الجاذبين يمنع بجذبه أن يحدث الآخر فيه مدافعة إلى جهته فلا اجتماع هناك بین الاعتمادين.
Third observation - Third purpose: Concerning i'timad (reliance/inclination), which is inclination. The necessary i'timads with one another, and likewise the acquired ones, (for the rope pulled from two sides) on the basis of resistance until it settled as has passed. Sometimes he said: In it there is mutual resistance for the two pullers—he finds (meaning each puller finds the rope's resistance to him) (necessarily) because each of them finds from himself the rope's inclination to the opposite of his direction, such that if not for his pulling it towards his direction, the rope would necessarily move due to that inclination to the opposite direction. He alluded to this by his saying: (for if not for his pulling it, it would necessarily move). So two acquired i'timads have come together in the rope. And sometimes he said: There is no resistance in it; rather it is like a stationary thing that refrains from moving. For each of the two pullers prevents by his pulling that the other should produce in it a resistance to his direction, so there is no conjunction of the two i'timads there.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage uses the example of a rope pulled from two opposite directions to illustrate the behavior of acquired i'timads. It presents two possible cases. In the first case, the rope resists both pullers because each puller feels the rope's inclination toward the opposite direction; if one puller released, the rope would move entirely toward the other side. This shows that two acquired i'timads can coexist in the same rope in a state of mutual resistance. In the second case, there is no resistance because each puller's force counteracts the other, making the rope like a stationary object that prevents movement; here the two acquired i'timads do not truly coexist since one prevents the other from manifesting. The passage thereby clarifies the conceptual distinction between the two scenarios of acquired i'timads.

Key Terms
اعتماد
اعتماد لازم
اعتماد مجتلب
مدافعة
تقاوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the rope example is a literal proof for the coexistence of i'timads in all cases.
Correction: The passage clearly shows two cases: one with coexistence (mutual resistance) and one without (no resistance). The example is an illustration of the conceptual distinction, not a universal proof.

Checkpoint

A) That they always coexist with mutual resistance.
B) That they never coexist because one prevents the other.
C) That they can either coexist with resistance or not, depending on the force applied.
D) That they are identical to necessary i'timads.

Key Takeaway
The rope example illustrates that acquired i'timads may or may not coexist depending on whether the opposing forces produce mutual resistance.
